Proof that the act indicates knowledge directly, not via otherness or creation
Al-Ash'ari proves the act indicates knowledge itself, not that it is other or created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari argues that the act of wisdom directly indicates knowledge without implying that knowledge is other than the knower or created.

Core Idea
The act indicates knowledge itself, not its otherness or creation.

Al-Ash'ari refutes those who claim that when an act of wisdom indicates a knower's knowledge, it must mean the knowledge is separate or created. He shows that the knower is knower due to knowledge, not due to otherness or creation, so the act directly indicates the attribute of knowledge. Similarly, knowledge is knowledge by itself, not because it is other or created. This passage clarifies the direct signification of acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Ash'ari uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The passage begins by stating the view it refutes: that the act of wisdom indicates knowledge is other than the knower and created. Understand this starting point.
The phrase 'ومما يبطل قول من قال' signals a refutation.
3
Follow the first invalidation
Al-Ash'ari argues that the knower is knower not due to otherness or creation, so the act indicating the knower indicates knowledge directly.
This uses the principle that the indication of an attribute is direct.
4
Follow the second invalidation
Knowledge is knowledge not due to otherness or creation because other and created things exist that are not knowledge. Thus the act does not imply those properties.
This shows that being other or created is not essential to being knowledge.
5
Conclude the lesson
Reflect on how al-Ash'ari has shown that the act's indication is neutral regarding otherness and creation—it simply indicates the existence of knowledge.
This refutes the opponent's inference and establishes direct indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وممّا يُبْطِلُ قول من قال : إنّ دلالة الفعل الحِكْمي على علم العالم منا ، دلالة على أنّه غيرُ العَالِم، وأنه مُحدَث : أنّ العالِمَ للعلم، ما كانَ عالما لا للغَيرية ولا للحدث، فوجب أن تكونَ الدلالة على أنّ العالم عالمٌ دِلالةً على العلم . و [أنّه ] لمْ يكُن العلمُ علما ؛ لأنه غيرُ العالم ولا لأنه مُحدَث؛ لوجودِ غَيرِ ليس بعلم، ومحدث ليسَ بِعلم، فلم يجب أن تكون الدلالة على أن العلم علم دلالة على أنه محدَثُ أو أنه غيرُ العالم
And among what invalidates the statement of those who say that the indication of the act of wisdom to the knowledge of the knower among us is an indication that it is other than the knower and that it is created: that the knower of knowledge was not a knower due to otherness nor due to creation, so it is necessary that the indication that the knower is a knower is an indication of knowledge. And that knowledge was not knowledge because it is other than the knower nor because it is created; because there exists that which is other but not knowledge, and created but not knowledge. So it is not necessary that the indication that knowledge is knowledge is an indication that it is created or that it is other than the knower.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and proof. Al-Ash'ari targets those who claim that the act of wisdom (الفعل الحكمي) indicates that knowledge is other than the knower and is created (محدث). He argues: the knower (العالم) is called a knower not because his knowledge is other than him or because it is created; rather, he is knower because he possesses knowledge. So the act that indicates he is a knower directly indicates the existence of knowledge, without implying otherness or creation. Conversely, knowledge is called knowledge not because it is other than the knower or created, because there exist things that are other (e.g., a stone) or created (e.g., a body) that are not knowledge. Therefore, the act's indication that knowledge is knowledge does not entail that it is created or other; it simply indicates its existence as a distinct attribute. Thus the opponent's inference from the act to otherness and creation is invalid.

Key Terms
الفعل الحكمي
الغيرية
الحدث
دلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an act indicates something, that thing must be other or created.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the act indicates knowledge directly, without implying otherness or creation.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ash'ari argue that the act's indication of knowledge does not imply that knowledge is other or created?
A) Because knowledge is eternal
B) Because the knower is knower for a different reason
C) Because otherness and creation are not essential to being knowledge
D) Because the act only indicates the knower

Key Takeaway
The act of wisdom indicates the attribute of knowledge itself, not any accidental properties like otherness or creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe signs of wisdom in creation, do you naturally assume that what is indicated is separate from God or created? How does al-Ash'ari's argument challenge that assumption, and how can you train yourself to see God's attributes directly in His acts without adding unnecessary intermediaries?
?
In your own relationship with God, do you sometimes feel that His knowledge or power is 'out there' or distinct from Him? How might this lesson help you remove that mental barrier and feel the immediacy of His attributes in your daily life?
?
If the act of wisdom points to knowledge itself, not to otherness or creation, what does that imply about how intimately God's attributes are tied to His essence? How should this affect the way you think about God when you see His actions in the world?

Objection on Wise Acts and Knowledge Refuted
Al-Ash'ari presents an objection and responds to it regarding evidence from wise acts for God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's refutation of the objection that a wise act indicates knowledge only due to the possibility of the knower's death.

Core Idea
A wise act proves knowledge regardless of whether the knower may die.

This passage presents a subtle objection against using wise acts as evidence for God's knowledge. The objector claims that the act only indicates knowledge because the agent could become ignorant. Al-Ash'ari responds by showing that this logic would lead to an absurd conclusion, thereby defending the soundness of the argument from wise acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Notice that the objector argues that the wise act indicates knowledge only because the knower is subject to death and ignorance.
The objection attempts to limit the evidential value of wise acts.
2
Identify the response
Read Al-Ash'ari's reply. He uses the same logic to counter the objector: if the possibility of death were relevant, it could also prove the opposite.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Understand the logical move
Consider why the objector's reasoning fails: it ties the indication of the act to a contingent property (mortality) of the knower, which would make the evidence unreliable.
Al-Ash'ari shows that the objector's criterion would prove something false, so it must be rejected.
4
Apply to divine attributes
Reflect on how this refutation strengthens the argument that wise acts in creation point to God's eternal and perfect knowledge, not to a temporary knowledge.
The acts themselves, regardless of the agent's mortality, indicate knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : [ما يمنع] من أنه إنما يدل الفعل الحكمي على علم العالم؛ لأنّهُ ممن يجوز أن يموتَ ويَجْهَلَ . قيل له : لو جاز هذا لقائله لجاز لزاعم أن يز أنْ يَزْعُمَ أنّ الفعلَ الحِكْميَّ يدل على أن العالِمَ عالِم ؛ لأنَّه ممّن يَجُوز أن يموت ويجهل .
For [someone] might say: 'What prevents that the wise act only indicates the knowledge of the knower because he is one who may die and become ignorant?' It is said to him: 'If this were permissible for its proponent, it would be permissible for a claimant to claim that the wise act indicates that the knower is knowing because he is one who may die and become ignorant.'
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector argues that a wise act (الفعل الحكمي) only indicates the knowledge of the knower because the knower is subject to death and ignorance—i.e., if the knower might become ignorant, then the wise act shows he has knowledge. Al-Ash'ari responds by pointing out that this logic would equally allow someone to claim the opposite: that the wise act indicates the knower is knowing precisely because he might die and become ignorant—which is absurd, since the wise act is evidence of knowledge regardless of the possibility of death. The refutation uses a reductio ad absurdum: the objector's own criterion is shown to be invalid because it would prove something false.

Key Terms
الفعل الحكمي (al-fi'l al-hikmi: wise act)
العالم (al-'alim: the knower)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is that God could die, making the argument from wise acts invalid.
Correction: The objection is not about God's mortality, but about the logical link between the wise act and knowledge. Al-Ash'ari refutes it by showing the absurdity of the objector's reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's initial argument regarding the wise act?
A) The wise act indicates the knower's knowledge only if the knower cannot die.
B) The wise act indicates the knower's knowledge only because the knower may die and become ignorant.
C) The wise act indicates nothing about knowledge.
D) The wise act indicates the knower is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari refutes the objection that wise acts indicate knowledge only due to the possibility of death, using a reductio ad absurdum that defends the reliability of such acts as evidence for God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Resolve today to critically evaluate every assumption that challenges your faith.
?

Refuting Otherness and Temporality of Knowledge
Al-Ash'ari refutes the idea that evidence of knowledge implies knowledge is other than the knower or created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's twofold argument that the indication of knowledge from a wise act does not imply knowledge is other than the knower or originated.

Core Idea
Proving a thing's existence does not prove its attributes.

Al-Ash'ari confronts an opponent who argues that because we infer a knower's knowledge from wise acts, that knowledge must be other than the knower and originated. This passage dismantles that inference using two arguments: one based on the nature of knowledge itself, and a reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Notice the two distinct arguments: one about knowledge, one about the knower.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says that the wise act's indication of knowledge implies that knowledge is other than the knower and is originated (temporal).
3
Understand the first refutation
Al-Ash'ari argues that knowledge is known as knowledge regardless of otherness or temporality. There exist things that are other or originated but not knowledge, so being other/originated is not necessary for knowledge.
This shows that the attribute 'knowledge' does not entail otherness or creation.
4
Understand the second refutation
Al-Ash'ari uses a reductio: if claiming knowledge is knowledge implies temporality/otherness, then claiming the knower is knowing would imply temporality and diversity in essence—which is absurd.
This shows the opponent's logic leads to an unacceptable conclusion.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Al-Ash'ari is not denying that God's attributes are distinct; he is refuting that the inference from a wise act proves those attributes are separate or created.
The argument preserves the unity of God's essence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وممّا يُبْطِلُ قول من قال : إنّ دلالة الفعل الحِكْمي على علم العالم منا ، دلالة على أنّه غيرُ العَالِم، وأنه مُحدَث : أنّ العالِمَ للعلم، ما كانَ عالما لا للغَيرية ولا للحدث، فوجب أن تكونَ الدلالة على أنّ العالم عالمٌ دِلالةً على العلم . و [أنّه ] لمْ يكُن العلمُ علما ؛ لأنه غيرُ العالم ولا لأنه مُحدَث؛ لوجودِ غَيرِ ليس بعلم، ومحدث ليسَ بِعلم، فلم يجب أن تكون الدلالة على أن العلم علم دلالة على أنه محدَثُ أو أنه غيرُ العالم . وأيضًا فلو جاز لزاعم أن يزعم أن الدلالة على أن العلمَ عِلْمٌ دلالة على حَدَثِهِ ، أَوْ دلالةٌ على أنّه غَيْرُ العالم : لجازَ لزاعمٍ أن يزعم أن الدلالة على أنّ العالِمَ [ عالمٌ] دِلالة على حَدَثِهِ، وأنه متغاير في ذاته .
And among what invalidates the statement of one who says that the indication of the wise act of the knowledge of the knower from us is an indication that it is other than the knower and that it is originated: that the knower of knowledge was not knowing by virtue of otherness or by virtue of temporality, so it is necessary that the indication that the knower is knowing is an indication of knowledge. And that knowledge was not knowledge because it is other than the knower nor because it is originated, because there exists other which is not knowledge, and originated which is not knowledge, so it is not necessary that the indication that knowledge is knowledge is an indication that it is originated or that it is other than the knower. And also, if it were permissible for a claimant to claim that the indication that knowledge is knowledge is an indication of its temporality or an indication that it is other than the knower, then it would be permissible for a claimant to claim that the indication that the knower is knowing is an indication of his temporality and that he is diverse in his essence.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage provides two refutations. First, Al-Ash'ari argues: if the wise act indicates the knower's knowledge, that does not mean knowledge is other than the knower or created. Why? Because the knower is known as knowing not due to otherness or temporality—rather, knowledge itself is evidence of being knowledge. Conversely, something can be other than the knower yet not be knowledge, and something can be created yet not be knowledge. So the attribute of being knowledge does not imply either otherness or creation. Second, he uses a reductio: if claiming that 'knowledge is knowledge' indicates its temporality or otherness, then claiming 'the knower is knowing' would also indicate temporality and diversity in essence—which is absurd. Thus the original claim is invalid.

Key Terms
غير العالم (ghayr al-'alim: other than the knower)
محدث (muhdath: originated/temporal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because we infer knowledge from wise acts, that knowledge must be a separate entity from the knower and created (temporal).
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the inference only goes to the existence of knowledge, not to its attributes. Knowledge can be eternal and intrinsic to the knower.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ash'ari, why does the indication that knowledge is knowledge not imply it is originated or other than the knower?
A) Because knowledge is identical to the knower.
B) Because there exist things that are other than the knower and not knowledge, and originated things not knowledge, so the category does not necessitate those attributes.
C) Because all knowledge is necessarily eternal.
D) Because the wise act only indicates the knower, not knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari shows that proving knowledge from a wise act does not prove that knowledge is a separate entity or created; the inference only goes as far as the existence of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe wisdom in the world, do you ever pause to consider that your inference about God's attributes might be incomplete? Will you commit to stopping at what the evidence actually proves, rather than adding your own assumptions?

Evidence for God's Power and Life
Al-Ash'ari extends the proof for knowledge to power and life, citing Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari extends the proof of God's knowledge to His power and life using Quranic verses.

Core Idea
God's power and life are proven by the same logic as His knowledge, supported by explicit Quranic texts.

Al-Ash'ari has already established that God has knowledge. He now argues that the same method proves God has power (qudra) and life (haya). He cites specific Quranic verses that attribute knowledge and strength to God, showing that these attributes are not identical to His essence but are real and distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that Al-Ash'ari begins by saying the evidence is 'like the evidence for knowledge'.
2
Identify the analogical reasoning
Al-Ash'ari asserts that the proof for power and life is analogous to the proof for knowledge. Ask yourself: what does that imply about the method?
He does not re-prove power and life; he extends the earlier proof.
3
Examine the Quranic citations
Find the three Quranic verses and note what each establishes: two for knowledge, one for strength (power).
The verse from Fussilat (41:15) uses 'strength' (quwwa), which Al-Ash'ari equates with power.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does Al-Ash'ari need to cite these verses? They provide scriptural support that God has attributes distinct from His essence.
This counters those who deny real attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أنَّ للهِ - تعالى - قدرة وحياةً كالدليل على أن لله -تعالى- علما . وقد قالَ اللهُ - جَلَّ ذِكْرُهُ - : ﴿أَنزَلَهُ بِعِلْمِهِ) [النساء : ١٦٦]، [الدليل ﴾ وقال: ﴿وَمَا تَحْمِلُ مِنْ أُنثَى وَلَا تَضَعُ إِلَّا بِعِلْمِهِ ﴾ [فاطر: ۱۱] فَثَبَّتَ العلم لنفسِهِ ، وقال - تعالى -: ﴿أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا أَنَّ قُوَّةً [فصلت : ١٥] فثَبَّتَ القوة لنفسِهِ . وممَّا يَدُلُّ على أن الله -تعالى- عالم بعلم، أنه لا يخلو : أن يكون الله تعالى عالما بنفسه، أو بعلم يستحيل أن يكون هو نفسَهُ : فإن كان عالما بنفسِهِ كانتْ نفسُهُ عِلما ، لأَن قائلا لو قال : إن الله - تعالى - عالِمٌ ، بمعنى هو غيره، لوجب عليه أن يكون ذلك المعنَى عِلمًا، ويستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ العِلمُ عالما، أو العالم علما ، أو يكونَ اللَّهُ - تعالى - بِمعنَى الصِّفَاتِ .
And the evidence that God—exalted—has power and life is like the evidence that God—exalted—has knowledge. And God—mighty is His mention—said: 'He sent it down with His knowledge' (4:166), and said: 'And no female conceives nor gives birth except with His knowledge' (35:11), so He established knowledge for Himself. And He—exalted—said: 'Do they not see that God who created them is more mighty in strength' (41:15), so He established strength for Himself. And among what indicates that God—exalted—is knowing by knowledge is that it is unavoidable: either God—exalted—is knowing by His essence, or by knowledge that cannot be that same essence. For if He were knowing by His essence, then His essence would be knowledge, because if someone said that God—exalted—is knowing, meaning that He is other than it, then it would be necessary that that meaning be knowledge. And it is impossible for knowledge to be knowing, or for the knower to be knowledge, or for God—exalted—to be [characterized] by the meaning of the attributes.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage consists of three parts. First, Al-Ash'ari asserts that the same reasoning used to prove God's knowledge also proves His power (قدرة) and life (حياة). Second, he cites Quranic verses: 'He sent it down with His knowledge' (An-Nisa 4:166) and 'no female conceives... except with His knowledge' (Fatir 35:11) to show that knowledge is attributed to God as a thing distinct from His essence. Similarly, the verse 'more mighty in strength' (Fussilat 41:15) establishes strength (قوة, i.e., power) for God. Third, he provides a logical proof that God knows by a knowledge that is not identical to His essence. The argument is a disjunction: either God knows by His essence, or by something else. If by His essence, then His essence would be knowledge itself—but that is impossible because knowledge is an attribute that is known, not the knower. If someone says 'God is knowing' meaning that He is other than knowledge, then that other (knowledge) must be knowledge. But knowledge cannot be the knower, nor can the knower be knowledge, nor can God be described by the meaning of attributes (i.e., the attributes are not identical to the essence but are real and distinct). Therefore, God knows by a knowledge that is distinct from His essence.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra: power)
حياة (haya: life)
قوة (quwwa: strength)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Quranic verses like 'with His knowledge' mean knowledge is part of God's essence, identical to Him.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari uses these verses to show that knowledge is something God has, not what He is. The phrase 'established knowledge for Himself' implies it is a distinct attribute.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Ash'ari claim about the evidence for God's power and life?
A) It is weaker than the evidence for knowledge.
B) It is like the evidence for knowledge.
C) It comes only from the Quran, not reason.
D) It is based solely on logical proof.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari uses analogical reasoning and Quranic citations to prove God's attributes of power and life are real and distinct from His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that God's attributes are real and distinct, not just concepts. Search your heart: do you live as if God's knowledge and power are active in your life today, or have you reduced them to intellectual propositions? The Logical Proof that God Knows by a Distinct Knowledge
Al-Ash'ari gives a logical disjunction to prove God knows by a distinct knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's logical argument that God's knowledge is a real attribute distinct from His essence.

Core Idea
If God knew by His essence, His essence would be knowledge—but that is impossible, so He knows by a distinct knowledge.

Al-Ash'ari presents a crisp logical disjunction: either God knows by His essence or by something else. By eliminating the first option, he establishes that knowledge is a real attribute added to the essence, not identical to it. This argument is crucial for the Ash'ari position that God's attributes are eternal and distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the logical argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: it begins with 'Among what indicates' and then presents a disjunction.
The argument is compact; take your time.
2
Identify the two options
Al-Ash'ari says: either God knows by His essence or by a knowledge that cannot be the essence itself. Find these two options in the text.
The second option is phrased as 'by knowledge that cannot be that same essence'.
3
Analyze the first option's consequence
If God knows by His essence, then His essence would be knowledge. Why? Because 'knowing' would be identical to the essence. Trace this reasoning.
Al-Ash'ari says: 'if He were knowing by His essence, then His essence would be knowledge.'
4
Understand why that is impossible
Knowledge cannot be the knower (the one who knows), and the knower cannot be knowledge. Also, God cannot be described by 'the meaning of the attributes' (i.e., the attributes are not the essence).
This shows that the first option leads to absurdity.
5
Conclude the proof
Since the first option is impossible, the second option must hold: God knows by a knowledge that is distinct from His essence.
This establishes that knowledge is a real attribute, not identical to God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أنَّ للهِ - تعالى - قدرة وحياةً كالدليل على أن لله -تعالى- علما . وقد قالَ اللهُ - جَلَّ ذِكْرُهُ - : ﴿أَنزَلَهُ بِعِلْمِهِ) [النساء : ١٦٦]، [الدليل ﴾ وقال: ﴿وَمَا تَحْمِلُ مِنْ أُنثَى وَلَا تَضَعُ إِلَّا بِعِلْمِهِ ﴾ [فاطر: ۱۱] فَثَبَّتَ العلم لنفسِهِ ، وقال - تعالى -: ﴿أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا أَنَّ قُوَّةً [فصلت : ١٥] فثَبَّتَ القوة لنفسِهِ . وممَّا يَدُلُّ على أن الله -تعالى- عالم بعلم، أنه لا يخلو : أن يكون الله تعالى عالما بنفسه، أو بعلم يستحيل أن يكون هو نفسَهُ : فإن كان عالما بنفسِهِ كانتْ نفسُهُ عِلما ، لأَن قائلا لو قال : إن الله - تعالى - عالِمٌ ، بمعنى هو غيره، لوجب عليه أن يكون ذلك المعنَى عِلمًا، ويستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ العِلمُ عالما، أو العالم علما ، أو يكونَ اللَّهُ - تعالى - بِمعنَى الصِّفَاتِ .
And the evidence that God—exalted—has power and life is like the evidence that God—exalted—has knowledge. And God—mighty is His mention—said: 'He sent it down with His knowledge' (4:166), and said: 'And no female conceives nor gives birth except with His knowledge' (35:11), so He established knowledge for Himself. And He—exalted—said: 'Do they not see that God who created them is more mighty in strength' (41:15), so He established strength for Himself. And among what indicates that God—exalted—is knowing by knowledge is that it is unavoidable: either God—exalted—is knowing by His essence, or by knowledge that cannot be that same essence. For if He were knowing by His essence, then His essence would be knowledge, because if someone said that God—exalted—is knowing, meaning that He is other than it, then it would be necessary that that meaning be knowledge. And it is impossible for knowledge to be knowing, or for the knower to be knowledge, or for God—exalted—to be [characterized] by the meaning of the attributes.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage consists of three parts. First, Al-Ash'ari asserts that the same reasoning used to prove God's knowledge also proves His power (قدرة) and life (حياة). Second, he cites Quranic verses: 'He sent it down with His knowledge' (An-Nisa 4:166) and 'no female conceives... except with His knowledge' (Fatir 35:11) to show that knowledge is attributed to God as a thing distinct from His essence. Similarly, the verse 'more mighty in strength' (Fussilat 41:15) establishes strength (قوة, i.e., power) for God. Third, he provides a logical proof that God knows by a knowledge that is not identical to His essence. The argument is a disjunction: either God knows by His essence, or by something else. If by His essence, then His essence would be knowledge itself—but that is impossible because knowledge is an attribute that is known, not the knower. If someone says 'God is knowing' meaning that He is other than knowledge, then that other (knowledge) must be knowledge. But knowledge cannot be the knower, nor can the knower be knowledge, nor can God be described by the meaning of attributes (i.e., the attributes are not identical to the essence but are real and distinct). Therefore, God knows by a knowledge that is distinct from His essence.

Key Terms
عالم بعلم ('alim bi-'ilm: knowing by knowledge)
علم ('ilm: knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'God is knowing' means He simply is knowing without any attribute, i.e., His essence is knowledge.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that if that were so, His essence would be knowledge, which is impossible because the knower cannot be identical to knowledge. Therefore, 'knowing' refers to a distinct attribute.

Checkpoint
In the logical proof, what is the impossible consequence of God knowing by His essence?
A) God would have no knowledge.
B) His essence would become knowledge.
C) Knowledge would be created.
D) God would be ignorant.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari proves that God's knowledge is a real attribute distinct from His essence through a logical disjunction that eliminates the possibility of knowledge being identical to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate that God's knowledge is a real attribute distinct from His essence, does that make His knowledge feel more concrete and personal to you? In what specific situation today can you actively remind yourself that God knows you with a knowledge that is truly His, not merely a theoretical concept?
?
The logical proof here prevents us from simplifying God's nature into something we can fully grasp. How does accepting that God's attributes are distinct but not separate challenge your natural tendency to imagine God in human terms? What can you do today to humbly submit to the reality of God's transcendence in your worship?

The Analogical Proof for Divine Power and Life
Al-Ash'ari draws an analogy and cites Quranic evidence for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses an analogy and Quranic verses to prove that God has attributes of power and life, just as He has knowledge.

Core Idea
The proof for Allah's power and life is identical in method to the proof for His knowledge.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari presents a concise argument by analogy for divine attributes. He begins by stating that the rational proof for God's power (qudra) and life is parallel to the proof for His knowledge. To substantiate this, he cites three Quranic verses that explicitly attribute knowledge and power to God, demonstrating that revelation confirms what reason already supports. This lesson unpacks his method and the evidence he provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Note the structure: an opening analogy followed by three Quranic verses.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The passage first states that the proof for power/life is like the proof for knowledge. Then it provides scriptural evidence. See how the verses for knowledge (two verses) and power (one verse) are used.
3
Reflect on the method
Ask yourself: why does al-Ash'ari use an analogy? What does this tell us about his approach to proving attributes? He is showing consistency: if knowledge is proven by revelation, so are power and life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن لله تعالى قدرة وحياة كالدليل على أن لله تعالى علما . وقد قال الله جل ذكره : {أنزله بعلمه} [النساء:166] ، وقال: {وما تحمل من أنثى ولا تضع إلا بعلمه} [فاطر:11] فثبت العلم لنفسه ، وقال تعالى: {أو لم يروا أن الله الذي خلقهم هو أشد قوة} [فصلت:15] فثبت القوة لنفسه .
The proof that Allah Almighty has power and life is like the proof that Allah Almighty has knowledge. And Allah, may His mention be exalted, said: 'He revealed it with His knowledge' [an-Nisa 166], and He said: 'No female conceives nor gives birth except with His knowledge' [Fatir 11], so He established knowledge for Himself. And He, exalted, said: 'Do they not see that Allah who created them is greater in might/power' [Fussilat 15], so He established power/might for Himself.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is an argument by analogy. al-Ash'ari begins by stating that the proof for God's power and life is the same kind as the proof for His knowledge. He then cites three Quranic verses: the first two establish knowledge as an attribute of God (by stating that actions occur with His knowledge), and the third establishes power (quwwa) as an attribute. The method is to take explicit scriptural attributions and treat them as evidence for real attributes. This is a direct appeal to revelation, but it is placed within a rational framework: the proof for one attribute is structurally identical to the proof for another.

Key Terms
قدرة
حياة
علم
قوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quranic references to God's attributes are merely metaphorical and do not indicate real, distinct attributes.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that the explicit attribution of knowledge and power in the verses proves that these are real attributes of God, not mere metaphors.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari proves divine power and life by analogy with knowledge, supported by explicit Quranic verses.

The Proof That Allah Knows by a Distinct Attribute
al-Ash'ari presents a rational proof that God's knowledge is an attribute distinct from His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's rational argument that Allah's knowledge is an attribute distinct from His essence, and why the alternative (knowing by essence) is logically impossible.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge is an attribute, not His essence – the proof from the meaning of 'knower'.

Al-Ash'ari begins by laying out a logical dilemma: either Allah knows by His essence or by a distinct attribute of knowledge. He then argues that the first option is impossible because it would entail calling His essence 'knowledge', which is an absurd confusion of subject and attribute. This concise proof establishes the foundational distinction in Ash'ari theology between the divine essence (dhat) and attributes (sifat).

Guided Path
1
Understand the disjunction
Notice that al-Ash'ari starts with an exclusive either/or: either Allah knows by His essence or by a knowledge that is not His essence. This frames the entire argument.
2
Examine the consequence of knowing by essence
If Allah knew by His essence, then His essence would itself be knowledge. Trace why that follows: if one says 'Allah is knowing' meaning that He is something else (attribute), that something else must be knowledge.
3
See the impossibility
Al-Ash'ari shows that it is impossible for knowledge to be the knower, or for the knower to be knowledge, or for Allah to be described as attributes (plural). He uses the human analogy: power is not knowledge.
4
Conclude the correct position
Since knowing by essence is impossible, the only remaining option is that Allah knows by a knowledge that is not His essence. This establishes the attribute as distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على أن الله تعالى عالم بعلم، أنه لا يخلو: أن يكون الله تعالى عالما بنفسه، أو بعلم يستحيل أن يكون هو نفسه: فإن كان عالما بنفسه كانت نفسه علما، لأن قائلا لو قال: إن الله تعالى عالم، بمعنى هو غيره، لوجب عليه أن يكون ذلك المعنى علما، ويستحيل أن يكون العلم عالما، أو العالم علما، أو يكون الله تعالى بمعنى الصفات. ألا ترى أن الطريق الذي يعلم [به] أن العلم علم [هو] أن العالم به علم؛ لأن قدرة الإنسان التي لا يعلم بها لا يجوز أن تكون علما، فلما استحال أن يكون الباري تعالى علما استحال أن يكون عالما لنفسه، فإذا استحال ذلك صح أنه عالم بعلم يستحيل أن يكون هو نفسه.
And among what indicates that Allah Almighty is knowing with knowledge is that it is inevitable: either Allah Almighty is knowing by His self, or by a knowledge which it is impossible that it be His self. For if He were knowing by His self, then His self would be knowledge, because if someone were to say: 'Allah Almighty is knowing' meaning 'He is something else,' then that something else would have to be knowledge. And it is impossible for knowledge to be knowing, or for the knower to be knowledge, or for Allah Almighty to be by way of the attributes. Do you not see that the way by which it is known that knowledge is knowledge is that the knower knows by it; because the power of a human which is not known by it is not permissible for it to be knowledge. So when it became impossible for the Creator Almighty to be knowledge, it became impossible for Him to be knowing by His self. So when that is impossible, it is correct that He is knowing with a knowledge which it is impossible that it be His self.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) for the distinction between God's essence and His attributes. al-Ash'ari sets up an exclusive disjunction: either God knows by His essence (bi nafsihi) or by a meaning (ma'na) that is not His essence. He then eliminates the first option. If God were knowing by His essence, then His essence would itself be knowledge. But the term 'knowing' ('alim) implies a relationship: the knower knows by means of knowledge. Knowledge is that by which the knower knows. If one says 'God is knowing' meaning that He is something else (i.e., an attribute), that something else must be knowledge. It is impossible for knowledge itself to be 'knowing' (because knowledge is an attribute, not a subject), nor for the knower to be identical with knowledge, nor for God to be described as 'attributes' (meaning multiple). He then gives an analogy: the way we know that something is knowledge is that the knower knows by it. A human's power (qudra) which is not known by it cannot be knowledge. Since it is impossible for the Creator to be knowledge, it is impossible for Him to be knowing by His essence. Therefore, He must be knowing by knowledge that is not His essence. This is a refined argument that uses the logical impossibility of a subject being identical to its own attribute.

Key Terms
عالم
علم
نفس
معنى
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular or assumes what it tries to prove.
Correction: Actually, it starts from the meaning of the terms 'knower' and 'knowledge' and uses logical elimination; it does not assume the conclusion.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, if God were knowing by His essence, what would follow?
A) His essence would be power
B) His essence would be knowledge
C) He would not be knowing
D) He would have multiple attributes

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari proves that Allah's knowledge must be an attribute distinct from His essence because the alternative leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that Allah's knowledge is not His essence but an attribute fully conveying His omniscience. Does this distinction make you feel more accountable, knowing that His knowledge of your every thought and deed is an active attribute that directly relates to you? What will you do today to live in the light of His all-encompassing knowledge?
?
If you truly believe that Allah’s knowledge is perfect and distinct from His essence, why do you sometimes act as if He does not see your private sins? Let this lesson press you to confess and turn back to Him with sincerity.
?
This rational proof uses human reasoning to affirm a divine truth. Does your heart submit to the findings of sound reason when they align with revelation, or do you harbor doubts that stem from arrogance? Identify one area where you have resisted a clear truth from the Qur'an or Sunnah because it clashed with your intellect, and repent from that pride today.

Refutation of the Objection and Generalization to All Attributes
Al-Ash'ari refutes an objection and generalizes to all essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari refutes the objection that God can be knowing without an essence or attribute, and how the argument extends to all essential attributes.

Core Idea
A predicate must refer to something; otherwise it is meaningless.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari addresses a clever objection: what if God is knowing but not by His essence and not by an attribute? The response is a powerful logical principle: if a predicate like 'knowing' referred to nothing at all, it would be meaningless. This refutation then becomes a general proof for all of God's essential attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let al-Ash'ari's argument come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection that the opponent raises: that God is knowing not by Himself and not by a meaning. Notice the wording.
The objector proposes a third possibility.
3
Understand the refutation's logic
Trace how al-Ash'ari refutes it: if the objection were allowed, the word 'knowing' would refer to nothing. Since that is impossible, the objection fails.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
See the generalization
Notice that the same logic applies to all essential attributes: life, power, hearing, sight, etc. The proof for knowledge becomes a pattern.
This shows the coherence of the Ash'ari doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: ما أنكرتم أن يكون الباري سبحانه عالما؛ لا بنفسه، ولا بمعنى يستحيل أن يكون هو نفسه. قيل له: لو جاز هذا، لجاز أن يكون قولنا: «عالم»: لم يرجع به إلى نفسه، ولا إلى معنى، ولم يثبت به نفسه، ولا معنى يستحيل أن يكون هو نفسه. وإذا لم يجز هذا بطل ما قالوه. وهذا الدليل يدل على إثبات صفات الله تعالى لذاته كلها: من الحياة، والقدرة، والسمع والبصر، وسائر صفات الذات.
If someone says: Why do you deny that the Creator, glorified, is knowing—neither by Himself, nor by a meaning which it is impossible that it be His self? It is said to him: If this were permissible, then it would be permissible for our saying 'knowing' to refer neither to His self nor to a meaning, and it would not establish His self nor a meaning which is impossible to be His self. And if this is not permissible, then what they said is invalid. And this proof indicates the establishment of all the attributes of Allah Almighty for His essence: life, power, hearing, sight, and the rest of the attributes of essence.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector suggests a third possibility: that God is knowing, but not by His essence and not by an attribute (ma'na). The response is straightforward: if this were allowed, then the term 'knowing' would not refer to anything—neither to the essence nor to a meaning. But the term 'knowing' must refer to either the essence or a meaning; otherwise, language becomes meaningless. Since this is impossible, the objection fails. The passage then concludes that this entire proof (from the previous lesson) establishes all the essential attributes: life, power, hearing, sight, and others. This generalizes the argument from knowledge to all attributes.

Key Terms
صفات الذات
الحياة
القدرة
السمع
البصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection that God can be knowing without an essence and without an attribute is a valid third possibility.
Correction: Every predicate must refer to either the essence or an attribute; a third option is meaningless because the term would refer to nothing.

Checkpoint
In the objection, the objector claims God is knowing ______.
A) by His essence
B) by a meaning
C) neither by essence nor meaning
D) by both

Key Takeaway
The proof that God is knowing by an attribute also applies to all essential attributes, establishing a unified argument for the Ash'ari doctrine of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Knowing,' do you truly believe that His knowledge is real and not just a title? How does Al-Ash'ari's logical argument challenge any casual or empty use of Allah's names in your speech?

If someone asked you to defend the reality of Allah's attributes, would you instinctively turn to this logical proof? What does your answer reveal about how seriously you take the intellectual defense of your faith?
?
Al-Ash'ari generalizes the proof to all essential attributes. Is there any attribute of Allah (e.g., His hearing or seeing) that you have diminished in practice, living as if He does not watch over you? Confess one specific moment today where you acted as if He did not see. What will you change?

Refuting the Identity of Will and Action
Ash'ari refutes the claim that God's will is identical to His action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari uses a reductio ad absurdum to refute the claim that God's will is identical to His action, thereby defending the distinction of divine attributes.

Core Idea
If will equals action, then speech and knowledge would also collapse into action.

This passage presents a classic kalam dialectical exchange. An opponent suggests that God's willing something simply means He does it, making will identical to action. Ash'ari responds not by asserting dogma, but by showing the logical consequences of that position, leading to absurdity. The argument forces the opponent to abandon their claim or accept further unacceptable claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and understand the opponent's position
Identify the opponent's claim: that God's will is nothing but His action, and 'willing' means 'doing'.
The opponent is likely a Mu'tazilī denying a distinct attribute of will.
2
Follow Ash'ari's reductio: from will to speech to knowledge
Notice how Ash'ari first extends the logic to speech (qawl) and then to knowledge ('ilm), showing that the same reasoning would eliminate multiple attributes.
The argument is structured as a chain of hypotheticals — 'if this, then that'.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Consider why collapsing attributes into action is problematic for Sunni theology: it leads to denying God's real speech and knowledge.
This shows the importance of maintaining distinct attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-ma'ānī).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : ما أنكرتم أن يكون معنى أن الله تعالى أراد الشيء : أنه فَعَله ، وهو مريد له في الحقيقة، بمعنى أنه فاعل له؟ قيل له : لو جاز هذا لقائله، لجَازَ لزاعم أَنْ يَزعَمَ أَنَّ الله عز وجل قائل للشيء في الحقيقة كن، ويزعم أنَّ معنى ذلك : أنه يُكَوِّنه، فيثبت لله تعالى قولا في الحقيقة هو المقول له، كما زعمتم أن الله تعالى إرادة في الحقيقة هي مُراده، ولو جاز لزاعم أن يزعم هذا، جاز لآخر أن يقول : إِنَّ عِلمَ اللَّهِ تعالى بالشيء هو فعله له
If someone says: What prevents you from [accepting] that the meaning of Allah - exalted - willing something is that He did it, and He is willing it in reality, meaning that He is the doer of it? It is said to him: If this were permissible for its proponent, then it would be permissible for a claimant to claim that Allah - mighty and majestic - is in reality a sayer to the thing 'Be', and to claim that the meaning of that is that He brings it into being, thus affirming for Allah - exalted - a saying in reality which is the thing said to it, just as you claimed that Allah - exalted - has a will in reality which is the thing willed. And if it were permissible for a claimant to claim this, it would be permissible for another to say: Indeed Allah's knowledge of the thing is His action of it.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (qīl wa qāla) dialectical argument. The opponent (likely a Mu'tazilī) argues that God's will ('irāda) is nothing but His action (fi'l) — i.e., there is no distinct attribute of will. The author (Ash'ari) responds with a reductio ad absurdum: if that were so, then one could equally claim that God's speech (qawl) is nothing but His act of bringing into being (takwīn), thus denying speech as a real attribute. Worse still, another could claim that God's knowledge ('ilm) is also identical to His action. This would lead to the elimination of all divine attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-ma'ānī), which is unacceptable. The argument proceeds in a stepwise logical fashion, showing that the opponent's principle is self-defeating.

Key Terms
إرادة
فعل
قول
علم
تكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage denies any connection between God's will and His action.
Correction: Ash'ari denies the identity of will and action, not their relation. He affirms that will is a real attribute distinct from action.

Checkpoint
What does Ash'ari argue would follow if one accepts that God's will is identical to His action?
A) That God's speech is identical to His action
B) That God's mercy is identical to His action
C) That God's power is identical to His action
D) That God's hearing is identical to His action

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari shows that identifying will with action opens the door to eliminating all distinct divine attributes, which is rationally unacceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the opponent's claim that will equals action, did you think it was a minor point? But Ash'ari shows it leads to eliminating knowledge and speech. Reflect: Are there areas in your own understanding of Allah where you might be tempted to reduce one attribute to another for convenience? How does this lesson challenge you to be precise?
?
Ash'ari's reductio exposes the logical consequences of a seemingly harmless statement. Think of a time when you accepted a simplified version of a religious concept without considering its implications. How can this lesson train you to think more carefully about the attributes of Allah?
?
The opponent's claim might seem appealing for its simplicity. Yet Ash'ari shows it unravels. Are there teachings about Allah that you find difficult to accept because they seem complex? Does this lesson help you trust that even logical arguments defend the complexity of divine attributes?

The Wall Metaphor: Why Creatures' Limitations Don't Apply to God
Ash'ari responds to an objection from the Quranic wall verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ash'ari's argument that metaphorical usage of attributes like 'will' for creatures does not imply that God's attributes are metaphorical.

Core Idea
What is impossible for creatures is not impossible for God.

In this passage, Ash'ari faces a clever objection: if the Quran can say a wall 'wants' to fall metaphorically, then why can't God's command 'Be' be metaphorical too? Ash'ari responds by drawing a crucial distinction between what is possible for created things and what is possible for the Creator. This distinction is central to understanding how we speak about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's objection
The opponent cites the Quranic verse about a wall 'wanting' to collapse as evidence that 'will' can be used metaphorically.
2
Understand the opponent's challenge
If 'will' is metaphorical for the wall, why shouldn't God's command 'Be' also be metaphorical?
The opponent tries to extend the metaphor to divine attributes.
3
Study Ash'ari's key distinction
Ash'ari points out that a wall, being inanimate, cannot truly will, but God can truly will and speak.
The difference lies in the nature of the subject: creature vs. Creator.
4
Apply the distinction to the argument
Therefore, God's saying 'Be' is literal, not metaphorical, because what is impossible for a creature is possible for God.
This preserves the reality of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : أليس قد قال الله تعالى : ﴿ جِدَارًا يُرِيدُ أَن يَنقَضَ ﴾ ولا إرادة للجدار في الحقيقة، وإنما قال : يريد توسعا، والمعنى : أنه ينقض. قيل له : نعم. فإن قال : فما أنكرتم أن يكون معنى : أن تقول له كن أي : نكونه فيكون؟ قيل له : الفرق بين ذلك، أن الجماد يستحيل - مع جماديته - أن يكون مريدا ، والبارى تعالى في الحقيقة لا يستحيل عليه أن يريد، أو يقول؛ فلذلك لم يكن قوله : أن نقول له كن فيكون بمعنى : نكونه.
If someone says: Did not Allah - exalted - say: 'A wall that wants to collapse' and there is no will for the wall in reality, but He said 'wants' by way of extension, and the meaning is that it collapses? It is said to him: Yes. Then he says: So what prevents you from [accepting] that the meaning of 'that We say to it Be' is that We bring it into being and it becomes? It is said to him: The distinction between that is that the inanimate object is impossible - given its inanimateness - to be willing, while the Creator - exalted - in reality it is not impossible for Him to will or to speak; therefore His statement 'that We say to it Be and it becomes' is not in the sense of 'We bring it into being'.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection from the opponent and Ash'ari's answer. The opponent cites the Quranic verse about a wall 'wanting' to collapse (Sūrat al-Kahf 18:77). This is clearly metaphorical, since a wall cannot truly will. The opponent argues that if 'will' can be used metaphorically for an inanimate object, then why can't God's word 'Be' (kun) be metaphorical for the act of creation? Ash'ari responds with a crucial distinction: an inanimate object cannot possess will in reality, but God can and does possess will and speech in reality. Therefore, the metaphorical usage for a creature does not entail that God's attributes are metaphorical. The literal meaning for God is preserved.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a word is used metaphorically for a creature, it must be metaphorical for God as well.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that the impossibility of a creature having the attribute (e.g., will) in reality does not apply to God; thus the metaphorical usage for creatures does not force a metaphorical interpretation for God.

Checkpoint

A) The wall is inanimate and cannot truly will, but God can truly will
B) The wall is animate and truly wills, but God's will is different
C) The wall metaphor shows that all will is metaphorical
D) The wall verse is not part of the Quran

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari shows that metaphorical language in the Quran about creatures does not force metaphorical interpretation of God's attributes, because what is impossible for a creature is not impossible for the Creator.

?
Think of an attribute of God you have reduced to a metaphor in your heart—like His speech or His power. What would it look like for you to live today as if that attribute is literally true, not just a convenient idea? Write down one practical consequence.

Absurd Consequences of Equating Will and Action
Ash'ari presents two arguments against equating will with action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari uses consistency and absurdity arguments to refute the claim that God's will is identical to His action.

Core Idea
If will equals action, then God has no superiority over a collapsing wall.

Ash'ari continues his critique of those who say God's will is merely His act of creation. He shows that this view leads to two devastating consequences: first, it would make God's will meaningless; second, it would strip God of any distinction from inanimate objects. The passage uses analogies to expose the logical contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Allow the author's argument to come through before interpreting.
The passage is cut off at the end but contains two complete arguments.
2
Understand the first argument (consistency)
Ash'ari uses an analogy: if the opponent says God's speech ('Be') means creation, then by the same logic, God's 'We willed it' would mean He did it without actually willing. This would be more consistent with their method but is absurd.
3
Understand the second argument (inanimate objects)
If will equals action, then an inanimate object's motion would count as its will. This would rob God of any superiority in will over a stone.
This shows the claim strips God of a distinguishing attribute.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why these consequences are unacceptable in Islamic theology. How does this strengthen the Ash'ari position on real will?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا : فلو كان قوله : أن نقول له ليس معناه إثبات قول له، وإنما معناه : أن نكونه، كما أن قوله : جدارا يريد أن ينقض معناه : أن ينقض، لجاز لزاعم أن يزعم أن معنى قوله : أردناه فعلناه، وهو في الحقيقة لا يريد فعله، كما أن قوله : جدارا يريد أن ينقض معناه : أنه ينقض، وهذا أولى في حقيقة القياس. وإذا لم يجز هذا لم يجز ما قلتموه.

ويقال لهم : إذا كان معنى أن الله تعالى أراد فعل الشيء : أنه فعله، ومعنى أراد حركة الشيء : أنه حركه، فما أنكرتم أن يكون الجماد في الحقيقة مريدا لحركة نفسه بمعنى أنه متحرك، وأن لا يكون للباري تعالى على الجماد مزية في الإرادة، وأن لا يكون له مزية على من وقع فعله وهو غير مريد له؛ لأنه قد حصل له
And also: If His statement 'that We say to it' does not mean affirming a saying for Him, but rather its meaning is that We bring it into being, just as His statement 'a wall that wants to collapse' means that it collapses, then it would be permissible for a claimant to claim that the meaning of His statement 'We willed it' is that We did it, while in reality He does not will His action, just as His statement 'a wall that wants to collapse' means that it collapses. And this is more appropriate in the reality of analogy. And if this is not permissible, then what you have said is not permissible.

And it is said to them: If the meaning of Allah - exalted - willing an action is that He did it, and the meaning of willing a motion is that He set it in motion, then what prevents you from [accepting] that the inanimate object is in reality willing its own motion in the sense that it is moving, and that the Creator - exalted - has no superiority over the inanimate object in will, and that He has no superiority over one whose action occurs while he does not will it? Because he has obtained...
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage contains two final arguments. First, Ash'ari uses a consistency argument: if the opponent insists that God's speech ('Be') is metaphorical for creation (takwīn), then by the same logic, God's statement 'We willed it' would be metaphorical for doing it without actual will. This would be even more consistent with the opponent's analogy, yet leads to the absurdity that God does not will His actions. Since this is unacceptable, the opponent's position is inconsistent. Second, Ash'ari points out a further absurd consequence: if will equals action, then an inanimate object's motion would make it 'willing' its motion, so God would have no superiority in will over a stone. This shows that denying real will strips God of any distinction in the attribute. The argument is cut off but the direction is clear.

Misconception: One might think that defining will as action is a harmless simplification.
Correction: Ash'ari shows it leads to God having no superiority over inanimate objects, which is theologically unacceptable.

Checkpoint

A) Inanimate objects would have will in the same sense as God
B) Inanimate objects would become gods
C) God would have no will at all
D) Inanimate objects would speak

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari demonstrates that equating will with action logically eliminates any distinction between God's will and that of lifeless objects.

Proof of Allah's Eternal Speech and Refutation of an Objection
Imam Ashari proves Allah's eternal speech and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Ashari's rational proof for Allah's eternal speech based on the impossibility of an eternal opposite, and how he defends it against an objection.

Core Idea
An eternal attribute of silence would make speech impossible forever.

Imam Ashari presents a powerful argument: if Allah were not always speaking, He would have an eternal opposite like silence, which could never cease, preventing Him from ever speaking. But we know He speaks (through revelation), so He must have always been speaking. This lesson walks through that proof and the objection it faces.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the opening clarification about a Quranic verse, then the proof, and finally the objection and answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the proof's structure
The proof is a conditional chain: if Allah were not eternally speaking → He would have an opposite (silence/defect) → that opposite would be eternal → an eternal cannot cease → so Allah could never acquire speech. This contradicts revelation.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Understand the objection and its answer
The opponent asks why not-speaking necessarily implies an opposite. Imam Ashari answers by analogy: for any living being, absence of speech implies its opposite; no evidence exempts Allah from this rule.
This refutes the opponent's skepticism.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the alternative leads to absurdity, it is established that Allah has never ceased to be speaking. This is a cornerstone of Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في القرآن والإرادة
فإن قال : فما معنى قوله - تعالى -: ﴿قَالَتَا أَتَيْنَا طَابِعِينَ﴾ [فصلت : ١١] قيل له : معنى ذلك أنهما قالتا في الحقيقة : أَتَيْنَا طَابِعِينَ.

ومما يدل من القياس على أن الله - تعالى - لم يزل متكلما : أنه لو كان لم يزل غير متكلم، وهو ممن لا يستحيل عليه الكلام، لكان موصوفًا بضد من أضداد الكلام من السكوت أو الآفة.

ولو كان لم يزل موصوفًا بضد الكلام لكان ضِدُّ الكلام قديما، ولو كان ضد الكلام قديما لاستحال أن يُعْدَمَ، وأن يتكلَّمَ الباري؛ لأنَّ القديم لا يجوزُ عدمه، كما لا يجوزُ حُدُوتُه، فكان يَجِبُ أن لا يكون الباري -تعالى- قائلًا ولا آمرا ولا ناهيا على وجه من الوجوه.

وهذا فاسد، عندنا وعندهم . وإذا فسد هذا صح وثبت أنَّ البارِيَ لم يزل متكلما قائلا .

فإن قال قائل : ولم زَعمتُم أنه لو كان لم يزل غير متكلّم، لكانَ موصوفا بضد الكلام؟

قيل له : لأنَّ الحيّ إذا لم يكن موصوفا بالكلام، كانَ موصوفًا بضده، كما أنه إذا لم يكن موصوفًا بالعلم، كان موصوفًا بضده. وذلك أنَّ الحيَّ فيما بيننا ذلِكَ حُكمُه، ولم تقم دلالة على حيّ يخلو مِنَ الكلام وأضداده في الغائب، كما لم تقم دلالة على حيّ
Chapter on Speech and Will. For if he says: What is the meaning of His statement - Exalted is He -: 'They said, We come willingly' [Fussilat 41:11]? It is said to him: The meaning of that is that they said in reality: We come willingly. And among what indicates by analogy that Allah - Exalted is He - has never ceased to be speaking: that if He had never ceased to be not-speaking, while He is one for whom speech is not impossible, He would be described with an opposite of the opposites of speech, such as silence or defect. And if He had never ceased to be described with an opposite of speech, then the opposite of speech would be eternal. And if the opposite of speech were eternal, it would be impossible that it should cease to exist and that the Creator should speak, because the eternal cannot undergo non-existence, just as it cannot undergo origination. So it would be necessary that the Creator - Exalted is He - should not be a speaker, nor a commander, nor a prohibitor in any way whatsoever. And this is false, according to us and according to them. And if this is false, then it is correct and established that the Creator has never ceased to be speaking, a speaker. Then if a speaker says: And why do you claim that if He had never ceased to be not-speaking, He would be described with an opposite of speech? It is said to him: Because a living being, when it is not described with speech, is described with its opposite, just as when it is not described with knowledge, it is described with its opposite. And that is because the living being among us has that judgment, and no evidence has been established for a living being free of speech and its opposites among the unseen, just as no evidence has been established for a living being
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage combines a response to a specific Quranic objection, a rational proof for Allah's eternal speech, and an objection-and-answer. It begins by addressing a question about the verse where the heavens and earth say 'We come willingly' (Fussilat 41:11), clarifying that this is real speech. Then the author presents a proof from analogy (قياس): if Allah were not eternally speaking, He would have an opposite of speech (such as silence or defect). That opposite would be eternal (since Allah is eternal), and an eternal attribute cannot cease to exist. Therefore, if that opposite were eternal, Allah could never acquire speech, which is false because He does speak (as evidenced by revelation). This proves that Allah has always been speaking. An objector then questions why not-speaking necessarily implies an opposite. The answer relies on the observed rule for living beings: any living being either has speech or its opposite; no evidence exempts Allah from this rule, so the same applies to Him. The argument is based on the kalam principle that an eternal attribute cannot be removed.

Key Terms
كلام
ضد
قديم
حي
آفة
سكوت
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes what it tries to prove.
Correction: The proof is not circular; it uses a premise accepted by the opponent (an eternal cannot cease to exist) and shows that the alternative leads to a contradiction with revelation.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Ashari argue that if Allah were not eternally speaking, He could never speak?
A) Because a not-speaking state would be an eternal opposite that cannot cease, preventing future speech.
B) Because any living being must have speech or its opposite, and Allah is not exempt.
C) Because the Quran explicitly states that Allah has always been speaking.
D) Because the opponents claim that Allah cannot speak at all.

Key Takeaway
Allah's eternal speech is proven by the impossibility of an eternal silence, which would prevent Him from ever speaking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If an eternal silence would mean you could never hear a single word from Allah, what does the fact that He speaks reveal about His desire for a relationship with you? Are you treating His words as a distant relic or as a living command for today?
?
The proof shows that Allah must have always been speaking. When you read the Quran, do you recognize it as the very speech of the One who could not have remained silent? What would change in your urgency to obey if you truly believed that?
?
Allah's speech is eternal and unchangeable. Have you ever wished He had said something different about your life, your struggles, or your future? How does the perfection of His eternal words challenge you to submit without reservation?

God's Eternal Speech: Proof by Analogy with Knowledge
Al-Ash'ari argues God is eternally speaking using analogy with knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses analogy from the attribute of knowledge to prove that God is eternally speaking.

Core Idea
Speech is to God as knowledge is to God: both are eternal and cannot be absent.

This passage from al-Ash'ari's Al-Luma presents a key argument for the eternity of God's speech. The author anticipates an objection and systematically refutes it by drawing a parallel between divine speech and divine knowledge. By the end, you will see why God cannot be without speech or its opposite, just as He cannot be without knowledge or its opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow. Note the logical connectors like 'فإن قال' (if someone says) and 'قيل له' (it is said to him).
The passage uses a dialectical style common in kalam.
2
Identify the main claim and the objection
The passage starts with a claim: if the opposite of speech were eternal, God could not speak. Then an objection is raised: why does non-speaking imply the opposite of speech? Find these two parts.
3
Analyze the analogy with knowledge
Al-Ash'ari argues that just as a living being either has knowledge or its opposite, so it either has speech or its opposite. No third state exists. Trace how he uses this analogy to reject the objection.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that God must be eternally speaking. See how the impossibility of a living being without knowledge or its opposite forces the same for speech, and how the argument closes by reinforcing that point.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قديما ، ولو كان ضد الكلام قديما لاستحال أن يُعْدَمَ، وأن يتكلَّمَ الباري؛ لأنَّ القديم لا يجوزُ عدمه ، كما لا يجوزُ حُدُوتُه ، فكان يَجِبُ أن لا يكون الباري -تعالى- قائلًا ولا آمرا ولا ناهيا على وجه من الوجوه.
وهذا فاسد، عندنا وعندهم . وإذا فسد هذا صح وثبت أنَّ البارِيَ لم يزل متكلما قائلا .
فإن قال قائل : ولم زَعمتُم أنه لو كان لم يزل غير متكلّم، لكانَ موصوفا بضد الكلام؟
قيل له : لأنَّ الحيّ إذا لم يكن موصوفا بالكلام، كانَ موصوفًا بضده، كما أنه إذا لم يكن موصوفًا بالعلم، كان موصوفًا بضده. وذلك أنَّ الحيَّ فيما بيننا ذلِكَ حُكمُه، ولم تقم دلالة على حيّ يخلو مِنَ الكلام وأضداده في الغائب، كما لم تقم دلالة على حيّ يخلو من العلم وأضداده ، حتَّى يكون لا موصوفًا بأنه عالم ولا بضد العلم . فقد اجتمع الأمرُ فيهما : أنَّهُ مستحيل، فيما بيننا ، حي غيرُ عالم ولا موصوف بضد العلم ، وأنه مستحيل فيما بيننا حي غير متكلّم ، وَلَا موصوف بضد الكلام، وأنه لم يقم على غير ذلك دلالة في الغائب. فلو جاز أحد الأمرين وهو حي غير متكلّم، ولا موصوف بضد الكلام، لجاز الأمر الآخر : وهو حي غير عالم، ولا موصوف بضد العلم .
وأيضا : فإنَّه يسْتَحيلُ - فيما بينَنا - عالِمٌ يُوصَفُ بضدّ العِلْمِ مَعَ عِلْمِهِ، ومُتَكَلِّمٌ يوصف بضدّ الكلام مَعَ كَلامِهِ، فلما اجتمعا في الإِحَالَةِ ، وجَبَ أن يكونَ مَنْ جَوَّزَ مُتَكَلِّمًا في الغائِبِ، يُوصَفُ بضَدِّ الكلام مع كلامِهِ ، كمن جوَّزَ عالمًا في الغائبِ يوصف بضدّ العِلمِ معَ عِلمه .
فكذلك يجب أيضًا : لما استحال فيما بيننا حي غير عالم، ولا موصوف بضدّ العِلم، وجَبَ أن يستحيل فيما بيننا حي غير متكلّم،
Eternally, and if the opposite of speech were eternal, it would be impossible for it to cease and for the Creator to speak; because the eternal cannot cease to exist, just as it cannot come into existence. So it would be necessary that the Creator (exalted) not be a speaker, nor a commander, nor a prohibitor in any way. This is false, according to us and them. When this is false, it is correct and established that the Creator has always been a speaker, a sayer. If someone says: 'Why do you claim that if He had always been non-speaking, He would be described by the opposite of speech?' It is said to him: Because a living being, if not described by speech, is described by its opposite, just as if it is not described by knowledge, it is described by its opposite. For a living being among us has that ruling, and no evidence has been established for a living being free of speech and its opposites in the unseen, just as no evidence has been established for a living being free of knowledge and its opposites, such that it is neither described as knowing nor by the opposite of knowledge. So the matter is common to both: it is impossible, among us, for a living being to be neither knowing nor described by the opposite of knowledge, and it is impossible among us for a living being to be neither speaking nor described by the opposite of speech, and no evidence has been established for anything else in the unseen. So if one of the two matters were allowed—that is, a living being neither speaking nor described by the opposite of speech—the other matter would be allowed: a living being neither knowing nor described by the opposite of knowledge. Also, it is impossible among us for a knowing being to be described by the opposite of knowledge while having knowledge, and for a speaking being to be described by the opposite of speech while having speech. Since they share in impossibility, it must be that whoever allows a speaker in the unseen to be described by the opposite of speech while having speech is like one who allows a knower in the unseen to be described by the opposite of knowledge while having knowledge. Likewise, it is also necessary: since it is impossible among us for a living being to be neither knowing nor described by the opposite of knowledge, it must be impossible among us for a living being to be non-speaking.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ash'ari's argument that God is eternally speaking. It is a proof by analogy with the attribute of knowledge. The passage begins by stating that if the opposite of speech (i.e., silence) were eternal, it could not cease, and thus God could not speak—a clearly false conclusion. Therefore, God must always have been speaking. Then an objection is raised: why does being non-speaking necessarily imply being described by the opposite of speech? The answer is that in our experience, a living being either has speech or its opposite; there is no third state. Similarly, a living being either has knowledge or its opposite. Since no evidence shows that the unseen (God) can be free of both speech and its opposite, the same ruling applies: God must have speech. The argument proceeds to show that the two attributes (knowledge and speech) are analogous: just as it is impossible for a knower to possess both knowledge and its opposite simultaneously, so it is impossible for a speaker to possess both speech and its opposite. Therefore, if someone allows a speaker in the unseen to be described by the opposite of speech while speaking, they would be inconsistent, because they would reject the same for knowledge. Finally, the conclusion is reinforced: since in our experience a living being cannot be without knowledge or its opposite, likewise it cannot be without speech or its opposite; hence God must be eternally speaking.

Key Terms
كلام
قديم
ضد
علم
حي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's not speaking at a given moment implies He is described by silence.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that God's speech is eternal, not temporal, so He is always speaking. The opposite of speech (silence) cannot be eternal, else God could never speak.

Checkpoint
What analogy does al-Ash'ari use to prove that God must be eternally speaking?
A) The analogy of life and death
B) The analogy of knowledge and its opposite
C) The analogy of power and weakness
D) The analogy of will and compulsion

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari proves that God is eternally speaking by showing that, just as a living being cannot be without knowledge or its opposite, so God cannot be without speech or its opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's speech is eternal and unchanging, what does that require of you when you hear or recite the Qur'an? Do you approach it as if it is God speaking to you at this very moment, or do you treat it as a book of the past?
?
Al-Ash'ari uses analogy with knowledge to prove God's eternal speech. Reflect: Do you live as if God knows you completely and His speech (the Qur'an) addresses your exact situation? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that?
?
The argument shows that a living being cannot be without either speech or its opposite. Your own speech is a sign of life. How does your speech reflect your spiritual life? Are your words consistent with the speech of God that you claim to follow?
