The Impossibility of a Living Being Without Knowledge or Speech
Al-Ash'ari argues by analogy from the witnessed to the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's argument that a living being cannot be devoid of both a quality and its opposite, and how this applies to God's attributes of knowledge and speech.

Core Idea
A living being must have either a quality or its opposite; God, as Living, must have knowledge and speech.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari establishes a foundational principle of his theology: that every living being must be characterized either by a quality (like knowledge) or its opposite (ignorance), never by a neutral absence. He then applies this rule to God, arguing that since God is Living, He must possess both knowledge and speech. This analogy from the witnessed world to the unseen is a core method in Sunni kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Ash'ari uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the key principle
Al-Ash'ari starts by stating the impossibility of a living being being devoid of knowledge and its opposite. Find this statement and understand its significance.
The principle is that a living being must have either a quality or its opposite.
3
Track the analogy between knowledge and speech
See how al-Ash'ari extends the same reasoning from knowledge to speech. He argues that if one were possible, the other would be possible, which is absurd.
The analogy is based on the shared impossibility in the witnessed world.
4
Apply the conclusion to God
Al-Ash'ari concludes that since impossibility in the witnessed world applies to the unseen without contradictory evidence, God must have both knowledge and speech.
This refutes the idea that God could be neutral between these attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في القرآن والإرادة

يخلو من العلم وأضداده ، حتَّى يكون لا موصوفًا بأنه عالم ولا بضد العلم . فقد اجتمع الأمرُ فيهما : أنَّهُ مستحيل، فيما بيننا ، حي غير عالم ولا موصوف بضد العلم ، وأنه مستحيل فيما بيننا حي غير متكلّم ، وَلَا موصوف بضد الكلام، وأنه لم يقم على غير ذلك دلالة في الغائب. فلو جاز أحد الأمرين وهو حي غير متكلّم، ولا موصوف بضد الكلام، لجاز الأمر الآخر : وهو حي غير عالم، ولا موصوف بضد العلم .

وأيضا : فإنَّه يسْتَحيلُ - فيما بينَنا - عالِمٌ يُوصَفُ بضدّ العِلْمِ مَعَ عِلْمِهِ، ومُتَكَلِّمٌ يوصف بضدّ الكلام مَعَ كَلامِهِ، فلما اجتمعا في الإِحَالَةِ ، وجَبَ أن يكونَ مَنْ جَوَّزَ مُتَكَلِّمًا في الغائِبِ، يُوصَفُ بضَدِّ الكلام مع كلامِهِ ، كمن جوَّزَ عالمًا في الغائبِ يوصف بضدّ العِلمِ معَ عِلمه .

فكذلك يجب أيضًا : لما استحال فيما بيننا حي غير عالم، ولا موصوف بضدّ العِلم، وجَبَ أن يستحيل فيما بيننا حي غير متكلّم، ولا موصوف بضد الكلام. ووجب أن يستحيل ذلك في الغائِبِ، وَوَجَبَ أن يكونَ مَنْ جَوَّزَ أحد الأمريين في الغائِبِ كَمَنْ جوَّزَ الأمر الآخَرَ .
Chapter on Speech and Will

[The living being] is devoid of knowledge and its opposites, so that it is not described as knowing nor as having the opposite of knowledge. The matter has come together in both: that it is impossible, among us, for a living being to be not knowing and not described with the opposite of knowledge, and that it is impossible among us for a living being to be not speaking and not described with the opposite of speech, and that no indication has been established for other than that in the unseen. So if one of the two matters were permissible—a living being not speaking and not described with the opposite of speech—then the other matter would be permissible: a living being not knowing and not described with the opposite of knowledge.

And also: It is impossible—among us—for a knower to be described with the opposite of knowledge along with his knowledge, and for a speaker to be described with the opposite of speech along with his speech. So when they agree in impossibility, it must be that whoever permits a speaker in the unseen described with the opposite of speech along with his speech is like one who permits a knower in the unseen described with the opposite of knowledge along with his knowledge.

Thus it is also necessary: since it is impossible among us for a living being to be not knowing and not described with the opposite of knowledge, it must be impossible among us for a living being to be not speaking and not described with the opposite of speech. And it must be impossible in the unseen, and it must be that whoever permits one of the two matters in the unseen is like one who permits the other matter.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an argument by analogy from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (ghaib) regarding the impossibility of a living being being devoid of both a quality and its opposite. Al-Ash'ari begins by considering a living being that is devoid of knowledge and its opposites—such a being would be neither knowledgeable nor ignorant, which is impossible in the witnessed world. He then applies the same reasoning to speech: a living being cannot be neither speaking nor having the opposite of speech (muteness or silence). Since no evidence has established a different ruling for the unseen, if it were permissible for God to be without speech and its opposite, then it would also be permissible for Him to be without knowledge and its opposite, which is absurd. Al-Ash'ari further argues that it is impossible in the witnessed world for a knowing being to simultaneously have the opposite of knowledge (ignorance) along with knowledge; likewise for a speaker to have the opposite of speech along with speech. Therefore, anyone who claims that God can be described with the opposite of speech while being speaking is equivalent to claiming that God can be described with the opposite of knowledge while being knowing. The conclusion is forced: since impossibility in the witnessed extends to the unseen, the same impossibility applies to God. Thus, God must be both knowing and speaking, without being characterized by the opposites.

Key Terms
علم
كلام
حي
ضد
غائب
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God could be neutral, having neither knowledge nor its opposite, neither speech nor its opposite.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari demonstrates that a living being cannot be neutral; it must have either the quality or its opposite. Since God is Living, He must have both knowledge and speech.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what is the ruling in the witnessed world for a living being that is not described as knowing and not described as ignorant?
A) Possible, as some beings are neither.
B) Impossible, as every living being must be one or the other.
C) Only applies to humans, not to God.
D) Only applies to bodies, not to abstract entities.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari proves that God must have knowledge and speech because a living being cannot be devoid of either a quality or its opposite, and the same rule holds for the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is ‘Living’ and therefore necessarily 'Knowing,' does this shape your conviction that He sees every hidden thought and intention of yours—even the ones you avoid acknowledging? How does that change what you will allow yourself to think today?
?
You recite the Quran, God's speech, daily. But do you approach it as the utterance of the One for whom muteness is impossible—the Living God who speaks with real attribute? Or do you treat it as a mere book of stories? What one verse will you read today with the awe that it is His actual speech?
?
Al-Ash'ari's argument ties knowledge and speech together as necessary for a living being. If you struggled to believe that God speaks (the Quran is His uncreated speech), would you also begin to doubt that He knows you? Examine your heart: where do you hesitate to attribute real, perfect speech to God? What is the cost of that hesitation for your relationship with His revelation?

Al-Ash'ari's Rational Proof for God's Eternal Will
Al-Ash'ari presents a rational proof that God is eternally willing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's rational proof that God is eternally willing, based on the impossibility of defects in God.

Core Idea
If God were not eternally willing, He would have a defect—impossible.

Al-Ash'ari presents a concise rational argument for the eternity of God's will. He establishes a general principle about living beings: the absence of will necessarily implies a defect. Applying this to God, who is free from all defects, we conclude that God must be eternally willing. This lesson unpacks that logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the logical structure: general principle, analogy, and conclusion.
2
Identify the general principle
Find the statement about a living being that is not willing. What does Al-Ash'ari say must be the case?
3
Apply the principle to God
Why does the principle lead to the conclusion that God is eternally willing? Trace the logic from impossibility of defects to necessity of eternal will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل العقلي على قدم الإرادة

وهذا هو الدليل على أن الله تعالى لم يزل مريدا، وذلك أن الحي إذا كان غير مريد لشيء أصلا، وجب أن يكون موصوفا بضد من أضداد الإرادات من الآفات كالسهو والكراهة والإيناء والآفات الأخرى، كما وجب أن الحي إذا كان غير عالم بشيء أصلا كان موصوفا بضد من أضداد العلوم من الآفات كالجهل والسهو والغفلة أو الموت أو ما أشبه ذلك. فلما استحال أن يكون الباري تعالى لم يزل موصوفا بضد الإرادة، لأن هذا يوجب أن لا يريد شيئا على وجه من الوجوه.
The rational proof for the eternity of the will.

And this is the proof that God, exalted, has not ceased to be willing. That is because if a living being is not willing of anything at all, it must be characterized by one of the opposites of wills, from defects such as forgetfulness, aversion, weakness, and other defects. Just as it must be that if a living being is not knowing of anything at all, it is characterized by one of the opposites of knowledge, from defects such as ignorance, forgetfulness, heedlessness, or death, or what resembles those defects. So since it is impossible that the Creator, exalted, has not ceased to be characterized by the opposite of will—because this would entail that He does not will anything in any way.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) demonstrating that God (al-Bari) is eternally willing (lam yazal muridan). Al-Ash'ari argues from a general principle: if a living being (al-hayy) is not willing of anything at all, then it must be characterized by one of the opposites of will, which are defects such as forgetfulness (sahw), aversion (karaha), weakness (ina'), and similar defects. He draws an analogy to the attribute of knowledge: if a living being is not knowing of anything at all, it must be characterized by the opposite of knowledge, which are defects like ignorance (jahl), forgetfulness, heedlessness (ghafla), or even death. Since it is impossible for God to be characterized by defects—as this would entail that He does not will anything at all—it follows that God must be eternally willing. The proof relies on the principle that the absence of a positive attribute necessarily entails the presence of its opposite, and since the opposite is impossible for God, the positive attribute must be eternal.

Key Terms
إرادة
مريد
ضد
آفات
سهو
كراهة
إيناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could exist without will or be neutral with respect to will.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari proves that absence of will entails a defect, and since God cannot have defects, He must be eternally willing.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ash'ari argue would be necessary if a living being is not willing of anything at all?
A) It must be characterized by the opposite of will, such as forgetfulness.
B) It must be characterized by the opposite of knowledge, such as ignorance.
C) It could be neutral without any attribute.
D) It would cease to be living.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's proof establishes that the absence of will in a living being necessarily implies a defect, and since God is flawless, His will must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God was not paying attention to your prayers, as if He were forgetful? This lesson teaches that forgetfulness is a defect impossible for God. How does that change the way you will think during your next moment of doubt?
?
If God's will is eternal and perfect, then nothing happens without His will. How does this truth confront your resistance to a recent difficulty in your life? Will you surrender that resistance right now?
?
What does it reveal about your heart if you secretly assume God's will can change like human will? This lesson says God's will is eternal—unchanging. What in your life needs to align with that unchanging will, even if it hurts?

The Rational Proof for God's Eternal Will
Imam Ashari presents a rational proof that God is eternally willing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ashari uses a rational reductio argument to prove that God's will is eternal.

Core Idea
If God were not eternally willing, He would be defective—which is impossible.

This lesson explores one of the earliest rational proofs in Ash'ari kalam. Imam Ashari argues that the absence of will in a living being necessarily implies a defect. Applying this to God, He must be eternally willing, otherwise He would be described with defects like heedlessness or aversion, which is impossible for the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to absorb its rhythm. Then read the English translation to grasp the logical flow.
Pay attention to the terms for defects (آفات) like السهو, الكراهة, الإيناء.
2
Follow the reductio logic
Identify the analogy: a living being without will must have a defect. Then apply to God: if God were not willing, He would have a defect, which is impossible. Therefore God must be willing eternally.
The argument uses the impossibility of the opposite (ضد) being eternal.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Contrast this rational proof with scriptural proofs. Understand that this establishes will as an eternal attribute of God.
This is a foundational argument in divine attribute theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل العقلي وهذا هو الدليل على أن الله - تعالى - لم يزل مريدا ، وذلك أن الحيَّ إذا كان غير مريد لشيءٍ أصلا ، وجَبَ أنْ يكونَ موصوفًا بضدٍ من أضدادِ الإرادات ؛ من الآفاتِ كالسهو، والكراهة، والإيناء، والآفات [الأُخرى]، كما وجَبَ أن الحيَّ إذا كان غير عالم بشيء أصلًا [ كانَ] موصوفًا بضد من أضداد العلوم من الآفات كالجهل والسهو والغفلة أو الموت، أو ما أشبه ذلك الآفات. فلما استحال أن يكون الباري -تعالى- لم يزل موصوفًا بضد الإرادة؛ لأن هذا يوجب أن لا يريد شيئًا على وجه من الوجوه . وذلك أن ضد الإرادة إذا كان الباري -تعالى- لم يزل موصوفا به يوجب قِدَمَه، ومحال قِدَمَه، ومحال عدم القديم، كما [هو] محال حدوث القديم، فإذا استحال عدمه وجب أن لا يريد الباري شيئًا ويقصد فعله على وجه من الوجوه، وذلك فاسد وإذا فسد هذا صح وثبت أنَّ الباري -تعالى- لم يزل مريدا
The rational proof: and this is the proof that God - exalted - has eternally been willing. That is because a living being, if it is not willing anything at all, must be described with an opposite from among the opposites of wills: from defects like heedlessness, aversion, and languor, and other defects. Just as a living being, if it is not knowing anything at all, must be described with an opposite from among the opposites of knowledge from defects like ignorance, heedlessness, forgetfulness, or death, or similar defects. So when it is impossible that the Creator - exalted - has eternally been described with an opposite of will (because this would entail that He does not will anything in any way), and because if the opposite of will were to be eternally described with it, it would necessitate its eternity, and the eternity of that opposite is impossible, and the non-existence of the eternal is impossible, just as the origination of the eternal is impossible. So when its non-existence is impossible, it would necessitate that the Creator does not will anything and does not intend to do anything in any way, and that is false. And when this is false, it becomes correct and established that the Creator - exalted - has eternally been willing.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the eternity of God's will (إرادة). The author proceeds by a reductio argument. First, he states the analogy: any living being that is not willing anything at all must be described with one of the opposites of will, such as heedlessness, aversion, or languor. Similarly, a living being that is not knowing anything must be described with an opposite of knowledge, like ignorance or forgetfulness. This is a necessary logical consequence. Applying this to God: if God were not eternally willing, He would have to be described with an opposite of will. But for God, being described with any such opposite is impossible because it would imply defect and imperfection. Moreover, if the opposite of will were eternal, it would be a co-eternal entity, which is impossible because only God is eternal. If that opposite were to cease to exist, that would imply change in God, which is also impossible. Therefore, the only possibility is that God is eternally willing. The argument concludes that it is false to say God does not will, so it is established that He is eternal in His will.

Key Terms
إرادة
ضد
آفات
سهو
كراهة
إيناء
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will is created or temporal, like human will.
Correction: This proof shows that God's will is eternal, because its absence would imply a defect, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why must God be eternally willing?
A) Because will is an essential attribute that cannot be absent.
B) Because if He were not willing, He would have to be described with a defect, which is impossible.
C) Because the Qur'an says He is willing.
D) Because all living beings have will.

Key Takeaway
God's will is eternal because its absence would imply an impossible defect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is eternal and perfect, and He cannot not will, then every moment of your life is willed by Him with complete intentionality. Have you ever truly believed that even your smallest trial is not random but explicitly willed by the Eternal? Would you live differently today if you fully internalized that His will for you has never changed—it has always been exactly this present moment, with all its difficulties?
?
The proof shows that lacking will is a defect. Yet when you face hardship, do you act as if God's will is weak, absent, or mistaken? In your heart, what defect are you attributing to Him when you complain about His decree? Confess honestly: is there an area where you resent His will, and how does this proof expose that resentment as a denial of His perfection?
?
God's will is not something He acquires; it is essential to Him. That means He has always willed what is best for you, before you existed. Does your trust in Him reflect that eternal, unchanging will, or do you treat His will as if it were temporary, conditional, or reactive to your actions? What one decision today can you make that shows you believe in His eternal will more than your own desires?

Objection and Answer: The Opposite of Action vs. Speech and Will
Imam Ashari refutes an objection about the opposite of divine action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction that inaction is not a real opposite attribute, unlike silence and aversion.

Core Idea
Silence is a real opposite of speech; inaction is not a real opposite of action.

Imam Ashari presents an objection from an imaginary questioner who tries to extend the logic of divine attributes to action. The objector argues that if God were not eternally acting, He would have to be described with the opposite of action, i.e., being a desister. Ashari's answer reveals a crucial distinction: the opposites of speech and will are real attributes distinct from them, while the absence of action does not constitute a positive attribute. This passage clarifies the boundaries of analogical reasoning in theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's analogy
Read the objection carefully: the questioner argues that just as lack of speech and will imply opposite attributes, lack of action should imply the opposite attribute of being a desister.
Notice the objector is trying to force an analogy.
2
Understand the distinction in the answer
Ashari responds by showing that speech and will have real opposites (silence, dislike) that are not the same kind, whereas action's opposite (inaction) is not a real attribute but a mere absence.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Apply the principle to other attributes
Reflect on why this distinction matters: not every negation creates a positive attribute. This protects God from being described with negative attributes that are not truly attributable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : لَمَّا قلتم : إذا كان من لم يزل غير متكلم ولا مريد وجب أن يكون موصوفًا بضد الإرداة والكلام؛ إذا كان ممن لا يستحيل عليه الكلام والإرادة فما أنكرتم مِنْ أن مَنْ لم يزل غير فاعل وجب أن يكون موصوفًا بضد الفعل، وأن يكون تاركًا فيما لم يزل؟ قيل له : لا يجب ما قُلتَه ، وذلك أن للكلام ضدا ليس بكلام وللإرادة ضدا ليس بإرادة ، فوجبَ لَوْ كان البارِي - تعالى - حيا غير متكلم ولا مُريد ؛ أن يكون موصوفًا بضد الكلام والإرادة.
If a questioner says: 'Since you have said: if one who has eternally not been speaking and not willing must be described with an opposite of will and speech, given that he is one for whom speech and will are not impossible, then why do you deny that one who has eternally not been acting must be described with an opposite of action, and that he is a desister forever?' It is said to him: 'What you have said is not necessary. That is because speech has an opposite that is not speech, and will has an opposite that is not will. So it is necessary that if the Creator - exalted - were a living being not speaking and not willing, He would be described with an opposite of speech and will.'
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector draws an analogy: just as the author argued that if God were not eternally speaking and willing, He must have an opposite attribute (like silence and aversion), similarly if God were not eternally acting (فاعل), He must be described with the opposite of action, i.e., being a desister (تارك). So why do you not accept that? The answer distinguishes between the opposites of speech and will on one hand, and the opposite of action on the other. For speech and will, their opposites are not of the same kind (e.g., silence is not speech, dislike is not will). Therefore, the absence of speech or will necessarily entails the presence of their distinct opposites. But for action, its opposite (ترك) is not a distinct attribute separate from action in the same way; rather, it is the absence of action. The argument implies that the opposite of action is not a real attribute that can be described of God. Thus the objection fails because the analogy is flawed.

Key Terms
فاعل
تارك
ضد الفعل
كلام
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the absence of any attribute or action requires a positive opposite attribute.
Correction: Ashari shows that only certain attributes have genuine opposites; inaction is not a positive attribute but a negation.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Ashari respond to the objection about the opposite of action?
A) He admits the analogy and says God is indeed described as a desister.
B) He argues that action does not have a true opposite in the same way speech and will do.
C) He says the objection is irrelevant because God is always acting.
D) He says the opposite of action is also not an action, so the analogy holds.

Key Takeaway
The absence of divine action does not imply a positive opposite attribute because inaction is not a real attribute like silence or aversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You've just learned that inaction (tark) is not a real opposite attribute for God. But what about your own life? When you choose not to do good, do you treat that as a mere absence or as a positive choice with real consequences? How does this lesson challenge you to view your omissions as actions that carry moral weight?
?
Imam Ashari distinguishes between attributes that have real opposites (like speech and will) and those that don't (like action). How does this affect your understanding of God's perfection? Does it make you reconsider any assumptions you had about what God 'must' do?
?
If the absence of action in God is not a flaw—since it is not a positive opposite—does this change how you think about divine mercy? When God delays or withholds something, is that a mere absence or a wise act? What comfort or urgency does this give you in your dua?

The Proof of God's Eternal Will
Al-Ash'ari presents a proof of God's eternal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be eternally willing based on the impossibility of the opposite being eternal.

Core Idea
God must be eternally willing because the opposite would be impossible.

Al-Ash'ari begins his discussion of divine attributes by establishing that God is eternally willing. He uses a simple but profound argument: if the opposite of will were eternal in God, that opposite would have to be impossible, which leads to absurdity. Therefore, God must have always willed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the logical structure: a conditional statement followed by a conclusion.
The argument is concise but dense.
2
Identify the conditional reasoning
The argument says: if the opposite of will were eternal, it would be impossible; therefore God cannot lack will. Trace each step.
'Opposite of will' means not willing.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does the impossibility of the opposite force us to say God is eternally willing?
Because if not-willing were possible, it would imply deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في القرآن والإرادة
وذلك أن ضد الإرادة إذا كان الباري -تعالى- لم يزل موصوفا به يوجب قِدَمَه، ومحال قِدَمَه، ومحال عدم القديم، كما هو محال حدوث القديم، فإذا استحال عدمه وجب أن لا يريد الباري شيئًا ويقصد فعله على وجه من الوجوه، وذلك فاسد وإذا فسد هذا صح وثبت أنَّ الباري -تعالى- لم يزل مريدا
فإن قال قائل : لَمَّا قلتم : إذا كان من لم يزل غير متكلم ولا مريد وجب أن يكون موصوفًا بضد الإرداة والكلام؛ إذا كان ممن لا يستحيل عليه الكلام والإرادة فما أنكرتم مِنْ أن مَنْ لم يزل غير فاعل وجب أن يكون موصوفًا بضد الفعل، وأن يكون تاركًا فيما لم يَزل؟ قيل له : لا يجب ما قُلتَه ، وذلك أن للكلام ضدا ليس بكلام وللإرادة ضدا ليس بإرادة ، فوجبَ لَوْ كان البارِي -تعالى- حيا غير متكلم ولا مُريد أن يكون موصوفًا بضد الكلام والإرادة.
وليس للفعل ضد ليس بفعل، فيجب بنفي الفعل عن الفاعل، وجودُ ضِدّه ؛ لأنَّ الموجودَ إذا لم يكُنْ فِعلا كان قديما ، والقديمُ لا يضادُّ المُحدَثاتِ.
فلما لم يكن للفعل ضد ليس بفعل، لم يجِبْ بنفي الفعل عنِ اللَّهِ -تعالى- فى أَزَلِهِ إثباتُ ضِدّ .
ولما كانَ للكلامِ ضِدُّ ليس بكلامٍ، وَجَبَ بنفي الكلام عن اللَّهِ -تعالى- في أزَلِه إثبات ذلك الضّدّ لا محالة.
Chapter on Speech and the Will. And that is because if the opposite of will was something that the Exalted Creator never ceased to be described with, it would necessitate its eternity, and its eternity is impossible, and the non-existence of the eternal is impossible, just as the temporal origination of the eternal is impossible. So when its non-existence is impossible, it follows that the Exalted Creator wills nothing and intends nothing in any way, and that is corrupt. And when this is corrupt, it is correct and established that the Exalted Creator never ceased to be willing. Then if someone says: since you said: if one who never ceased to be non-speaking and non-willing must be described with the opposite of will and speech, since he is one for whom speech and will are not impossible, then why do you deny that one who never ceased to be non-acting must be described with the opposite of action, and that he is a deserter in what never ceased? It is said to him: What you have said is not necessary. That is because speech has an opposite that is not speech, and will has an opposite that is not will. So if the Exalted Creator were alive, non-speaking and non-willing, He would necessarily be described with the opposite of speech and will. But action does not have an opposite that is not an action. Therefore, by negating action from the agent, the existence of its opposite is necessary; because if an existent is not an action, it is eternal, and the eternal does not oppose temporal things. So since action does not have an opposite that is not an action, it is not necessary, by negating action from God Almighty in His eternity, to affirm an opposite. And since speech has an opposite that is not speech, it is necessary, by negating speech from God Almighty in His eternity, to affirm that opposite without exception.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a proof and an objection-and-answer. Al-Ash'ari opens with the chapter heading 'Chapter on Speech and the Will.' He then argues that God must be eternally willing, because the opposite of will—if it were eternal in God—would be impossible (since it would imply a deficiency). Therefore, God cannot lack will; He must be willing eternally. He anticipates an objection: if lack of speech and will imply their opposites, then lack of action should also imply an opposite (like abandonment or incapacity). Al-Ash'ari answers by distinguishing speech and will from action. Speech and will have real opposites: non-speech and non-will. But action does not have a corresponding opposite that is not itself an action. Whatever exists that is not an action is either eternal (like God's essence) or a contingent thing; it is not a proper opposite of action. Hence, while it is necessary to affirm that God is eternally speaking and willing to avoid impossible opposites, it is not necessary to affirm an opposite for His non-action.

Key Terms
الإرادة
ضد
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Ash'ari argue that God must be eternally willing?
A) Because the opposite of will, if eternal, would be impossible.
B) Because God's will is created.
C) Because God must will everything.
D) Because will is an action.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal will is proven by the impossibility of its opposite being eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is eternal and unchanging, then every moment of your life is under His perpetual intention. Are you at peace with that, or do you secretly wish for areas where your will is autonomous?
?
The proof shows that without an eternal will, God would be forced to will nothing. How does this truth confront any tendency you have to doubt God's constant involvement in your life?
?
If God never ceases to will, then your repentance, growth, and trials are all part of His eternal plan. Does this comfort you, or does it challenge you to surrender control more fully today?

Why God's Lack of Action Differs from Lack of Speech
Al-Ash'ari answers an objection about action vs. speech/will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying speech or will from God implies an opposite, while denying action does not.

Core Idea
Speech and will have opposites; action does not.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari anticipates an important objection: if lack of speech and will imply their opposites, why doesn't lack of action imply an opposite? His answer hinges on the nature of action—it has no true opposite that is not itself an action. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the analogy being proposed: just as non-speech implies opposite, non-action should imply opposite.
The objector tries to make a parallel argument.
2
Read Al-Ash'ari's answer
Notice the key distinction: speech and will have real opposites (non-speech, non-will) that are not speech or will. Action has no such opposite.
Whatever is not an action is either eternal or temporal, not a proper opposite.
3
Reflect on the implications
Why does this matter for theology? It preserves the uniqueness of divine action while affirming God's speech and will.

Refuting the Claim that Non-Action Implies Incapacity
Al-Ash'ari refutes the objection that non-action implies incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari argues that God's non-action from eternity does not imply incapacity, and how the coexistence of action and incapacity in created beings supports this.

Core Idea
Incapacity and action can coexist, so non-action does not mean incapacity.

This passage presents an objection to the Ash'ari position that God is eternally non-acting. The objector claims that non-action must entail either incapacity or deliberate abandonment. Al-Ash'ari refutes this by showing that incapacity ('ajz) is not a true opposite of action. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objector presents a conditional argument: if God was non-acting from eternity, then He must be either incapable ('ājiz) or a deserter (tārik).
The objector assumes non-action always has a cause.
2
Identify al-Ash'ari's key claim
Al-Ash'ari immediately denies that incapacity is an opposite (ḍidd) of action. This is the crux of his reply.
3
Understand the proof
He supports his claim by noting that God can create any action (motion, rest, etc.) in us even while incapacity is present. Therefore, they are not mutually exclusive.
This empirical observation refutes the supposed opposition.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since incapacity and action can coexist, God's non-action does not imply incapacity. The objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: فيجب إذا كان القديم غير فاعل، فيما لَمْ يَزَلْ، أَنْ يكون عاجزًا أو تاركًا .
قيلَ لَهُ : فَلَيْسَ العجز مضادًا للفِعْلِ ، وذلكَ أنَّه لَيْسَ مِنْ جنس من أجناس الفعل من حركة وسكون وغيرهما من سائر الأعراض، إلا وقد يجوز أن يخلقه الله [فينا] مع العجز، فعلمنا بذلك أن العجز لا يضاد الفعل ؛ لأنَّ الأجسام، والأعراض من أفعال اللَّه
Then if he says: then it is necessary, if the Eternal was non-acting in what never ceased, that He be incapable or a deserter. It is said to him: Incapacity is not an opposite of action. That is because there is not a single genus of the genera of action, such as motion and rest and other accidents, except that it is possible that God create it in us along with incapacity. Thus we know by this that incapacity does not oppose action, because bodies and accidents are from the actions of God.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a further objection-and-answer. The objector argues: if God is non-acting from eternity, then He must be either incapable or a deserter (tārik). Al-Ash'ari replies that incapacity ('ajz) is not a true opposite of action. His proof: there is no type of action—whether motion, rest, or any other accident—that God cannot create in us simultaneously with incapacity. For example, we may perform actions while being incapable in some sense? The exact meaning is that incapacity does not preclude the occurrence of action; therefore, they are not mutually exclusive opposites. Since incapacity and action can coexist, the absence of action in God does not imply incapacity. Thus, the objection fails.

العجز
الفعل
ضد
الأعراض
الأجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that lack of action must be due to incapacity.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that incapacity and action can coexist, so non-action does not imply incapacity.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ash'ari argue that incapacity is not an opposite of action?
A) Because incapacity cannot coexist with action.
B) Because God can create actions in us even when we are incapable.
C) Because action has no opposite at all.
D) Because incapacity is not an attribute of God.

Key Takeaway
Incapacity is not a true opposite of action because actions can coexist with incapacity, so God's non-action does not imply incapacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for something and do not see an immediate answer, do you ever think Allah is unable to grant it? How does al-Ash'ari's argument that non-action does not imply incapacity challenge that doubt? Will you now trust that He can act even when He chooses not to?
?
Consider a sin you keep committing. Do you sometimes feel that Allah's forgiveness is beyond your reach because of your repeated failure? How does the logic of this lesson—that inability and action are not opposites—help you understand that Allah's mercy and justice are not limited by your perception of His ‘inaction’? What will you change about your repentance today?

Negating Action vs. Speech in Pre-Eternity
Al-Ash'ari argues that negating action does not necessitate an opposite, but negating speech does.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negating action from God in pre-eternity does not imply an opposite, while negating speech necessarily implies silence.

Core Idea
Negating action does not force an opposite; negating speech forces silence.

Al-Ash'ari draws a careful distinction between action and speech. While action has no true opposite that is itself not action, speech has a clear opposite—silence. This difference determines whether denying an attribute in pre-eternity leads to affirming its opposite. In this passage, al-Ash'ari explains the logical reasoning behind this asymmetry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the parallel structure: first action, then speech.
The Arabic is compact; read slowly.
2
Understand the key claim about action
Al-Ash'ari states that action has no opposite that is not action. That means any opposite of action would itself be an action, so denying action does not imply a non-action opposite.
Think about what an opposite of action could be: e.g., stillness? But stillness is not an action, yet it is not an opposite in the same way.
3
Compare with speech
For speech, the opposite (silence) is not speech. Therefore, denying speech forces us to affirm silence as its opposite.
This asymmetry is crucial for understanding God's attributes in pre-eternity.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters for theology: God's speech is eternal but silent in pre-eternity? This leads to further discussions about the nature of divine speech.
This is a classic Ash'ari argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس للفعل ضد ليس بفعل، فيجب بنفي الفعل عن الفاعل، وجودُ ضِدّه ؛ لأنَّ الموجودَ إذا لم يكُنْ فِعلا كان قديما ، والقديمُ لا يضادُّ المُحدَثاتِ. فلمَّا لم يكن للفعل ضد ليس بفعل، لم يجِبْ بنفي الفعل عنِ اللَّهِ - تعالى - فى أَزَلِهِ إثباتُ ضِدّ . ولما كانَ للكلامِ ضِدُّ ليس بكلامٍ، وَجَبَ بنفي الكلام عن اللَّهِ -تعالى- في أزَلِه إثبات ذلك الضّدّ لا محالة.
And there is no opposite for action that is not action, so by negating action from the agent, the existence of its opposite is necessary; because if the existent is not action, it is eternal, and the eternal does not oppose originated things. So since action has no opposite that is not action, negating action from Allah - exalted - in His pre-eternity does not necessitate affirming an opposite. And since speech has an opposite that is not speech, negating speech from Allah - exalted - in His pre-eternity necessitates affirming that opposite necessarily.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about the implications of negating action and speech from God in pre-eternity. The author first states that action has no opposite that is not itself an action. Therefore, if we deny that God performs an action in pre-eternity, we are not compelled to affirm an opposite (like inactivity) because any opposite of action would have to be an action itself or something else. However, for speech, there is an opposite that is not speech, namely silence. Therefore, when we say God was not speaking in pre-eternity, we must affirm that He had the opposite of speech, i.e., He was silent. This is a crucial distinction between the attributes of action and speech.

Key Terms
ضد
فعل
كلام
قديم
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating any attribute from God implies its opposite automatically.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows this only applies when the attribute has a genuine opposite outside its own genus; action does not have such an opposite, while speech does.

Checkpoint
Why does negating action from God in pre-eternity not necessitate affirming an opposite?
A) Because action has no opposite that is not itself an action.
B) Because God cannot have an opposite.
C) Because the opposite of action is silence, which is eternal.
D) Because negating action is the same as affirming inaction.

Key Takeaway
In pre-eternity, negating speech from God necessarily implies affirming its opposite (silence), while negating action does not imply any opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience God's silence in your life—when prayers seem unanswered—do you assume He is absent or that His silence itself carries meaning? How does al-Ash'ari's point that speech has a necessary opposite (silence) challenge you to trust that even His silence is purposeful? What will you do this week when you feel God is silent?
?
Al-Ash'ari shows that negating action does not imply an opposite. Think of a situation where you failed to act justly—did you consider your inaction as neutral or as a sin? How does this lesson help you distinguish between culpable inaction and innocent non-action? What specific step can you take today to turn a passive attitude into an active deed for Allah's sake?
?
This lesson forces us to accept that some negations have positive implications (speech implies silence) while others do not. Where in your own beliefs have you assumed that because God does not do something, He must be limited or lacking? How can you apply the distinction between action and speech to trust that God's 'not acting' in a certain situation is not a deficiency but a different kind of divine wisdom? Write down one area where you will stop inferring a deficiency from God's apparent inaction and instead trust His eternal wisdom.

Incapacity Does Not Oppose Action
Al-Ash'ari refutes the claim that God's lack of action implies incapacity or abandonment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari argues that God's lack of action in pre-eternity does not imply incapacity or abandonment.

Core Idea
Incapacity opposes power, not action; abandonment is itself an action.

We often assume that if something doesn't act, it's either unable or unwilling. But al-Ash'ari challenges this when applied to God. In this passage, he refutes a hypothetical objector by distinguishing incapacity from action and showing that abandonment is a type of action. Let's see how he builds his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall flow of the objection and al-Ash'ari's response.
Notice that the objector's claim is based on a necessity: if God never acted, He must be either incapable or deliberately refraining.
2
Identify the key distinction
Al-Ash'ari argues that incapacity is not the opposite of action. Look for where he explains what incapacity actually opposes.
He says: 'إنما يضاد القدرة' — it only opposes power.
3
Understand the arguments for coexistence
Al-Ash'ari gives examples: actions can occur in us even when we are incapable, and if incapacity opposed movement, it would prevent God from creating movement. See how he uses these examples.
The argument from bodies and accidents shows that actions are from God, not from us.
4
Consider the implication for God
Since incapacity does not oppose action, and abandonment is itself an action, God's pre-eternal non-action does not force us to say He is incapable or abandoning. Reflect on why this preserves God's perfection.
The conclusion: negating action does not necessitate incapacity or abandonment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : فيجب إذا كان القديم غير فاعل، فيما لم يَزَلْ، أَنْ يكون عاجزًا أو تاركًا . قيلَ لَهُ : فَلَيْسَ العجز مضادًا للفِعْلِ ، وذلكَ أنَّه لَيْسَ مِنْ جنس من أجناس الفعل من حركة وسكون وغيرهما من سائر الأعراض، إلا وقد يجوز أن يخلقه الله [فينا] مع العجز، فعلمنا بذلك أن العجز لا يضاد الفعل ; لأنَّ الأجسام، والأعراض من أفعالِ اللَّهِ -تعالى-، فعَلِمْنا أن العجز لا يضادُّ الفعل؛ لأن عجْزِي لَوْ ضادَّ للحركةِ، لَكَانَ يُضادُّ وقوع الحركةِ مِنْ رَبِّي في جِسْمِي . ألا ترَى أَنَّهُ إِذا اسْتَحالَ أنْ أَفْعَلَ في عِلما ، معَ المَوْتِ ، اسْتَحالَ أَنْ يفعَلَ ربِّي في معَ الموتِ، عِلما؟ فلما لم يكن العجز مضادا للفعل، وإنما يضادُّ القدرة، وكانَ التركُ للشيء فِعلَ ضِدّه ، فكان الباري -تعالى- لم يزل غير فاعل لشيء ، على وجه من الوجوه ، لم يُوجِبْ نفي الفعل عنْهُ في أزَلِهِ عَجْزًا ولا تركا.
If he says: It is necessary, if the Eternal is not acting in what was always, that He be incapable or abandoning. It is said to him: Incapacity is not opposite to action, because there is no genus of action from movement and stillness and other accidents, except that it is possible that Allah creates it in us with incapacity, so we know by that that incapacity does not oppose action; because bodies and accidents are from Allah's acts - exalted - so we know that incapacity does not oppose action; because if my incapacity were opposite to movement, it would oppose the occurrence of movement from my Lord in my body. Do you not see that if it is impossible for me to do in knowledge [؟], with death, it is impossible for my Lord to do in me with death, knowledge? So since incapacity is not opposite to action, but rather opposes power, and abandonment of a thing is doing its opposite, and the Creator - exalted - never ceased not acting at all, in any way, negating action from Him in His pre-eternity does not necessitate incapacity nor abandonment.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector claims that if God was never acting in pre-eternity, then He must have been either incapable or deliberately refraining. Al-Ash'ari replies by distinguishing incapacity from action. He argues that incapacity is not the opposite of action; rather, it opposes power (qudra). He supports this by noting that in our own experience, we can be incapable yet actions still occur in us (e.g., God creates movement in us while we are incapable). Thus, incapacity can coexist with action. Furthermore, abandonment (tark) is itself an action (doing the opposite), so it does not negate action per se. Therefore, negating action from God does not force us to ascribe incapacity or abandonment to Him. This preserves God's transcendence and power.

Key Terms
عجز
ترك
قدرة
ضد
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Not acting implies inability or unwillingness.
Correction: Incapacity is opposite to power, not action; abandonment is itself an action, so negating action does not necessitate either.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what is incapacity (عجز) opposite to?
A) Action
B) Power
C) Silence
D) Will

Key Takeaway
Incapacity opposes power, not action; abandonment is an action; thus God's pre-eternal non-action does not imply incapacity or abandonment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often excuse your inaction as 'I can't' or 'I won't.' Al-Ash'ari forces you to see that incapacity is not the opposite of action—it only opposes power. When have you blamed your inability to change when the real issue is your unwillingness to surrender your will to Allah? Will you today act in obedience even when you feel powerless, trusting that Allah can create action in you?
?
If abandonment is itself an action—doing the opposite—then what does your inactivity say about your priorities? You claim you haven't had time for prayer, Quran, or charity. But is that true? Or are you actively doing their opposite? What will you stop doing today so that you can start doing what matters?

God's Non-Action: No Incapacity, No Abandonment
Al-Ash'ari argues that God's eternal non-action does not imply incapacity or abandonment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's not acting in eternity does not indicate any imperfection such as incapacity or abandonment.

Core Idea
Incapacity opposes ability, not action; thus God's non-action is not incapacity.

This passage from Al-Ash'ari's Al-Luma' addresses a potential misunderstanding: if God did not act for eternity, does that mean He was incapable or neglectful? Al-Ash'ari clarifies the logical relationship between incapacity and action, showing that non-action does not necessitate an opposite attribute like silence does to speech. By understanding this distinction, we uphold God's perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical flow: Al-Ash'ari starts with a general statement about incapacity and action, then applies it to God.
The passage uses an example involving the human body to illustrate a point.
2
Understand the core distinction
Identify the claim that incapacity (العجز) does not oppose action (الفعل) but only ability (القدرة). Think about why this distinction matters: if incapacity opposed action, it would prevent any action, even from God, which is false.
Al-Ash'ari uses a reductio argument: if incapacity opposed movement, it would prevent God from moving your body, which is not the case.
3
Apply to God's non-action
Since incapacity only opposes ability, God's not acting in eternity does not imply He was incapable. Rather, non-action is simply the absence of action, not a positive opposite like silence is to speech.
Contrast with speech and will: their absence necessarily implies their opposites (silence and aversion), but action's absence does not imply incapacity or abandonment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلمنا أن العجز لا يضاد الفعل؛ لأن عجزي لو ضاد الحركة، لكان يضاد وقوع الحركة من ربي في جسدي. ألا ترى أنه إذا استحال أن أفعل في علم مع الموت، استحال فعلي أن يفعل ربي في مع الموت، علما؟ فلما لم يكن العجز مضادا للفعل، وإنما يضاد القدرة، وكان الترك للشيء فعل ضده، فكان الباري -تعالى- لم يزل غير فاعل لشيء، على وجه من الوجوه، لم يوجب نفي الفعل عنه في أزله عجزا ولا تركا. وأيضا فإن الحي إذا كان غير متكلم ولا مريد، وجب أن يكون موصوفا بضد الإرادة والكلام، وليس إذا كان غير فاعل لشيء وجب إثبات ضد هو عجز، أو ترك؛ إذ كان عجز الإنسان لا يضاد فعله، فلم يجب بنفي الفعل عن الله -تعالى- في أزله، إثبات ترك أو عجز، كما وجب في نفي الكلام والإرادة عنه في أزله إثبات أضدادهما.
So we know that incapacity does not oppose action; because if my incapacity opposed movement, it would oppose the occurrence of movement from my Lord in my body. Do you not see that when it is impossible that I do in knowledge with death, it becomes impossible for my Lord to do in knowledge with death? So since incapacity is not opposite to action, but rather opposes ability, and refraining from something is acting its opposite, and the Exalted Creator was eternally not doing anything at all, it does not necessitate negating action from Him in His eternity to be incapacity or abandonment. Also, if the living being is not speaking and not willing, it must be described as the opposite of will and speech. But if He is not doing something, it does not necessitate establishing an opposite which is incapacity or abandonment, since man's incapacity does not oppose his action. So negating action from God in His eternity does not necessitate establishing abandonment or incapacity, as was necessitated in negating speech and will from Him in His eternity to establish their opposites.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is an argument demonstrating that God's not acting in eternity does not imply any attribute of imperfection like incapacity or abandonment. The author begins by establishing that incapacity (العجز) does not oppose action itself, but rather opposes ability (القدرة). He uses an example: if his own incapacity opposed movement, it would prevent movement caused by God in his body, which is not the case. Then he clarifies that refraining from an action is itself the act of doing its opposite. Thus, God's eternal non-action is not due to incapacity or abandonment. He then contrasts this with speech and will: if a living being is not speaking and not willing, it necessarily has the opposite attributes (silence and aversion), because these are mutually exclusive. But non-action does not force such an opposite, because the opposite of action is not a single necessary attribute but could be simply not acting. Therefore, God's eternal non-action is not a defect.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's not acting in eternity means He was incapable or abandoned His creation.
Correction: The passage explains that non-action is not the opposite of action in the same way; incapacity only opposes ability, and God's non-action is simply His eternal state without creation, not a defect.

Checkpoint

A) Action (الفعل)
B) Ability (القدرة)
C) Movement (الحركة)

Key Takeaway
God's eternal non-action is not due to incapacity or abandonment; it is simply His free choice, as incapacity only opposes ability, not action.

God's Non-Motion Does Not Necessitate Stillness
Al-Ash'ari refutes the claim that God's non-motion implies stillness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's lack of movement does not imply that He is still, and how al-Ash'ari uses the distinction between possible and impossible attributes to defend divine transcendence.

Core Idea
For God, non-motion does not mean stillness because motion is impossible for Him.

In this short objection-and-answer passage, al-Ash'ari confronts a common theological confusion: if God does not move, must He be described as still? The answer hinges on a key distinction between what is possible for a being and what is impossible. By examining the logic of opposites, al-Ash'ari clarifies that God's transcendence means He is beyond such categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection starts with 'If he says' and the answer starts with 'It is said'.
2
Identify the objection and answer
Identify the objector's claim: that negating motion from God necessitates stillness. Then find al-Ash'ari's response, which introduces a condition.
The answer uses the phrase 'If He were one for whom movement is possible'.
3
Understand the key condition
Ask yourself: why does the answer work? The key is the possibility of the attribute. For God, movement is impossible, so the opposite (stillness) is not implied.
Contrast this with speech and will, where the opposites are implied because those attributes are possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: فيجب بنفي الحركة عن الله -تعالى- في أزله أن يكون ساكنا. قيل: لو كان ممن يجوز أن يتحرك، لوجب لعمري - بنفي الحركة عنه أن يكون ساكنا، كما يجب بنفي الكلام والإرادة عنه في أزله إثبات أضدادهما؛ إذ كان ممن لا يستحيل ذلك عليه.
If he says: Then it is necessary, by negating movement from God in His eternity, that He be still. It is said: If He were one for whom movement is possible, then by my life, by negating movement from Him, He would have to be still, just as it is necessary by negating speech and will from Him in His eternity to establish their opposites, since He is one for whom that is not impossible.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector argues that if God had no movement in eternity, then He must be described as still (ساكن). The author's answer distinguishes the case: stillness would be a necessary opposite only if movement were possible for God. But for God, movement is not a possible attribute; it is impossible. Therefore, non-motion does not imply stillness. The author draws a contrast with speech and will: for those, the opposite attributes (silence and aversion) are indeed necessitated because they are possible and normal for a living being. For God, however, stillness is not a necessary implication of non-motion because God is not subject to motion and rest in the same way.

الضد
الجواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think that if God does not move, He must be still.
Correction: For God, motion is impossible, so non-motion is simply a negation, not a positive attribute of stillness.

Checkpoint

B) If movement is possible for the subject
C) Never
D) Only for humans

Key Takeaway
God's non-motion does not imply stillness because stillness is the opposite of motion only when motion is possible; for God, motion is impossible, so non-motion is simply a negation.

God's Non-Favor Is Not Miserliness
Al-Ash'ari refutes the claim that God's non-bestowal implies miserliness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's non-bestowal of favor does not imply miserliness, because favor is defined as a voluntary act that the bestower may or may not do.

Core Idea
Favor is voluntary; not giving it does not equal stinginess.

The objector argues that if God never showed favor in eternity, He must be miserly. Al-Ash'ari counters with a crisp definition: favor is something the bestower may choose to give or withhold. This lesson unpacks how a simple definitional move refutes the accusation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the structure: 'If he says... it is said to him.'
The objector's claim is presented as a logical necessity.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the words 'تفضل' (favor) and 'بخيل' (miserly). Understand how the author uses 'تفضل' to redefine the issue.
3
Understand the refutation
The author does not argue about God's actions directly. Instead, he redefines 'favor' as optional. If it's optional, withholding it is not miserliness.
Miserliness only applies when something obligatory is withheld.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: فيجب بنفي التفضل عنه، فيما لم يزل، أن يكون بخيلا. قيل له: التفضل هو: ما للمتفضل أن يتفضل به، وله أن لا يتفضل.
If he says: Then it is necessary, by negating favor from Him, in what never ceases, that He be miserly. It is said to him: Favor is what the bestower may bestow, and He may not bestow.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This is another objection-and-answer. The objector claims that if God never showed favor (التفضل) in eternity, then He would have to be described as miserly (بخيل). The author's answer is a swift definitional refutation: favor is something that the bestower has the option to bestow or not. It is not obligatory. Therefore, not bestowing does not imply miserliness; it simply indicates that God chose not to bestow. Miserliness would be if He withheld what He is obliged to give, but no such obligation exists. Hence, non-favor in eternity does not attribute a negative quality to God.

Key Terms
التفضل
البخل
المتفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God does not give, He is stingy.
Correction: Favor is optional; withholding it is not stinginess because there is no obligation to give.

Checkpoint

A) What God must do
B) What the bestower may bestow or not
C) Any good act
D) What is deserved

Key Takeaway
God's non-favor does not imply miserliness because favor is inherently optional, not obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Negating Action Does Not Imply Incapacity or Omission
Ash'ari argues that negating action does not necessitate its opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negating action from God in pre-eternity does not necessitate establishing an opposite attribute, while negating speech and will does.

Core Idea
Not all attribute negations force an opposite; it depends on the attribute's nature.

Imam Ash'ari tackles a subtle theological issue: when we say God was not doing something in pre-eternity, does that force us to say He was incapable or idle? He argues no, and provides a key distinction. This passage from Al-Luma' sharpens our understanding of divine attributes and logical opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice the contrast Ash'ari draws between action and speech/will.
The passage uses comparative logic: 'as it was necessary... not so for action.'
2
Identify the two cases
Find where Ash'ari discusses negating speech/will and where he discusses negating action. What is the different outcome?
For speech/will, opposites are established; for action, they are not.
3
Understand the reason
Ash'ari provides a reason: human incapacity does not contradict action. Grasp why this analogy breaks the necessity of opposite.
Think about how 'incapacity' relates to action in general, not a specific action.
4
Connect to divine attributes
Reflect on why this distinction matters for Islamic theology. It protects God from having any imperfection.
This argument defends God's perfection in pre-eternity without attributing deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون موصوفا بضد الإرادة والكلام، وليس إذا كان غير فاعل لشيء وجبَ إثباتُ ضد هو عجز ، أو ترك ؛ إذْ كانَ عجزُ الإنسانِ لا يضادُّ فعله ، فلم يجب بنفي الفعل عن الله - تعالى - في أَزَلِه ، إثبات ترك أو عجز، كما وجب في نفي الكلام والإرادة عنه في أزله إثباتُ أضدادهما
He is described with the opposite of will and speech. And it is not the case that if He is not a doer of something, then the establishment of an opposite that is incapacity or abandonment is necessary. For the incapacity of a human does not contradict his action. So it is not necessary, by negating action from God—exalted is He—in His pre-eternity, to establish abandonment or incapacity, just as it was necessary, by negating speech and will from Him in His pre-eternity, to establish their opposites.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is an argument explaining the logic of negating attributes in God. It begins by stating that God is described with the opposite of will and speech. Then it argues that if God is not a doer of something, it does not necessarily imply an opposite such as incapacity (عجز) or abandonment (ترك). The reason given is that human incapacity does not contradict his action—meaning that a human can be incapable of some action while still performing others, so incapacity is not the direct contradictory of action in general. Therefore, negating action from God in pre-eternity does not force us to attribute incapacity or omission to Him. This is contrasted with the case of speech and will: when we negate speech and will from God in pre-eternity, we must affirm their opposites (silence and lack of will) because those attributes have opposites that are mutually exclusive with them. The passage thus distinguishes between types of attributes: some have opposites that are necessary upon negation, while others do not.

إرادة
كلام
فعل
عجز
ترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating any attribute from God automatically necessitates affirming its opposite.
Correction: Ash'ari shows this only holds for attributes whose opposites are logically necessary (like will vs. lack of will), but for action, the opposite is not forced because incapacity does not contradict action in general.

Checkpoint

A) Because God is beyond opposites.
B) Because human incapacity does not contradict his action.
C) Because action is not an attribute of God.
D) Because the opposite of action is only omission.

Key Takeaway
Negating action from God does not force an opposite, unlike speech and will, because the nature of action's opposite is not logically necessary.

?

?

What change in your worship does this insight demand?

Negating Motion Does Not Necessarily Imply Rest
Ash'ari refutes the claim that negating motion implies rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical condition under which negating motion implies rest, and why it does not apply to Allah.

Core Idea
Negation implies its opposite only where the attribute is possible.

Imam Ash'ari addresses an objection: if we say that Allah is not moving in pre-eternity, does that mean He was motionless? The answer reveals a crucial logical principle: negation implies its opposite only when the attribute is possible. This lesson examines the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's claim: negating motion from God in pre-eternity must entail that He is motionless. Notice the assumption that motion and rest are logical opposites.
The objector assumes that every absence of motion is rest.
2
Identify the condition in the answer
The answer says: 'If He were among those for whom motion is possible, then by my life...' This shows the key condition. Underline 'ممن يجوز أن يتحرك'.
The condition is possibility (jawaz) of motion.
3
Compare with the example of speech and will
Ash'ari draws a parallel: negating speech and will from God in pre-eternity does imply their opposites because speech and will are possible attributes for God. Understand why the parallel supports the argument.
The example shows when negation does imply opposite.
4
Apply the principle
Conclude: since motion is impossible for God (as an accident), negation does not establish rest. Rest is not a meaningful attribute for God.
This protects divine transcendence from anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : فيجب بنفي الحركة عن الله -تعالى- في أزلِهِ أن يكون ساكنا . قيل : لو كان ممن يجوز أن يتحرك، لَوجبَ لَعَمْرِي- بنفي الحركة عنه أن يكون ساكنا ، كما يجب بنفي الكلام والإرادة عنه في أزلِه إثباتُ أضدادهما
If he says: Then it must follow, by negating motion from God—exalted is He—in His pre-eternity, that He be motionless. It is said: If He were from those for whom motion is possible, then by my life, it would be necessary by negating motion from Him that He be motionless, just as it is necessary by negating speech and will from Him in His pre-eternity to establish their opposites.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning motion. The objector argues that if we negate motion from God in pre-eternity, we must conclude that He is motionless (ساكن). The answer refutes this by pointing out that this logic would only hold if God were the kind of being capable of motion. Since God is not subject to motion (motion being an accident), the negation of motion does not imply the opposite. The answer uses a comparative: just as negating speech and will in pre-eternity requires affirming their opposites, this is because those attributes are possible for God and their opposites are necessary when negated. But for motion, since God cannot move, the negation of motion does not have the same implication. The phrase 'لَعَمْرِي' (by my life) emphasizes the certainty of the conditional.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
ضد
جواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating motion from something always implies that it is in a state of rest.
Correction: It implies rest only if the thing is capable of motion. For God, who cannot move, negating motion does not establish rest.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari, negating motion from God would require affirming that He is motionless only if:
A) Motion is an attribute of perfection.
B) God is capable of moving.
C) Motion and rest are logical opposites.
D) God is subject to change.

Key Takeaway
Negating an attribute from Allah only establishes its opposite if that attribute is possible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson forces you to distinguish between what is possible for Allah and what is impossible. When you think about Allah's attributes, have you ever unconsciously assumed that Allah is limited by the same logical opposites as created beings? What specific belief about Allah might you need to correct because of this lesson?
?
The refutation hinges on a precise condition: negation implies its opposite only if the attribute is possible. In your own understanding of Allah, are there areas where you have accepted a conclusion about Allah without checking whether that attribute is even possible for Him? How will you change the way you reason about Allah's attributes from now on?
?
Ash'ari uses an oath 'by my life' to emphasize the logical necessity under the condition. Do you approach theological statements with the same seriousness and precision? What step can you take this week to ensure your beliefs about Allah are built on sound reasoning, not on assumptions?

Negating Graciousness Does Not Imply Stinginess
Ash'ari refutes the claim that negating graciousness implies stinginess.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negating graciousness from God does not imply stinginess, based on the distinction between voluntary favors and obligatory dues.

Core Idea
Stinginess is about withholding what is due; God owes nothing, so He cannot be stingy.

In this passage, Ash'ari addresses a common objection: if God was not always gracious, does that make Him stingy? He answers by clarifying what graciousness and stinginess truly mean. Graciousness is voluntary; stinginess is withholding what is owed. Since God is not obligated to anyone, failing to bestow favors is not stinginess. Let's examine his precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the objector's claim followed by Ash'ari's response.
2
Identify the definitions
Ash'ari defines 'graciousness' (تفضل) as an act one may do or not, and 'stinginess' (بخل) as withholding what is due. Underline these definitions.
The key is that graciousness is not obligatory.
3
Apply the logic
Ask yourself: If God is not obligated to do anything, can His not bestowing favors be called stinginess? The answer is no, because stinginess requires an obligation.
This shows why the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : فيجب بنفي التفضل عنه ، فيما لم يزل، أن يكون بخيلا . قيل لَهُ : التفضُلُ هو : ما لِلْمُتَفَضّل أن يتفضلَ بهِ، وَلَهُ أَن لَا يتفضل والبُخْلُ إِنَّما يجبُ بِمَنْع مُستَحَقِّ اسْتُحِقَ عَلَى مَنْ بَخِلَ، والبارِي -تعالى- لا يجب عليه فعلُ شيءٍ
If he says: Then it must follow, by negating graciousness from Him in what has not ceased, that He be stingy. It is said to him: Graciousness is that which the gracious one may bestow and may not bestow. And stinginess is only necessarily by withholding what is due that is owed to the one who is stingy, and the Creator—exalted is He—is not obligated to do anything.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents another objection and answer regarding graciousness (تفضل). The objector claims that if God was not gracious in pre-eternity, He must be stingy (بخيل). The answer defines تفضل as something that a gracious person may choose to do or not do—it is voluntary and not obligatory. Then it defines stinginess (بخل) as withholding what is due to someone. Since God is not obligated to do anything for anyone, He cannot be described as stingy for not bestowing favors. Therefore, negating graciousness does not imply stinginess. The answer clarifies the conceptual distinction between generosity and stinginess, showing that they are only opposites in contexts of obligation.

Key Terms

بخل
استحقاق
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Lack of graciousness from God means He is stingy.
Correction: Stinginess only applies when one withholds what is due; God is not obligated to bestow favors.

Checkpoint

A) Because God is always gracious.
B) Because stinginess only applies when one withholds what is due, and God is not obligated.
C) Because graciousness and stinginess are not opposites.
D) Because God's graciousness is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Negating graciousness from God does not imply stinginess because stinginess only occurs when withholding what is due, and God is not obligated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Denying God's Justice Does Not Entail Injustice or Incapacity
Ash'ari refutes the objection that denying God's justice implies injustice or incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari argues that denying divine justice does not logically require affirming injustice or incapacity in God.

Core Idea
Absence of an attribute does not automatically imply its opposite.

Ash'ari addresses a common objection: if we say God is not 'just' in the human sense, does that mean He is unjust or incapable? In this passage, he carefully unpacks the logic of negation and opposites, showing that denying justice does not force us to affirm its opposite. This lesson will guide you through his reasoning, using analogies from human behavior and the concept of moral action being defined by divine command.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Begin by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the objector presents a dilemma, and Ash'ari responds step by step.
The objector assumes that if God is not just, He must be unjust or incapable. Pay attention to how Ash'ari challenges this assumption.
2
Analyze the argument about incapacity
Ash'ari first refutes the idea that denying justice implies incapacity. He argues that God can perform acts of justice even alongside incapacity, so the link is not necessary.
Think about what 'incapacity' means here. Ash'ari is separating the ability to do an act from the act itself.
3
Analyze the argument about injustice
Next, he refutes the implication of injustice. He uses a human example: a person can lack justice (e.g., not performing just acts) without being unjust. This shows that the absence of justice does not force the presence of injustice.
This step is crucial for understanding the logic of opposites in kalam.
4
Examine the genus argument
Finally, Ash'ari argues that justice and injustice can belong to the same genus (e.g., being in a place). The same action can be just or unjust depending on divine command. This further undermines the claim that denying justice necessitates injustice.
This argument shows that moral qualities are not intrinsic but depend on God's command.
5
Reflect on the methodology
Consider how this passage illustrates a key principle of Ash'ari theology: negation of one attribute does not entail affirmation of its opposite. This is a common tool in defending divine transcendence (tanzih).
This lesson connects to broader kalam discussions about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإِنْ قالَ : فَيَجِبُ بنَفْيِ العَدْلِ عنه، في أَزَلِهِ، أَنْ يكونَ جائرًا أو عاجزا. قيل له : ليسَ يجِبُ بنفي العدل ضد هو عجز أو جَوْرٌ؛ لأنه ليس مِن جنس من أجناس العدلِ إلَّا وَيَجوزُ أن يفعله الله - تعالى - فينا معَ العجز ، فلم يجِبْ بنفي العدلِ إثباتُ ضد هو عَجْز . ولم يجب أيضًا إثباتُ الجورِ ؛ لأنَّ الإنسان قد لا يكون عادلا إذا لم يكن منه عدل كسبه ولا فَعَلَهُ، ولا يكون جائِرًا ، فَلَيْسَ مَنْ نفينا عنه العدل أثبتنا له ضدّا هو جور أو عجز ؛ إذ كنا قد ننفي ذلك عنا ولا نثبت ضدا هو عجز أو جور، والحيُّ هو عجز أو جور، والحيُّ منَّا ومِنْ غيرِنا ، إذا لم يكن عالما، كانَ موصوفًا بِضَدِّ العِلْمِ. وأيضًا : فقَد لا يكونُ الإنسانُ عادلا ولا يكونُ جائرًا ، بجورٍ مِن جنس العدْلِ، فَلَيْسَ يجبُ بنفْيِ العدلِ ضِدُّ هُوَ جَوْرٌ ، كما وجبَ في الكلام والإرادة؛ لأن الإنسان قد يكون عادلاً بالكون في المكان، إذا أمره الله - تعالى - أن يكون فيه ، ويكون في وقتٍ آخر جائرًا بالكونِ فيه، إذا نهاه الله تعالى عن الكون فيه، فيكون العدلُ من جنس الجوْرِ ؛ لأنَّ الكونَ في المكان من جنسِ الكَونِ فيه .
If he says: Then by denying justice of Him, in His eternity, He must be unjust or incapable. It is said to him: It is not necessary that by denying justice there follows an opposite that is incapacity or injustice; because there is no genus of justice except that it is possible for God - exalted - to do it in us along with incapacity. So it is not necessary by denying justice to affirm an opposite that is incapacity. Nor is it necessary to affirm injustice; because a person may not be just if there is no justice from him that he acquired and did, and he is not unjust. So we do not, when we deny justice of him, affirm for him an opposite that is injustice or incapacity; since we may deny that of ourselves and not affirm an opposite that is incapacity or injustice. And the living being is incapacity or injustice? And the living being from us and from others, if he is not knowing, is described by the opposite of knowledge. Moreover, a person may not be just and not be unjust, with injustice of the genus of justice. So it is not necessary by denying justice an opposite that is injustice, as was necessary in speech and will; because a person may be just by being in a place, if God - exalted - commands him to be in it, and at another time he is unjust by being in it, if God - exalted - forbids him from being in it. Thus justice is of the same genus as injustice; because being in a place is of the genus of being in it.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that if God is not described as just in eternity, then He must be unjust or incapable. The author refutes this by showing that denying justice does not necessitate affirming an opposite (incapacity or injustice). He argues that for any genus of justice, God can perform it even alongside incapacity. Moreover, a human can lack justice without being unjust. The same action can be just or unjust depending on divine command. Thus, absence of justice does not entail presence of its opposite.
