The Generality of God's Will Over All Created Things
Al-Ash'ari argues for the generality of God's will and answers an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's three arguments that God's will encompasses all created things and how he defends this against an objection.

Core Idea
God's essential will must be universal, otherwise imperfection would follow.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari addresses a foundational question: does God's will extend to everything that happens, or are some things outside His will? The questioner challenges the claim, and al-Ash'ari responds with a series of proofs drawn from the nature of essential attributes, scriptural testimony, and rational necessity. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the full translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the dialectical structure: question, three proofs, then a further objection and answer.
2
Identify the three proofs
Find the three distinct arguments al-Ash'ari gives: (1) parallel with knowledge as an essential attribute, (2) scriptural proof from the Quran, (3) dominion argument.
Each proof builds on the idea that an essential attribute cannot be limited.
3
Understand the objection and reply
Read the objection: why does the failure of a will to occur imply weakness? Then study the analogy with knowledge: if a known event does not occur, ignorance is implied, so similarly if a willed event does not occur, weakness is implied.
This strengthens the initial claim by showing the logical consequence of denying it.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this doctrine matters: it affirms God's absolute sovereignty and perfection, and refutes any notion that something could happen against His will.
This is a cornerstone of Ash'ari theology regarding divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ
فإن قال قائل : لم قلتُم : إِنَّ اللَّهَ - تعالى - مُرِيدٌ لكلِّ شيءٍ يَجوزُ أن يُراد؟ قيل له : قُلنا ذلكَ لأن الإرادة إذا كانت من صفات الذات، بالدلالة التي ذكرناها، وجبَ أنْ تكونَ عامةً، في كلِّ ما يَجوزُ أن يُرادَ على حقيقتِه، كما إذا كان العلم من صفات الذات وجبَ عُمومه بكلِّ ما يجوزُ أنْ يُعلَمَ علَى حَقيقَتِهِ .
وأيضًا فقد دَلَّتِ الدلالة على أنَّ الله - تعالى - خالقُ كلِّ شيءٍ حادث، ولا يجوز أنْ يَخْلُقَ مَا لَا يُرِيدُهُ، وقد قال الله -تعالى- : ﴿فَعَّالٌ لِمَا يُرِيدُ﴾ [هود : ١٠٧].
وأيضًا فإنه لا يجوز أن يكونَ في سُلطانِ اللَّهِ -تعالى- ما لا يُريدُه ؛ لأنَّه لو كانَ في سُلطانِ اللَّهِ - تعالى - ما لا يُريدُه ، لوجب أَحَدُ أَمرين : إمَّا إثباتُ سَهْوِ وغَفْلَةٍ، أو إثباتُ ضَعْفٍ، وعَجْزِ، ووَهَنِ، وتقصيرٍ عَنْ بُلوغ ما يُريدُهُ. فلمَّا لم يَجُز ذلك على اللَّهِ -تعالى- استحال أن يكون في سلطانه ما لا يريده .
فإِنْ قالَ : ولِمَ زعمتُمْ ذلكَ؟ قيل له : زَعَمْنَا ذلكَ ؛ لأنَّ المرادَ إِذا وَقَعَ ، لم يلحَقِّ مَنْ وقَعَ مُرَادُه ضعف، ولا تقصير عن بلوغ ما يُريدُه ، بوَقْعِ المرادِ، فَوَاجِبٌ أن يلحَقَهُ الضعفُ والتَّقصيرُ عن بلوغ ما يريده إذا لمْ يقَعْ مُراده . ألا ترى أنَّ مَنْ وقَعَ ما يعلَمُهُ لمْ يلْحَقْه جهل، ولا ضِدُّ مِن أضدادِ العلم بذلك الشيء، إذا وقع وهو يعلمه فدل ذلك على أنه إذا لَمْ يقع ، وهو لا يعلمه، وجبَ جهلُهُ أو وَصْفُه بضدّ مِنْ أَضدادِ العِلْمِ . وكذلك إذا كانَ ما يريده لم يجِبْ سَهْرُهُ ولا ضعفه ولا وَهَنُه ، ولا تقصيرُهُ عن بلوغ ما يريده. وإذا كانَ ما لا يُرِيدُهُ وجبَ سَهوهُ، أو ضعفُهُ وَوَهَنُه ، وتقصيرُهُ عن بلوغ ما يُرِيدُ .
Chapter on the discourse about the will and that it encompasses all created things. If a questioner says: Why do you say that God - exalted - is willing for everything that it is possible to be willed? It is said to him: We said that because the will, when it is an attribute of the self, by the proof we mentioned, it necessarily follows that it be general, in everything that it is possible to be willed in its true sense, just as when knowledge is an attribute of the self, its generality necessarily follows with respect to everything that it is possible to be known in its true sense. Also, the proof has indicated that God - exalted - is the creator of every originated thing, and it is not permissible that He create what He does not will, and God - exalted - said: 'Doer of whatever He wills' [Hud 11:107]. Also, it is not permissible that there be in the dominion of God - exalted - what He does not will, because if there were in the dominion of God - exalted - what He does not will, one of two things would be necessary: either the affirmation of forgetfulness and heedlessness, or the affirmation of weakness, incapacity, feebleness, and failure to attain what He wills. Since that is not permissible for God - exalted -, it is impossible that there be in His dominion what He does not will. If he says: And why do you claim that? It is said to him: We claimed that because when the object of will occurs, the one whose will occurs is not affected by weakness nor failure to attain what He wills, by the occurrence of the object of will. So it is necessary that weakness and failure to attain what He wills affect Him when His object of will does not occur. Do you not see that when what one knows occurs, he is not affected by ignorance nor by any opposite of knowledge concerning that thing, since it occurred while he knows it. That indicates that if it does not occur while he does not know it, his ignorance or his description with an opposite of knowledge becomes necessary. Likewise, when what He wills does not occur, His alertness, weakness, feebleness, and failure to attain what He wills are not necessary. And when what He does not will occurs, His forgetfulness, or weakness and feebleness, and failure to attain what He wills become necessary.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a theological proof establishing that God's will (al-irada) is general, encompassing all created things. The author, al-Ash'ari, begins with a hypothetical objection: 'Why do you say that God is willing for everything that can be willed?' The answer proceeds with three rational arguments.

First argument (a): The will is an attribute of God's essence (sifatu al-dhat). Just as divine knowledge must be universal, applying to everything knowable as it truly is, so too the divine will must be universal, applying to everything willable as it truly is. The parallel with knowledge is crucial: both are essential attributes, and essential attributes cannot be limited.

Second argument (b): Scriptural proof is combined with rational necessity. God is the creator of all originated things. It is impossible that He creates what He does not will. The Quranic verse 'Doer of whatever He wills' (Hud 11:107) confirms that His will is effective in all His acts.

Third argument (c): Suppose something existed in God's dominion that He does not will. This would force one of two unacceptable conclusions: either God is subject to forgetfulness and heedlessness (which is unworthy of Him), or He is weak, incapable, and unable to achieve His will. Both are impossible for God. Therefore, nothing occurs in His dominion that He does not will.

The opponent then raises an objection: 'Why do you claim that? That is, why does the existence of an unwilled thing imply weakness?' The answer draws an analogy with knowledge. If a person's intended object occurs, that person suffers no weakness or failure. Conversely, if the intended object does not occur, then weakness or failure must be present. Similarly, if someone knows something and it occurs, no ignorance is attributed; but if it does not occur while he is ignorant, ignorance is attributed. By extension, if God's will is not realized, that would imply imperfection, which is impossible. Therefore, whatever occurs must be willed by God; otherwise, imperfection would follow. The analogy with knowledge reinforces the logic: knowledge and will are both essential attributes; their non-realization would entail their opposites (ignorance for knowledge, weakness for will).

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irada)
صفات الذات (sifatu al-dhat)
المراد (al-murad)
السَّهْو (al-sahw)
الغَفْلَة (al-ghafla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will is like human will, subject to change or frustration.
Correction: Divine will is an essential attribute that must be universally effective; otherwise it would imply imperfection in God.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari's argument, what would be the consequence if something occurred in God's dominion that He did not will?
A) It would prove God's power is limited.
B) It would imply either God's forgetfulness or weakness.
C) It would be a mere possibility with no implication.
D) It would show God's will is not essential.

Key Takeaway
God's will is an essential attribute that encompasses all created things; nothing occurs in His dominion without His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship or a tragedy, do you truly believe that it is willed by God for a wise purpose, or does your heart waver and accuse God of oversight or weakness like the objection in this passage? Commit to trusting His will even when you don't understand.
?
In your daily decisions, do you acknowledge that everything you experience is under God's will, or do you live as if some events slip from His control? How does that affect your reliance on Him?
?
If God's will is essential and universal, can you ever claim that something 'just happened' without His will? Contemplate a recent event you attributed to chance and repent for making God less than what He is.

Nothing Unwilled Occurs in Allah's Dominion
Al-Ash'ari proves by contradiction that nothing unwilled can exist in Allah's dominion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's proof by contradiction that everything occurring in Allah's dominion is willed by Him.

Core Idea
If anything unwilled existed, Allah would be weak or forgetful — impossible.

Al-Ash'ari presents a crisp logical argument to show that nothing can happen in Allah's creation without His will. The argument works by showing what would follow if the opposite were true—and those consequences are impossible for Allah. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument as a whole
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the flow without pausing.
Notice how Al-Ash'ari uses 'if... then' logic.
2
Identify the two absurd consequences
The proof says if something unwilled existed, two impossible attributes would follow. Find both and understand why they are impossible.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since those attributes cannot apply to Allah, the original possibility (unwilled events) is impossible. Grasp how this shows Allah's will covers all events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج- وأيضًا فإنه لا يجوز أن يكونَ في سُلطانِ اللَّهِ تعالى ما لا يُريدُه؛ لأنَّه لو كانَ في سُلطانِ اللَّهِ تعالى ما لا يُريدُه، لوجب أَحَدُ أَمرين: إمَّا إثباتُ سَهْوِ وغَفْلَةٍ، أو إثباتُ ضَعْفٍ، وعَجْزِ، ووَهَنِ، وتقصيرٍ عَنْ بُلوغ ما يُريدُهُ. فلمَّا لم يَجُز ذلك على اللَّهِ تعالى استحال أن يكون في سلطانه ما لا يريده.
And also, it is not permissible that there be in the dominion of Allah, exalted, what He does not will, because if there were in the dominion of Allah, exalted, what He does not will, then one of two matters would be necessary: either the affirmation of forgetfulness and negligence, or the affirmation of weakness, incapacity, feebleness, and failure to attain what He wills. So since that is not permissible for Allah, exalted, it is impossible for there to be in His dominion what He does not will.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction regarding divine will. Al-Ash'ari argues that if something occurred in Allah's dominion that He did not will, it would force one to attribute to Him either (1) forgetfulness/negligence or (2) weakness/inability/failure. Both are absolutely impossible for Allah. Therefore, nothing unwilled can exist in His dominion. The argument is concise: the premise leads to an absurdity, so the premise is false.

الغفلة

Correction: This passage shows that any event occurring in Allah's dominion (the entire universe) must be willed by Him; otherwise, Allah would be imperfect. Therefore, His will encompasses everything that happens.

Key Takeaway
Everything that happens in Allah's dominion happens by His will; otherwise, imperfection would be attributed to Him.

Objection and Answer on Will and Weakness
Al-Ash'ari responds to an objection by drawing an analogy from knowledge to will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses an analogy between knowledge and will to argue that non-occurrence of the willed entails weakness in the agent.

Core Idea
If a willed thing does not occur, weakness necessarily follows.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari addresses an objection by drawing a parallel between knowledge and will. He argues that just as ignorance follows when a known thing does not occur, weakness follows when a willed thing does not occur. This logical analogy reinforces the necessary connection between divine attributes and their effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Begin by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a response.
The objector asks for the rationale behind the earlier claim.
2
Identify the analogy from knowledge
Al-Ash'ari draws an analogy: just as occurrence of a known thing implies no ignorance, non-occurrence implies ignorance. Identify the parallel for will.
3
Understand the logical necessity
The argument shows that the relationship between will and its object is necessary, not arbitrary. Weakness follows from non-occurrence just as ignorance follows from non-knowledge.
4
Reflect on the implication for divine will
Consider how this analogy supports the claim that God's will is not weak or forgetful. This guards against misconceptions about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: ولِمَ زعمتُمْ ذلكَ؟
قيل له: زَعَمْنَا ذلكَ؛ لأنَّ المرادَ إِذا وَقَعَ، لم يلحَقِّ مَنْ وقَعَ مُرَادُه ضعف، ولا تقصير عن بلوغ ما يُريدُه، بوقوع المرادِ، فواجب أن يلحَقَهُ الضعفُ والتَّقصيرُ عن بلوغ ما يريده إذا لمْ يقَعْ مُراده. ألا ترى أنَّ مَنْ وقَعَ ما يعلَمُهُ لمْ يلْحَقْه جهل، ولا ضِدُّ مِن أضدادِ العلم بذلك الشيء، إذا وقع وهو يعلمه فدل ذلك على أنه إذا لَمْ يقع، وهو لا يعلمه، وجبَ جهلُهُ أو وَصْفُه بضدّ مِنْ أضدادِ العلمِ.
وكذلك إذا كانَ ما يريده لم يجِبْ سَهْوُهُ ولا ضعفه ولا وَهَنُه، ولا تقصيرُهُ عن بلوغ ما يريده. وإذا كانَ ما لا يُرِيدُهُ وجبَ سَهوُهُ، أو ضعفُهُ وَوَهَنُه، وتقصيرُهُ عن بلوغ ما يُرِيدُ .
If he says: And why do you claim that? It is said to him: We claim that because when the willed thing occurs, the one whose willed thing occurs is not affected by weakness nor failure to attain what he wills, due to the occurrence of the willed thing. So it is necessary that weakness and failure to attain what he wills affect him if his willed thing does not occur. Do you not see that the one for whom something he knows occurs is not affected by ignorance nor any opposite of knowledge concerning that thing, when it occurs while he knows it; so that indicates that if it does not occur while he does not know it, his ignorance or description by an opposite of knowledge becomes necessary. And likewise, if what he wills occurs, his forgetfulness, weakness, feebleness, and failure to attain what he wills do not become necessary. And if what he does not will occurs, his forgetfulness, or weakness and feebleness, and failure to attain what he wills become necessary.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector asks for the rationale behind the earlier claim. The answer provides a logical analogy: the occurrence of a willed thing does not cause weakness in the one who wills, just as the occurrence of a known thing does not cause ignorance in the knower. However, if the willed thing does not occur, weakness necessarily follows, just as if the known thing does not occur while the knower is ignorant, ignorance necessarily follows. The analogy shows that the relationship between will and its object mirrors that between knowledge and its object.

المراد

التقصير

الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine will is arbitrary, with no necessary connection to its object.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the will necessarily entails the occurrence of its object, otherwise weakness would follow. This is similar to the necessary connection between knowledge and its object.

B) Ignorance or its opposite becomes necessary

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari demonstrates that the necessary connection between will and its object mirrors that between knowledge and its object.

Extending the Argument to Actions of Others
Al-Ash'ari argues that the same implications hold for unwilled actions of others as for Allah's own actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the same logical implications that arise from un-willed acts of Allah also arise from un-willed acts of others within His dominion, thus reinforcing the universality of divine will.

Core Idea
Allah's will encompasses all events, not just His own actions.

Al-Ash'ari extends his earlier argument about unwilled actions of Allah to unwilled actions of others. He shows that the same undesirable attributes (like forgetfulness or weakness) would have to be attributed to Allah if He did not will an action occurring in His dominion, regardless of whether the actor is Allah Himself or a created being. This passage solidifies the doctrine that Allah's will (irādah) covers all created events without exception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that al-Ash'ari asserts that the same necessity applies to unwilled actions of others as to Allah's own actions.
2
Examine the two analogies
The passage provides two analogies: one about knowledge (if ignorance follows from an unknown action of Allah, it also follows from an unknown action of another) and one about divine information (if discrediting Allah follows from a false report about His action, it also follows from a false report about another's action).
These analogies parallel the earlier argument but apply to others' actions.
3
Understand the conclusion
Al-Ash'ari concludes that there is no difference between Allah's own actions and those of others in terms of the necessity of will. This reinforces that Allah's will encompasses all events.
The conclusion is stated explicitly: 'There is no difference in that between what we have agreed is His action and what occurs from other than Him.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا إذا كانَ في كون ما لا يريدُهُ مِن أفعاله - التي اجتمعنا على أنَّها أفعاله - وجوبُ السهو، والغفلة، أو الضعفِ، والوهَنِ، والتقصيرِ عَنْ بُلوغ ما يريدُهُ، فكذلك يَلزَمُ في كونِ ما لا يريدُهُ مِنْ غَيرِهِ ما يلزَمُ في كون ما لا يريدُهُ ممَّا اتفقنا على أنَّه مِنْ أَفعالِهِ. ألا ترى أنَّه إِذا لَزِمَ، مَنْ زَعَمَ أَنَّهُ يَكونُ مِنْ أَفعالِ اللَّهِ ما لا يعلَمُه، أنْ يصفه بالجهل، أو بضد من أضدادِ العلمِ؛ لَزِمَ مثلُ ذلك مَنْ زعمَ أنه يكونُ مِنْ غَيْرِه ما لا يعلَمُه، وكذلك إِذا لَزِمَ مَنْ زعم أَنَّ اللَّهَ يُخْبِرُ أَنَّه يكون من فِعله ما لا يكُونُ - التكذيبُ لِلَّهِ، لَزِمَ مَنْ زعمَ أَنَّ اللَّهَ تعالى يُخبرُ أنه يكون من غيرِهِ ما لا يَكُونُ، التكذيب أيضًا، لا فرق في ذلك بين ما اتفقنا على أنه فعله، وبينَ ما يَكُونُ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ. وكذلك إذا لزم في كونِ ما لا يُريدُه الباري تعالى مِنْ فعِلهِ ضعف وتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده، أو سهو وغفلةٌ، لَزِمَ ذلك في كونِ ما لا يريده مِنْ غَيْرِهِ.
And also, if in the existence of what He does not will from His actions – which we have agreed are His actions – there is necessity of forgetfulness and negligence, or weakness, feebleness, and failure to attain what He wills, then likewise it becomes necessary in the existence of what He does not will from other than Him the same that becomes necessary in the existence of what He does not will from what we have agreed are His actions. Do you not see that if it becomes necessary for one who claims that there is in Allah's actions what He does not know to describe Him with ignorance or with an opposite of knowledge, the like of that becomes necessary for one who claims that there is from other than Him what He does not know. And likewise, if it becomes necessary for one who claims that Allah informs that there will be from His action what will not be – the discrediting of Allah – it becomes necessary for one who claims that Allah, exalted, informs that there will be from other than Him what will not be, discrediting as well. There is no difference in that between what we have agreed is His action and what occurs from other than Him. And likewise, if necessity of weakness and failure to attain what He wills, or forgetfulness and negligence, is established in the existence of what the Creator, exalted, does not will from His action, the same is established in the existence of what He does not will from other than Him.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage extends the previous argument to actions of others. Al-Ash'ari argues that the same consequences that follow from an unwilled action of Allah also follow from an unwilled action of another being (e.g., a human) that occurs in Allah's dominion. He uses two analogies: one from knowledge (if ignorance follows from an unknown action of Allah, it also follows from an unknown action of another), and another from divine information (if discrediting Allah follows from a false report about His own action, it also follows from a false report about another's action). The conclusion is that there is no difference in logical implication between Allah's own actions and those of others when it comes to divine will encompassing them. This reinforces the doctrine that Allah's will (irādah) encompasses all created events.

The Analogical Proof for Divine Will Encompassing All Acts
Ash'ari presents a proof by analogy that divine will encompasses all created acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari uses analogy to prove that God's will must encompass all created acts, and why denying this leads to attributing imperfection to God.

Core Idea
If God's own acts need His will, so do others' acts—or else imperfection follows.

Ash'ari now extends his argument about divine will from God's own acts to all created acts. He uses a powerful analogy: whatever implications follow from claiming that something occurs against God's will in His own acts must also follow for acts of others. This lesson unpacks that analogy and its two concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the chapter heading stating the topic.
The passage uses a dense analogical structure.
2
Identify the main claim and analogical structure
Ash'ari argues that what is true for God's own acts regarding will is also true for others' acts. This is a proof by analogy (tamthīl).
3
Examine the two analogies: knowledge and truthfulness
First analogy: If God were ignorant of His own act, He'd be ignorant; same for others' acts. Second: If God's information about His own act were false, He'd be a liar; same for others' acts.
Both analogies are reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the conclusion
There is no distinction between God's acts and others' acts in these implications. Therefore, God's will must encompass all acts.
5
Reflect on the significance
This establishes that denying God's will over any event leads to impossible attributes in God, preserving His perfection.
This argument safeguards divine omnipotence and will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ
وأيضًا إذا كانَ في كون ما لا يريدُهُ مِن أفعاله - التي اجتمعنا على أنَّها أفعاله - وجوبُ السهو، والغفلة، أو الضعفِ، والوهَنِ، والتقصيرِ عَنْ بُلوغ ما يريدُهُ ، فكذلك يَلزَمُ في كونِ ما لا يريدُهُ مِنْ غَيرِهِ ما يلزَمُ في كون ما لا يريدُهُ ممَّا اتفقنا على أنَّه مِنْ أَفْعالِهِ. ألا ترى أنَّه إِذا لَزِمَ مَنْ زَعَمَ أَنَّهُ يَكونُ مِنْ أَفعالِ اللَّهِ ما لا يعلَمُه، أنْ يصفه بالجهل ، أو بضد من أضدادِ العِلمِ؛ لَزِمَ مثلُ ذلك مَنْ زعمَ أنه يكونُ مِنْ غَيْرِه ما لا يعلَمُه ، وكذلك إِذا لَزِمَ مَنْ زعم أَنَّ اللَّهَ يُخْبِرُ أَنَّه يكون من فِعله ما لا يكُونُ - التكذيبُ لِلَّهِ ، لَزِمَ مَنْ زعمَ أَنَّ اللَّهَ - تعالى - يُخبرُ أنه يكون من غيرِهِ ما لا يَكُونُ، التكذيب [أيضًا ] ، لا فرق في ذلك بين ما اتفقنا على أنه فعله، وبينَ ما يَكُونُ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ. وكذلك إذا لزم في كونِ ما لا يُريدُه الباري -تعالى- مِنْ فعِلهِ ضعف وتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده، أو سهو وغفلةٌ، لَزِمَ ذلك في كونِ ما لا يريده مِنْ غَيْرِهِ.
Chapter: Discourse on Will and That It Encompasses All Created Things. And also, if there is, in the occurrence of what He does not will from His acts - which we agree are His acts - the necessity of forgetfulness, heedlessness, or weakness, frailty, and deficiency from attaining what He wills, then likewise it follows in the occurrence of what He does not will from others what follows in the occurrence of what He does not will from what we agreed are His acts. Do you not see that if it follows, for one who claims that there occurs from God's acts what He does not know, that he describes Him with ignorance or with a contrary of knowledge, then the like of that follows for one who claims that there occurs from other than Him what He does not know. And likewise, if it follows for one who claims that God informs that there will occur from His act what does not occur - the accusation of lying against God - then it follows for one who claims that God - exalted - informs that there will occur from other than Him what does not occur, the accusation of lying as well. There is no difference in that between what we agree is His act and what occurs from other than Him. And likewise, if it follows in the occurrence of what the Creator - exalted - does not will from His act, weakness and deficiency from attaining what He wills, or forgetfulness and heedlessness, then that follows in the occurrence of what He does not will from other than Him.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy (*tamthīl*) establishing that God's will encompasses all created acts. Ash'ari begins with the chapter heading stating the topic: will encompasses all created things. He then argues: if we agreed that God's own acts require His will—otherwise lacking will would imply in Him forgetfulness, weakness, etc.—then the same must hold for acts of others. He draws two parallels: (1) just as claiming God acts without knowledge leads to attributing ignorance to Him, so claiming others act without His knowledge does the same; (2) just as claiming God's information about His own act being false leads to charging God with lying, so claiming the same about others' acts does. He concludes there is no distinction between God's acts and others' acts in these implications. The argument is a *reductio* against anyone who separates God's will from His acts and others' acts.

Key Terms
إرادة
سهو
جهل
تكذيب
ضعف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ash'ari is equating human acts with God's acts in all respects.
Correction: The passage only argues that the implications of denying God's will are the same; it does not claim identity of essence or nature between divine and human acts.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari's analogy, what follows if one claims that God's own act occurs without His will?
A) It implies God is wise.
B) It implies forgetfulness or weakness in God.
C) It implies God is all-powerful.
D) It implies human free will.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari's analogy shows that denying God's will over any act entails attributing imperfection to Him, whether the act is His or another's.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every event in your life—including your own choices—occurs only because God willed it? When you face something you dislike, do you ever secretly resent that it could have been otherwise? If so, are you attributing to God an inability to will what He does not want?
?
Ash'ari argues that denying God's will over others' acts leads to accusing God of lying about His foreknowledge. How does your daily trust in God's promises and news reflect (or fail to reflect) your conviction that His will is accomplished in all things?
?
If you were to fully internalize that nothing happens without God's will, what area of your life would need the most radical change in attitude—your worries, your ambitions, or your relationships? Why is that area hardest to surrender to His will?

All Created Things Are God's Acts
Ash'ari argues from creation to necessary will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Ash'ari argues that nothing can occur in the universe against God's will.

Core Idea
Everything created is God's act, so nothing occurs without His will.

In this passage, Imam al-Ash'ari presents a foundational proof for the Ash'ari doctrine of qadar. He begins with a simple premise: all created things are created by God. From this, he derives a powerful conclusion about the scope of God's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the compact style.
2
Identify the premise
The argument starts with the premise that all created things are creations of God. Note the phrase 'دلَّتِ الدّلالةُ' indicating proof.
This premise is taken as established.
3
Follow the logical conclusion
Since God cannot do what He does not will, and all created things are His acts, it follows that nothing from others can happen against His will. Understand why this is a necessary conclusion.
Think about what would happen if the premise were false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا فقد دلَّتِ الدّلالةُ على أن كلَّ المُحدَثاتِ مخلوقات للَّهِ تعالى. فإذَا استحالَ أَنْ يفعل الباري -تعالى- ما لا يريدُهُ استحالَ أَنْ يقعَ مِن غيرِهِ ما لا يُرِيدُهُ إِذْ كانَ ذلك أجمعُ أفعالا لله -تعالى.
وأيضًا فلو كان في العَالَمِ ما لا يريده الله -تعالى-، لكانَ ما يَكْرَهُ كَوْنَهُ، ولو كَانَ ما يَكْرَهُ كَوْنَه، لَكَانَ مَا يَأْبَى كَونَه . وهذا يُوجِبُ أَنَّ المعاصِيَ كانَتْ ، شاءَ اللَّهُ أَم أَبَى ، وهذه صِفةُ الضعيف المقهور، وتعالَى ربُّنا عن ذلكَ علوا كبيرًا .
And also, the proof has indicated that all created things are creations of God - exalted. So if it is impossible for the Creator - exalted - to do what He does not will, it is impossible for anything to occur from other than Him that He does not will, since all of that is acts of God - exalted. And also, if there were in the world something that God - exalted - does not will, then there would be something He dislikes to be, and if there were something He dislikes to be, then there would be something He abhors to be. This necessitates that sins occurred whether God wills or abhors, and this is the attribute of the weak and overpowered, and our Lord is exalted above that, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a two-part proof establishing that nothing occurs contrary to God's will. First, Ash'ari states a premise: evidence proves all created things (*al-muḥdathāt*) are God's creations. Since it is impossible for God to do what He does not will, it follows that nothing from others can occur against His will, because all such occurrences are ultimately God's acts. Second, he considers the contrary: if something existed that God does not will, it would be something He dislikes to exist, and that would mean He abhors its existence. This would imply that sins happen regardless of God's wish, which is a sign of weakness and being overpowered—impossible for God. The argument reinforces that everything occurs by God's will, including acts of obedience and disobedience, but He may dislike the latter yet still will them for wisdom.

Key Terms
محدثات (created things)
مخلوقات (creations)
استحال (is impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that evil deeds are not willed by God.
Correction: This passage shows that all created things are God's acts, so even sinful acts are willed by God in the sense of existing, though He may dislike them.

Checkpoint
Why does Ash'ari conclude that nothing occurs from others against God's will?
A) Because others are independent agents.
B) Because all created things are God's acts.
C) Because God cannot will evil.
D) Because God does not know the future.

Key Takeaway
All created things are God's acts, so nothing in creation defies His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every event in your life—even the painful ones—is an act of God that He has willed, how does that reshape your reaction to a recent disappointment or loss? Will you now turn to Him in gratitude or complaint?
?
Do you truly believe that your own choices are under God's will, or do you secretly reserve some area of your life as 'yours'? What would it mean to surrender that area to Him today?
?
When you sin, do you think God merely permits it, or do you accept that He willed it for a wisdom beyond your understanding? How might this change your repentance and reliance on His mercy?

The Absurdity of Anything Against God's Will
Ash'ari uses reductio to argue against anything occurring against God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for anything to occur in the world without God's will, using a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Core Idea
If anything occurred against God's will, God would be weak and overpowered.

Imam al-Ash'ari strengthens his case with a second argument. He imagines the hypothetical scenario where something exists that God does not will. He shows that this leads to an absurd consequence: that God would be weak and overpowered, which is impossible for the Almighty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the second argument carefully. Notice the hypothetical 'if' statement at the beginning.
This argument is a conditional chain.
2
Trace the conditional chain
Identify the sequence: if something exists that God does not will → it is something He dislikes to be → it is something He abhors to be → this implies sins occur whether God wills or not.
Each step follows from the previous.
3
Understand the absurd consequence
The final consequence is that God would be described as 'weak and overpowered', which is impossible for the Lord. This proves the initial hypothesis is false.
Reductio ad absurdum: if a premise leads to impossibility, the premise is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا فقد دلَّتِ الدّلالةُ على أن كلَّ المُحدَثاتِ مخلوقات للَّهِ تعالى. فإذَا استحالَ أَنْ يفعل الباري -تعالى- ما لا يريدُهُ استحالَ أَنْ يقعَ مِن غيرِهِ ما لا يُرِيدُهُ إِذْ كانَ ذلك أجمعُ أفعالا لله -تعالى.
وأيضًا فلو كان في العَالَمِ ما لا يريده الله -تعالى-، لكانَ ما يَكْرَهُ كَوْنَهُ، ولو كَانَ ما يَكْرَهُ كَوْنَه، لَكَانَ مَا يَأْبَى كَونَه . وهذا يُوجِبُ أَنَّ المعاصِيَ كانَتْ ، شاءَ اللَّهُ أَم أَبَى ، وهذه صِفةُ الضعيف المقهور، وتعالَى ربُّنا عن ذلكَ علوا كبيرًا .
And also, the proof has indicated that all created things are creations of God - exalted. So if it is impossible for the Creator - exalted - to do what He does not will, it is impossible for anything to occur from other than Him that He does not will, since all of that is acts of God - exalted. And also, if there were in the world something that God - exalted - does not will, then there would be something He dislikes to be, and if there were something He dislikes to be, then there would be something He abhors to be. This necessitates that sins occurred whether God wills or abhors, and this is the attribute of the weak and overpowered, and our Lord is exalted above that, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a two-part proof establishing that nothing occurs contrary to God's will. First, Ash'ari states a premise: evidence proves all created things (*al-muḥdathāt*) are God's creations. Since it is impossible for God to do what He does not will, it follows that nothing from others can occur against His will, because all such occurrences are ultimately God's acts. Second, he considers the contrary: if something existed that God does not will, it would be something He dislikes to exist, and that would mean He abhors its existence. This would imply that sins happen regardless of God's wish, which is a sign of weakness and being overpowered—impossible for God. The argument reinforces that everything occurs by God's will, including acts of obedience and disobedience, but He may dislike the latter yet still will them for wisdom.

Key Terms
يَكْرَهُ (dislikes)
يَأْبَى (abhors)
ضعيف مقهور (weak and overpowered)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God wills sin, He approves of it.
Correction: This argument merely shows that He wills the existence of sinful acts, not that He approves of them. He dislikes and abhors them in terms of moral approval.

Checkpoint
What does Ash'ari conclude would be the attribute of God if something existed against His will?
A) Merciful and just.
B) Weak and overpowered.
C) Knowing and wise.
D) Patient and forgiving.

Key Takeaway
Anything occurring against God's will would imply divine weakness, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you found yourself believing that something happened outside of God's will, perhaps due to its apparent evil? How does this passage correct that belief, and what does it ask you to trust about God's sovereignty—even over what you dislike?
?
What sin in your life have you considered 'too great' to be included in God's will? How does the reductio argument force you to either accept that God's will encompasses all things—including your sin—or face the absurd conclusion that God is weak?
?
If you truly believed that nothing occurs against God's will, how would that change the way you respond to a recent disappointment or tragedy? Would you still feel entitled to question God's wisdom, or would you humble yourself before His absolute power?

The Logical Proof That All Acts Are by Allah's Will
The first part of Ash'ari's proof: logical necessity of universal divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical connection between Allah's will and the occurrence of all events, and why the occurrence of sins does not imply weakness in Allah.

Core Idea
If Allah cannot act against His will, nothing can happen against His will.

Ash'ari opens this passage with a powerful logical proof: since it is impossible for Allah to do what He does not will, it follows that no creature can do what Allah does not will, because all acts are ultimately His. This sets the foundation for understanding divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first part of the Arabic text carefully. Notice the conditional structure 'إذا ... استحال ... استحال'.
This is a tightly reasoned argument; each clause matters.
2
Understand the implication
The argument is that if Allah cannot do something against His will, then creatures cannot do something against His will either. Why? Because all acts are attributed to Allah as Creator.
3
Consider the consequence
If something occurred against Allah's will, it would mean He dislikes its existence, which would imply weakness. But Allah is exalted above that.
This shows the absurdity of the contrary assumption.
4
Reflect on the significance
This proof shows that even sins are willed by Allah in the sense of His creative will, not necessarily His legislative approval.
Keep this distinction in mind as we proceed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذَا استحالَ أَنْ يفعل الباري -تعالى- ما لا يريدُهُ استحالَ أَنْ يقعَ مِن غيرِهِ ما لا يُرِيدُهُ إِذْ كانَ ذلك أجمعُ أفعالا لله -تعالى-.
وأيضًا فلو كان في العَالَمِ ما لا يريده الله -تعالى-، لكانَ ما يَكْرَهُ كَوْنَهُ، ولو كَانَ ما يَكْرَهُ كَوْنَه، لَكَانَ مَا يَأْبَى كَونَه . وهذا يُوجِبُ أَنَّ المعاصِيَ كانَتْ، شاءَ اللَّهُ أَم أَبَى ، وهذه صِفةُ الضعيف المقهور، وتعالَى ربُّنا عن ذلكَ علوا كبيرًا .
مِثالُ الزَّمِنِ فإن قال قائل : خَبِّرُونَا عن ملك من ملوكِ الدُّنْيَا، لَوْ مَرَّ برجلٍ مُقْعَدِ زَمِن أعمى فشتَمَهُ، والملك لا يريد شَتْمه، أتقولون إِنَّ الملك يلْحَقُهُ في ذلك ضعفٌ ووَهَنْ وتقصير عَنْ بُلُوغ ما يُرِيدُهُ؛ إذ أرادَ أنْ لَا يَشْتُمَهُ فَشَتَمَهُ؟
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثاتِ
قيل له : أجل؛ ولَوْ لم يكُنْ ما أرادَ الزَّمِنُ مِن شَتْمِهِ وكانَ ما أرادَهُ الملِكُ مِنْ مَدْحِهِ إيَّاه، كانَ ذلك أولَى بِزَوالِ الضعفِ والوَهَنِ عَنْهُ.
على أن الملك إذا لم يُرِدْ شتمَ الأعمى المقْعَد لَهُ، فقد كَرِهَ شَتمُهُ إيَّاهُ، وأَبَى ذلكَ، وقَدْ كانَ شَتْمُهُ، شَاءَ ذلِكَ الملِكُ أو أَبَاه، وهذِهِ صِفَةُ الضَّعْفِ والوَهَنِ .
وأيضًا فإن مَنْ إذا أرادَ مِنَّا أمرًا كانَ، وإذا لم يُرِدْ كونَهُ لَمْ يَكُنْ، أَوْلَى بصفة الاقتدارِ، ممنْ يُريدُ كونَ ما لَا يَكُونُ، وأنْ لا يكون ما يكونُ. وربُّ العالمين لا يوصف إلا بالوصف الذي هو أولى بصفة الاقتدار .
If it is impossible for the Creator - exalted - to do what He does not will, then it is impossible for anything else to occur that He does not will, since all of that are acts of Allah - exalted. And also, if there were in the world something that Allah - exalted - does not will, then He would dislike its existence, and if He dislikes its existence, then He would reject its existence. This entails that sins occur whether Allah wills or refuses, and this is the attribute of the weak and overcome, and our Lord is exalted above that greatly. The example of the lame: if a speaker says: Tell us about a king of the kings of the world, if he passed by a man who is quadriplegic, lame, blind, and he insulted him, while the king does not will his insult, do you say that the king is thereby affected by weakness, feebleness, and deficiency from attaining what he wills, since he willed that he not insult him but he insulted him? Chapter on speech about the will and that it encompasses all originated things. It was said to him: Yes; and if what the lame man willed of insulting him did not occur, and what the king willed of his praising him occurred, that would be more fitting for the removal of weakness and feebleness from him. Moreover, since the king did not will the insult of the blind quadriplegic to him, he disliked his insulting him, and he rejected that, and yet his insult occurred, whether that king willed or rejected it, and this is the attribute of weakness and feebleness. And also, whoever among us, when he wills a matter it occurs, and when he does not will its existence it does not occur, is more deserving of the attribute of power than one who wills the existence of what does not occur and that what occurs not occur. And the Lord of the worlds is not described except by the attribute which is more fitting for the attribute of power.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that all events occur by Allah's will, followed by an objection-and-answer using an analogy. The structure is: (1) a logical proof, (2) an objection, (3) an answer. 

The author first argues that if it is impossible for Allah to do what He does not will, it is likewise impossible for any creature to do what Allah does not will, because all acts are ultimately acts of Allah. If something occurred against His will, He would dislike and reject it, but since sins do occur, they would then occur against His will, implying weakness—which is impossible for Allah. 

Then an objector raises an analogy: a king passes a lame blind man who insults him, but the king does not will the insult. Does this mean the king is weak for failing to prevent the insult? The objector implies that if the king were truly powerful, he would prevent the insult. 

The answer begins by agreeing ('ajal') but turns the analogy around: if the king had willed praise and that praise had occurred, that would show his power more than the undesired insult occurring. The point is that the occurrence of what one does not will does not necessarily indicate weakness; rather, the inability to prevent it would be weakness. Since the king did not will the insult, and it occurred, one might think him weak, but the true attribute of power is that one's will is effective—what one wills occurs, and what one does not will does not occur. This applies to the king only partially, but Allah is absolutely powerful: whatever He wills occurs, and whatever He does not will does not occur. Thus, the occurrence of sins cannot be against His will, because then His will would be ineffective.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الضعف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sins occur against Allah's will because Allah hates them.
Correction: Ash'ari argues that if Allah hated sins and they still occurred, that would imply He is weak. Instead, Allah wills all events in the sense of creative will, though He hates them in terms of legislative will.

Checkpoint
According to the logical proof, why is it impossible for anything to occur against Allah's will?
A) Because Allah controls everything directly.
B) Because Allah cannot do what He does not will, and all acts are His.
C) Because Allah punishes anyone who does wrong.
D) Because angels prevent it.

Key Takeaway
All events, including sins, occur by Allah's will; otherwise, Allah would be weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every act, including your sins, occurs by Allah's will, does that make you feel less accountable or more dependent on His mercy? How does this truth change the way you approach repentance?
?
When you disobey Allah, do you truly believe that He could have prevented it but willed to let it happen for a wisdom? How does that affect your gratitude for His forgiveness?
?
The passage says that if something occurred against Allah's will, He would be weak. Do you live as though Allah is in full control, or do you sometimes act as if your choices escape His will? What would it look like to surrender that illusion today?

The King Analogy Refuting the Objection of Weakness
Ash'ari's analogy answering the objection of weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari uses the analogy of a king and a lame man to show that the occurrence of what one does not will does not necessarily indicate weakness.

Core Idea
True power is having one's will fulfilled, not preventing every unwanted event.

An objector raises a scenario: a king passes a lame man who insults him, but the king does not will the insult. Does this mean the king is weak? Ash'ari answers by turning the analogy around and then drawing the real lesson about divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy passage
Carefully read the story of the king and the lame blind man. Understand the objector's point: the king could not prevent the insult.
Notice the dialogue structure.
2
See the initial response
The author first agrees ('ajal') but then shifts the focus to what would show true power.
The agreement is a rhetorical device.
3
Understand the criterion
True power is that what one wills occurs and what one does not will does not occur. The king's will is not fully effective.
This is the key principle.
4
Apply to Allah
Allah's will is absolutely effective, so nothing can occur against His will. The analogy shows that the occurrence of unwanted events does not prove weakness in Allah.

If it is impossible for the Creator - exalted - to do what He does not will, then it is impossible for anything else to occur that He does not will, since all of that are acts of Allah - exalted. And also, if there were in the world something that Allah - exalted - does not will, then He would dislike its existence, and if He dislikes its existence, then He would reject its existence. The example of the lame: if a speaker says: Tell us about a king of the kings of the world, if he passed by a man who is quadriplegic, lame, blind, and he insulted him, while the king does not will his insult, do you say that the king is thereby affected by weakness, feebleness, and deficiency from attaining what he wills, since he willed that he not insult him but he insulted him? Chapter on speech about the will and that it encompasses all originated things. It was said to him: Yes; and if what the lame man willed of insulting him did not occur, and what the king willed of his praising him occurred, that would be more fitting for the removal of weakness and feebleness from him. Moreover, since the king did not will the insult of the blind quadriplegic to him, he disliked his insulting him, and he rejected that, and yet his insult occurred, whether that king willed or rejected it, and this is the attribute of weakness and feebleness. And also, whoever among us, when he wills a matter it occurs, and when he does not will its existence it does not occur, is more deserving of the attribute of power than one who wills the existence of what does not occur and that what occurs not occur. And the Lord of the worlds is not described except by the attribute which is more fitting for the attribute of power.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that all events occur by Allah's will, followed by an objection-and-answer using an analogy. The structure is: (1) a logical proof, (2) an objection, (3) an answer. 

The author first argues that if it is impossible for Allah to do what He does not will, it is likewise impossible for any creature to do what Allah does not will, because all acts are ultimately acts of Allah. If something occurred against His will, He would dislike and reject it, but since sins do occur, they would then occur against His will, implying weakness—which is impossible for Allah. 

Then an objector raises an analogy: a king passes a lame blind man who insults him, but the king does not will the insult. Does this mean the king is weak for failing to prevent the insult? The objector implies that if the king were truly powerful, he would prevent the insult. 

The answer begins by agreeing ('ajal') but turns the analogy around: if the king had willed praise and that praise had occurred, that would show his power more than the undesired insult occurring. The point is that the occurrence of what one does not will does not necessarily indicate weakness; rather, the inability to prevent it would be weakness. Since the king did not will the insult, and it occurred, one might think him weak, but the true attribute of power is that one's will is effective—what one wills occurs, and what one does not will does not occur. This applies to the king only partially, but Allah is absolutely powerful: whatever He wills occurs, and whatever He does not will does not occur. Thus, the occurrence of sins cannot be against His will, because then His will would be ineffective.

Key Terms
الضعف
الوهن
الاقتدار
الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something happens that Allah does not will, that means Allah is weak because He could not prevent it.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that true power is in the effectiveness of one's will, not in preventing every unwanted event. Since Allah's will is absolutely effective, nothing can occur against His will.

Checkpoint
In the king analogy, what does Ash'ari argue is the true sign of power?
A) Being able to prevent all insults.
B) Having one's will fulfilled (what one wills occurs, and what one does not will does not occur).
C) Being strong enough to punish the offender.
D) Being feared by everyone.

Key Takeaway
The occurrence of what one does not will does not prove weakness; rather, weakness is when one's will is ineffective. Allah's will is never ineffective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something happens that you strongly disliked, did you ever feel that Allah's power was diminished or that He didn't care? How does this passage correct that feeling?
?
Consider an area of your life where you will something (e.g., to stop a sin) but it keeps happening. How does the king analogy help you understand your own weakness and turn to Allah's perfect power?
?
Are you willing to accept that every event, even those you hate, occurs by Allah's will? What does that demand from you in terms of submission and trust?

Objection to Divine Will as Requiring Helpers
Objection and partial response on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilī objection that God's will, if analogous to human will, implies weakness, and the beginning of Imam Ash'ari's rebuttal challenging that analogy.

Core Idea
God's will does not need helpers—unlike human will.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange from Imam Ash'ari's al-Luma'. An objector (likely Mu'tazilī) argues that if God's will operated like human will, it would require external support, implying imperfection. Ash'ari begins his response by questioning the basis of this analogy. The argument is incomplete, ending mid-sentence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a chapter title, an objection, and the start of a response.
The text ends abruptly—be aware of the incompleteness.
2
Identify the objection
The objector uses two analogies: a king and a crippled blind man, and a general statement about human will. Summarize the objection in your own words.
3
Identify the beginning of the response
Ash'ari responds by turning the objector's reasoning against him: if power depends on followers, then one who wills the opposite would be more powerful. He also challenges the premise that human power comes from helpers.
The response is incomplete; note where it breaks off.
4
Reflect on the incompleteness
Given the abrupt end, consider what Ash'ari might have concluded. How would he defend the doctrine that divine will does not entail weakness?
This lesson covers only the available text.

Chapter on speech concerning will and that it encompasses all originated things. It was said to him: Yes, and if what the ailing man intended of his insult did not occur, and what the king intended of his praise of him occurred, that would be more fitting for the removal of weakness and frailty from him. And also, whoever among us when he wills a thing it is, and when he does not will it to be it is not, is more deserving of the attribute of power than one who wills that what is not should be and that what is should not be. And the Lord of the worlds is not described except by the attribute which is more deserving of the attribute of power. Then if a questioner says: When one among us wills a thing it is, and when he does not will it it is not, his power is only through those who follow and help him, and his weakness is due to the fewness of his helpers and followers, and the Lord of the worlds does not seek reinforcement through anyone. It is said to them: What prevents you, if this is as you claim, that one who from his act wills that what is not should be and that what is should not be is more deserving of the attribute of power than one who wills that what is should be and that what is not should not be, because he is only correctly described with power because he is one who seeks reinforcement through his act, his power must be through those who help him, and his weakness through those who fail him? And it is said to them: Why do you claim that one among us who wills that what is should be is only correctly described with power because he is one who becomes strong through the multitude of those who follow him and becomes weak through the multitude of those who fail him? Then if they say: Because this among us is so, it is said: Likewise, the wise act only indicates that one from whom it appears is knowing and powerful because he is one who knows through knowledge and is powerful through power, because thus we have found among us those whose wise acts indicate that he is knowing and powerful. Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is part of a dialectical argument about divine will (al-irāda) and power (al-iqtidār). It opens with a chapter title stating that God's will encompasses all originated things. The text then presents an objection (likely from a Mu'tazilī opponent) arguing that if God's will were similar to human will, it would entail weakness because human will only succeeds with helpers. The objection uses a comparison: a king who wills praise from a disabled person but does not will insult, yet the insult still occurs—this shows the king's weakness. The objector concludes that one whose will is always effective is more deserving of power than one whose will sometimes fails. The objector then asserts that human effective will depends on followers and helpers, so God, who does not need reinforcement, cannot be described with such will. The author's response (beginning on the next line) uses a counter-argument: If the opponent's reasoning is followed, then one whose will produces the opposite of what occurs would be more powerful. The author then challenges the opponent's premise that human power depends on numbers of followers. Finally, the author draws an analogy: just as wise acts in humans indicate knowledge and power from knowledge and power (not from helpers), so too should God's acts indicate His attributes. The argument is cut off mid-sentence, so the conclusion is missing.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will is like human will and requires external support or helpers.
Correction: God's will is independent of any helper; His power is intrinsic, not dependent on followers.

B) Such a person's power is only through those who follow and help him.
C) Such a person's will never fails.
D) Such a person is comparable to God in power.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilī objection that God's will implies weakness is rebutted by showing that human power's dependence on helpers does not apply to God.

?
The author argues that true power is shown by the ability to produce opposite outcomes. When a prayer of yours goes unanswered, do you feel God's power is limited? How can the logic of this passage reshape your trust in God's will, even when it contradicts your desires?

The Reductio on Divine Power
Ash'ari presents a reductio against Mu'tazila definition of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari uses reductio ad absurdum to challenge the Mu'tazila notion that divine power is analogous to human power depending on followers.

Core Idea
If power means willing what exists, then willing the opposite would be even greater power.

Ash'ari begins by attacking the Mu'tazila assumption that God's power is like human power, which requires followers for strength. He presents a reductio: if the criterion of power is bringing about what one wills, then someone who wills the opposite would be more powerful. This forces the Mu'tazila to reconsider their definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the rhetorical question structure.
The author uses a direct address 'It is said to them'.
2
Identify the reductio premise
Find the conditional statement 'if this is as you claim' and what follows. What assumption of the Mu'tazila is being challenged?
3
Understand the definition of power
The passage implies that the Mu'tazila define power as bringing about what one wills. How does the reductio expose a problem with that definition?
Consider who would be considered 'more powerful' under that definition.
4
Reflect on the implication about supporters
Notice the phrase about being strengthened by action and power depending on supporters. What does this reveal about the Mu'tazila's underlying assumption?
This sets up the next part of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال لهم : فما أنكرتُم إن كانَ هذا على ما تَدعونَ، أَنْ يكونَ مَنْ أرادَ مِنْ فِعله كونَ ما لا يكونُ، وأن لا يكون [ما يكون] فهو أولى بصفة الاقتدار مِمَّن يريدُ كونَ ما يكونُ وألا يكون ما لا يكونُ، [لأنه ] إنما يصح وصفه بالاقتدار ؛ لأنه ممن يتكثّرُ بفعله، ويجب اقتدارُه بِمَن ينصرُهُ، وضَعْفُهُ بِمنْ يَقْعُدُ عَنْهُ؟! ويقال لهم : لم زعمتم أن من أراد مِنا كونَ ما يكون إنما يصح وصفه بالاقتدارِ ؛ لأنّه ممنْ يقْوَى بِكثرة مَنْ يتبعُه، ويضعُفُ بكثرةٍ مَنْ يقْعُدُ عَنْهُ؟ فإنْ قالوا : لأنَّ هذا فيما بيننا هكذا، قيل : وكذلك إنما يدلُّ الفعلُ الحِكْميُّ على أنّ مَنْ ظَهرَ مِنْه عالِمٌ قادِرٌ ؛ لأنَّه ممَّنْ يَعلَمُ بعِلم ويَقْدِرُ بقُدرَةٍ ؛ لأنَّا كذلكَ وجَدْنا، فيما بيننا، مَنْ دلتِ الأفعال الحكمية على أنه عالِمٌ قادِرُ . فما أنكرتُمْ من أنه واجب على اعتلالكم أن لا تدل الأفعال الحكمية على أنَّ البارِيَ -تعالى- قادر عالمٌ .
It is said to them: 'What prevents you, if this is as you claim, that one who from his action wills the existence of what does not exist, and the non-existence of what exists, is more worthy of the attribute of power than one who wills the existence of what does exist and the non-existence of what does not exist? Because he is only correctly described as powerful because he is one who is strengthened by his action, and his power depends on those who support him, and his weakness by those who hold back from him?' And it is said to them: 'Why do you claim that one among us who wills the existence of what exists is only correctly described as powerful because he is strengthened by the multitude of his followers, and weakened by the multitude of those who hold back from him?' If they say: 'Because this is how it is among us,' it is said: 'Likewise, the wise act only indicates that the one from whom it appears is knowing and powerful because he is one who knows by knowledge and is powerful by power, because we have found it thus among us: that wise acts indicate that one is knowing and powerful. So what prevents you from it being necessary according to your reasoning that wise acts do not indicate that the Exalted Creator is powerful and knowing?'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's claim that God's power is analogous to human power, which depends on followers. The Ash'ari begins with a reductio ad absurdum: if power means willing what exists and not willing what does not exist (as humans do), then one who wills the opposite would be even more powerful. Then he questions their standard that human power depends on followers. They respond that this is how it is among humans. The Ash'ari then turns their own analogy against them: if human wise acts indicate knowledge and power because humans have knowledge and power, then by the same reasoning, God's wise acts should indicate His knowledge and power—but on their own reasoning, since God has no followers, His acts would not indicate power and knowledge, which is absurd. Thus their position is self-defeating.

Key Terms
الاقتدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power is similar to human power in that it depends on followers.
Correction: God's power is absolute and does not depend on any external support.

Checkpoint
What is the reductio premise that the Ash'ari challenges?
A) That God's power depends on angels.
B) That power means bringing about what one wills.
C) That power is measured by the number of followers.
D) That human power is limited.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila definition of power leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Turning the Analogy Against the Mu'tazila
Ash'ari turns the Mu'tazila's analogy against them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari turns the Mu'tazila's own analogy to show that their reasoning undermines their belief in God's power and knowledge.

Core Idea
If human acts indicate power because of followers, then God's acts would not indicate His power on that logic.

After the reductio, Ash'ari directly challenges the Mu'tazila's claim that power depends on followers. When they admit this is based on human experience, he turns the tables: if we use human experience to judge divine attributes, then since God has no followers, His actions would not indicate His power and knowledge—an unacceptable conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the direct question and response structure.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's response
Find how they justify the claim that power depends on followers. What reason do they give?
They appeal to human experience.
3
See how Ash'ari applies the same logic to God
Notice the parallel: if human wise acts indicate knowledge and power because of human experience, then the same should apply to God. But what is the problem?
God does not have followers like humans do.
4
Understand the self-defeating conclusion
The Ash'ari concludes that on their reasoning, wise acts would not indicate God's power and knowledge. Why is that problematic for the Mu'tazila?
They themselves affirm that God is powerful and knowing.

Why God Does Not Compel Faith
Ash'ari refutes Mu'tazila on divine will and compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school holds that God's will is for faith to be voluntary so that reward is meaningful, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite objection that God could compel belief.

Core Idea
God wills faith voluntarily to preserve human accountability and reward.

Imam Ash'ari opens a new chapter on divine will, asserting that God's will encompasses all created acts. He then addresses a Mu'tazilite objection: if God is all-powerful, why doesn't He compel belief? This would show no weakness. Ash'ari's response is subtle: God wills faith to be a voluntary act so that believers earn reward. Compulsion would nullify the very basis of reward. This passage clarifies that God's will is not constrained by inability but by a wise purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Ash'ari structures his argument: statement, objection, response.
2
Identify the components
Identify the initial statement about divine will, the Mu'tazilite objection, and the Ash'ari response. Understand the flow of the argument.
3
Understand the analogy used
The Mu'tazila use an analogy: human wise acts indicate a knowing/powerful agent. Reflect on how this is used to set up the objection about compulsion.
This analogy is a common kalam tool.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objection says compulsion is possible and would show no weakness. Ash'ari responds by explaining the purpose of will: voluntary faith for reward.
Key insight: God's will is purposeful, not arbitrary.
5
Grasp the theological implication
Consider why this matters for understanding divine justice and human accountability. The response preserves human free will in earning reward.
This connects to broader soteriology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلامِ في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ الحكمية على أنَّ البارِيَ -تعالى- قادر عالمٌ . وكلك يُعَارَضُونَ بأَنَّ الأفعال الحكمية تدل على أن من ظهرت منه عالِمٌ قادِر ؛ لأنه ممن له علم وقدرة، من أجل أن ذلك فيما بيننا كذلك. فإن قال قائل : ما أنكرتُم أن لا يلحق الباري الضعفُ والوَهنُ والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده ؛ لأنّه يقدر أنْ يُلجِيءَ عِبادَه إلى ما أرادَ كونَهُ منهم؟ قيل لهم : إن الباري -تعالى- إنما أراد كون الإيمان منهم - أصلك بأن يقع ذلك منهم طوعًا يستحقون عليه الثواب، وإذا أَلْجَأَهُم إليه لم يكونوا - عِنْدَكَ - طائعين، ولا للثواب مستحقين .
Chapter on speech about the will, and that it encompasses all created things that are wise acts, that the Exalted Creator is powerful and knowing. And all of them are refuted by [the statement] that wise acts indicate that the one from whom they appear is knowing and powerful, because he is one who has knowledge and power, because that is how it is among us. So if someone says: 'What prevents you from asserting that weakness, feebleness, and inability to achieve what He wills do not befall the Creator, because He is able to compel His servants to what He wills to come from them?' It is said to them: 'The Exalted Creator only willed the existence of faith from them according to your principle, that it occurs from them voluntarily so that they deserve reward for it, and if He compelled them to it, they would not be — in your view — obedient, nor deserving of reward.'
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on the divine will, asserting that it encompasses all created things and that God is powerful and knowing. It then presents a refutation: the Mu'tazila claim that wise acts indicate the agent is knowing and powerful because they have knowledge and power—based on human analogy. An objection is raised: if God is powerful, why could He not compel people to believe? That would show no weakness. The answer is that God wills faith to be voluntary so that humans deserve reward. Compulsion would nullify the voluntary nature and thus reward. This refutes the idea that God's inability to compel is a defect; rather, it is by His wise will that He leaves humans free to earn reward.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المُحْدَثَات
الطوع
الإلجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's inability to compel belief shows weakness or limitation in His power.
Correction: Ash'ari explains that God deliberately wills faith to be voluntary so that humans can earn reward; compulsion would negate the purpose of faith as a test.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari, why does God not compel people to have faith?
A) Because He is unable to compel them.
B) Because He wills it to be voluntary so they deserve reward.
C) Because compulsion would make them stronger in faith.
D) Because the Mu'tazila deny compulsion.

Key Takeaway
God wills human faith to be a voluntary act, not compelled, so that it becomes a basis for reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you could be compelled to believe without doubt, would you choose that? Why does God not do that? How does that affect your gratitude for being given a choice?
?
In what areas of your life are you trying to compel others to believe or act as you want, ignoring that God Himself does not compel? What does this passage teach you about respecting others' free will?

God's Will and the Refutation of Compulsion
Ash'ari announces the scope of divine will and refutes an objection about God's power to compel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari argument distinguishes between God's power to compel and His wise choice not to, and how this is grounded in the purpose of faith and reward.

Core Idea
God's not compelling faith is not weakness but wisdom, preserving the voluntary reward of faith.

Imam al-Ash'ari anticipates a powerful objection: if God can compel belief, why does He not? Some might think this implies a limit on His power. In this passage, Ash'ari refutes that by clarifying the very purpose of faith—it must be voluntary to merit reward. Let's read the passage to see how he defends divine will and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage opens the chapter and then immediately presents an objection and response.
The passage is a complete unit: statement of topic, objection, and answer.
2
Identify the objection
Find the sentence that begins with 'فإن قال قائل'. This is the opponent's challenge. What exactly does he claim about God? That weakness, frailty, and failure could be attributed to God if He does not compel His servants to do what He wills.
The opponent uses God's ability to compel as a premise to argue that not compelling implies imperfection.
3
Analyze the answer
Read Ash'ari's response starting with 'قيل لهم'. He does not deny God's ability to compel. Instead, he points out that God willed faith to occur voluntarily (ṭawʿan) so that believers would deserve reward (thawāb). Compulsion would negate voluntariness and thus the basis for reward.
This shows that divine wisdom, not lack of power, explains why God does not force faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلامِ في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ الحكمية على أنَّ البارِيَ -تعالى- قادر عالمٌ . وكذلك يُعارَضون بأن الأفعال الحكمية تدل على أن من ظهرت منه عالم قادر؛ لأنه ممن له علم وقدرة، من أجل أن ذلك فيما بيننا كذلك. فإن قال قائل: ما أنكرتم أن لا يلحق الباري الضعف والوهن والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده؛ لأنه يقدر أن يُلجئ عباده إلى ما أراد كونه منهم؟ قيل لهم: إن الباري تعالى إنما أراد كون الإيمان منهم - أصلك بأن يقع ذلك منهم طوعاً يستحقون عليه الثواب، وإذا ألجأهم إليه لم يكونوا - عندك - طائعين، ولا للثواب مستحقين.
Chapter on speech about the will and that it encompasses all contingent things, according to the fact that the Creator - exalted - is powerful, knowing. And similarly they object by saying that the contingent actions indicate that the one from whom they appear is knowing, powerful; because he is one who possesses knowledge and power, because that is the case among us. So if someone says: What prevents you from admitting that weakness, frailty, and failure to achieve what He wills might befall the Creator, because He is able to compel His servants to do what He willed from them? It is said to them: The Creator - exalted - only willed the occurrence of faith from them - according to your principle - that it should occur from them voluntarily, deserving reward for it; and if He compelled them to it, they would not - according to you - be voluntary, nor deserving of reward.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on the divine attribute of will (al-irāda). The author first states the topic: that the will encompasses all created things, and that God is described as powerful and knowing. He then anticipates an objection from the Mu'tazila, who argue that contingent actions (al-afʿāl al-ḥukmiyya) indicate a doer who is knowing and powerful, based on analogy with human actions. The objector then poses a challenge: why do you deny that weakness (ḍaʿf), frailty (wahn), and failure (taqṣīr) could affect God if He does not attain what He wills? After all, He is able to compel (yuljiʾ) His servants to do what He wills from them. The author's answer is that God willed faith to occur voluntarily (ṭawʿan) so that they would deserve reward (thawāb). If He compelled them, they would not be voluntary agents and would not deserve reward. Thus, God's not compelling is not a sign of weakness, but rather a wise choice to preserve the meaningfulness of reward. The argument follows the structure of objection (ifāda) and answer (jawāb).

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's failure to force everyone to believe means He is unable to do so, implying a limitation on His power.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that God has the power to compel (as the objector admits), but He chooses not to because faith must be voluntary for reward to be meaningful. Thus, not compelling is a matter of wisdom, not inability.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Ash'ari's response, why did God not compel people to believe?
A) Because He is not able to compel them.
B) Because He wanted faith to be voluntary so they could deserve reward.
C) Because He does not will faith from them.
D) Because compulsion would be an injustice.

Key Takeaway
God's will for faith to be voluntary is the reason He does not compel, not any lack of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey Allah, do you check your heart: is this action born of genuine love and desire for reward, or am I going through the motions out of habit? How would knowing that Allah willed your faith to be voluntary change the way you perform your next prayer?
?
If Allah had compelled you to believe from birth, would your faith be as valuable to you? Right now, are you taking responsibility for the voluntary nature of your faith, or are you relying on circumstances to keep you obedient?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. Are you making excuses that Allah willed it, rather than facing your own choice to disobey? The lesson says God willed faith voluntarily—how does this truth demand that you stop blaming your environment and start taking ownership of your repentance?
