Weakness in God: A Parallel Argument
Ash'ari presents a parallel argument: as not willing implies weakness, so not having power over a willed outcome implies weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari uses a parallel argument to show that denying God's power to compel implies weakness in God.

Core Idea
Denying God's power to compel entails weakness just as not willing something does.

Imam al-Ash'ari continues his refutation by drawing a parallel. If the opponents claim that God cannot compel disbelief, then they must accept that God is weak in achieving His will. This lesson unpacks that logical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage is a single argument in two parts.
2
Identify the two sides of the parallel
The argument compares two cases: not willing something without power to compel, and willing something without power to realize it in the desired way. Find each case.
The first case is hypothetical about not willing; the second is about willing in a particular way.
3
Understand the logical necessity
Imam al-Ash'ari uses 'yajibu' (it is necessary) to show that both cases result in weakness. Ask yourself why this is a necessary consequence.
The necessity comes from the opponent's own premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكما يجب بكون ما لا يريده الضعف والوهن والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده، لو لم يوصف بالقدرة على أن يُلجئهم إلى ذلك، فكذلك يجب له الضعف والوهن والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده، إذا أراد كونه على وجه لا يوصف بالقدرة على وقوعه على ذلك الوجه. وأيضاً فإنه يلزم القدرية - إذا كان من قدر أن يؤمن قدر أن يكفر - أن لا يكون الباري تعالى موصوفاً بالقدرة على الأمر، الذي لو فعله لكانوا مؤمنين لا محالة؛ لأنهم يقدرون - عندهم - على أن يكفروا، عند نزول الآيات الملجئات إلى الإيمان، كما يقدرون أن يؤمنوا قبل ذلك، ومن قدر على الكفر عند نزول الآية لم يؤمن وقوعه منه.
For just as it is necessary that not willing something is weakness, frailty, and failure to achieve what He wills, if He were not described as having power to compel them to that, so likewise, weakness, frailty, and failure to achieve what He wills must befall Him, if He wills it to be in a way such that He is not described as having power over its occurrence in that way. And also, it necessarily follows for the Qadariyya - if one who is able to believe is able to disbelieve - that the Creator - exalted - is not described as having power over the matter which, if He did it, they would inevitably be believers; because they are able - according to them - to disbelieve at the time of the compelling signs to faith, just as they are able to believe before that, and one who is able to disbelieve at the time of the sign cannot be sure that it will not occur from him.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage continues the author's refutation by presenting a parallel argument and then targeting the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) position. The author first argues by analogy: just as it is necessary (yajibu) that if God does not will something, and He is not described as having the power to compel them to it, that would be considered weakness (ḍaʿf) and deficiency, similarly, if God wills something to occur in a particular way (e.g., voluntary faith) and He is not described as having power to bring it about in that way (i.e., without compulsion), then that also would entail weakness. Since the opponents deny that God could compel, they are forced to admit that God cannot achieve faith in a voluntary manner, which is absurd. Then the author makes a specific point against the Qadariyya (who affirm human free will and deny God's creation of acts). If a person who is able to believe is also able to disbelieve, then even when God sends compelling signs (āyāt muljiʾāt) that would normally force belief, the person could still choose to disbelieve according to their own ability. This means God cannot guarantee that they will believe, and thus God would not be described as having power over the matter of making them inevitably believers. This is a reductio ad absurdum of the Mu'tazila position, showing it undermines God's omnipotence. The argument is structured as a conditional proof and a refutation of the Qadariyya's claim about human power.

Key Terms
ضعف
وهن
تقصير
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'ari merely asserts that weakness follows without logical proof.
Correction: Ash'ari's argument is a logical parallel: if the opponent's position is true, then weakness necessarily follows from their own premises.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari's argument, what follows if God wills something but is not described as having power over its occurrence in that way?
A) It is weakness, frailty, and failure.
B) It is not possible for God.
C) It is just and wise.
D) It shows God's compassion.

Key Takeaway
Denying God's power over the manner of events leads to attributing weakness to Him, just as not willing something without power does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God has power over the exact manner of every event, including the way you respond to Him, why do you still hold back from total trust? What area of your life are you treating as outside His control? How will you surrender that area to Him today?
?
When you disobey God, do you ever comfort yourself with the thought that He could have compelled you to obey but didn't? Does this argument challenge you to see your sin as a deeper rebellion against His will and power? How will you respond to His patience without compulsion?

Refuting the Qadariyya on Power to Disbelieve
Ash'ari refutes the Qadariyya by showing their view leads to denying God's power to ensure faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qadariyya position on human power to disbelieve undermines divine omnipotence, according to Imam al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
If a person can always disbelieve, God cannot guarantee faith even with compelling signs.

Imam al-Ash'ari targets the Qadariyya view that humans have independent power to choose faith or disbelief. He shows that this leads to a contradiction: God would not be able to ensure belief even when sending clear signs. This lesson explores that logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the second part of the Arabic passage and its translation. This argument is a reductio against the Qadariyya.
The Qadariyya are the Mu'tazila who affirm human free will.
2
Understand the Qadariyya premise
The premise: 'one who is able to believe is able to disbelieve.' This is the Qadariyya claim about human power.
This premise is accepted for the sake of argument.
3
Follow the reductio
If a person can disbelieve at the time of compelling signs (āyāt muljiʾāt), then God cannot be sure that belief will occur. Therefore, God is not described as having power over making them inevitably believers.
The compelling signs are miracles that would force belief.
4
Conclude the implication
The conclusion is that the Qadariyya position implies a limit on God's power, which is absurd for those who affirm divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage continues the author's refutation by presenting a parallel argument and then targeting the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) position. Checkpoint

?

The Universality of God's Will: Al-Ash'ari's Argument
Chapter heading with proof, objection, and rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's argument that if God does not will all actions, weakness would be attributed to Him, and why the objection that human and divine actions differ is invalid.

Core Idea
If God does not will an action, that implies weakness—so He must will all things.

This passage from al-Luma marks the beginning of Al-Ash'ari's discussion on the divine will. He argues that God's will must encompass all originated things, including human actions. To reject this would lead to an absurdity: attributing weakness to God. Al-Ash'ari then presents an objection from those who claim that human actions not willed by the agent are due to forgetfulness or weakness, which does not apply to God, and he refutes it.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter heading and initial argument
Start by reading the chapter heading and the first conditional argument. Notice how Al-Ash'ari sets up the logical structure.
The heading announces that the will encompasses all originated things.
2
Identify the objector's attempt to differentiate
The objector claims that when a human does not will his own action, it is due to forgetfulness or weakness, but this does not apply to God's actions. Identify this distinction.
3
Follow Al-Ash'ari's rebuttal
Al-Ash'ari responds by saying the situation is the same: if an agent knows the action and it is not willed, weakness follows regardless. Trace his reasoning.
The rebuttal is cut off, but the logic is clear.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this argument establishes that God must will all actions. How does rejecting the distinction preserve God's perfection?
The full answer likely completes the parity argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائر المُحْدَثَاتِ وأيضًا فلو كان يقعُ من الإنسان ما لا يريده الباري - سُبحانَه - ولا يلحقُ الباري بذلك وهن ولا ضعف ؛ لأنه قادر أن يُلجِنَّهُمْ إِلَيْهِ ، لجازَ أن يقعَ مِنَ الباري - سُبحانَه مِنْ أفعاله ما لا يريده، ولا يلحقه ضعف وتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده ؛ لأنه قادر على إيقاعه وتكوينه. فإن لم يجب هذا، ولزم بكون ما لا يريده من فعله الضعفُ والوهن ، لزم ذلك في فعلِ عباده. فإن قال قائل : ما أنكرتُم أن يكونَ كونُ ما لا يريدُهُ الإِنسانُ مِنْ فعلِهِ، يُوجِبُ وقوع ذلك عن سهو أو عن ضعفٍ وَوَهَنِ، وليسَ يلزم في كون ما لا يريدُه مِنْ فِعْلِ غيره، فوجب مثلُ ذلك في القديم أيضًا؟ قيل له : ليسَ الأمر كما ظننت ، بل القِصَّةُ فيما يكون من الإنسانِ ومن غيرِهِ واحدةٌ، وذلك أنَّ الإنسان إذا كان من فعله ما لا يريده، فإما أن يكون ذلك عن سهو، أو عن ضعف ووهن و تقصير عن بلوغ ما يُريدُه . وكذلك القولُ فيما يكونُ مِن غيرِهِ؛ لأنه إِنْ لم يكن ذلك عن سهو، فواجب أن يكون عن ضعف وتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده . وذلك أن العلة التي لها لزِمَ الإنسانَ - إذا كان عالما بما وقع منه ، وهو غيرُ
Chapter on speech about the will and that it encompasses all originated things. And also, if it were that what the human does that the Exalted Creator does not will occurs, and no weakness or feebleness attaches to the Creator because of that, because He is powerful to compel them to it, then it would be permissible that from the Exalted Creator—from His actions—what He does not will occurs, and no weakness or falling short of attaining what He wills attaches to Him, because He is powerful to cause it and bring it into existence. If this is not necessary, and weakness and feebleness necessarily follow from the occurrence of what He does not will from His own action, then the same necessarily follows in the action of His servants. If someone says: What prevents you from saying that the occurrence of what a human does not will from his own action necessitates its occurrence due to forgetfulness or due to weakness and feebleness, and it is not necessary in the occurrence of what he does not will from the action of another, so the like of that is necessary for the Eternal as well? It is said to him: The matter is not as you thought; rather, the story concerning what occurs from a human and from what occurs from another is one and the same. That is because the human, if there occurs from his action what he does not will, then either that is due to forgetfulness, or due to weakness and feebleness and falling short of attaining what he wills. And the same is the statement concerning what occurs from another; because if that is not due to forgetfulness, then it must be due to weakness and falling short of attaining what he wills. And that is because the cause due to which it was necessary for the human—if he is knowledgeable of what has occurred from him, and he is not...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and a proof followed by an objection and answer. The chapter is on the will (al-irāda) and its universality over all originated things. Al-Ash'ari begins with a conditional argument: if human actions that God does not will occur, and no weakness attaches to God because He can compel them, then by the same logic, if God Himself performs actions that He does not will, no weakness would attach because He is powerful to bring them about. The objector (Qadari) tries to distinguish: human actions not willed by the agent often occur due to forgetfulness or weakness, but when the action is from another (i.e., God), that is not the case—forgetfulness and weakness are impossible for God. Al-Ash'ari's answer is that the situation is the same: in both cases, if the agent knows the action and it is not willed, it must be due to weakness. For the human, if not forgetfulness, then weakness. So for God, if an action is not willed, it implies weakness. Therefore, to avoid attributing weakness to God, we must affirm that He wills all actions that occur.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
سهو (forgetfulness)
ضعف (weakness)
وهن (feebleness)
علة (cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God can allow actions He does not will without any implication of weakness, because He has the power to prevent them.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues that if an action occurs without God's will, it implies either forgetfulness (impossible for God) or weakness, because the agent cannot achieve what He wills. Thus, to preserve divine perfection, God must will all actions that occur.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ash'ari's rebuttal, what is the cause that forces weakness if an action occurs without the agent's will?
A) The action occurs due to forgetfulness or weakness
B) The action is caused by another agent
C) The action is inherently evil
D) The action is a test from God

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's argument for the universality of God's will is based on rejecting any distinction that would let human actions escape God's will without implying divine weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every action you take is willed by God, how would that change the way you view your own mistakes and shortcomings? Are there areas in your life where you act as if your choices are outside God's will, and what would it mean to surrender those to Him starting today?
?
Al-Ash'ari argues that any knowledge of an action occurring without one's will implies either forgetfulness or weakness. When you do something you know is wrong and later regret it, do you ever reflect on the fact that if God had not willed it, it would not have happened? How does that realization compel you to turn back to Him in repentance and trust, rather than despair?
?
Consider a recent situation where you felt that your own will conflicted with what happened. Instead of seeing it as a failure of your plans, could you see it as a manifestation of God's will overriding yours? What would it look like to actively embrace that and say, 'I accept Your decree, for You are never weak'?

The Universality of Divine Will: The Initial Argument
Ashari states the topic and presents a reductio argument for universal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Ashari's foundational argument that God's will must encompass all created events because any unwilled event would imply divine weakness.

Core Idea
If God did not will something, He would be weak—so all events must be willed.

This passage opens the chapter on divine will. Imam Ashari immediately asserts that God's will covers all created things. He then presents a powerful reductio argument: if a human could do something God does not will, and God is not weakened, then the same would apply to God's own actions; but if an unwilled act implies weakness in an agent, then it would apply to humans as well. This sets the stage for a rigorous defense of universal divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical flow: claim → conditional → reductio.
2
Identify the initial claim and the conditional argument
The passage begins by stating that divine will encompasses all created things. Then it proposes a conditional: if an unwilled human act does not weaken God, then an unwilled divine act also does not weaken God. Look for key terms like 'لجاز' and 'فإن لم يجب هذا'.
The argument uses symmetry between human and divine actions.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
If one denies the conditional and insists that an unwilled act implies weakness, then the same would apply to human acts. Why? Because humans also act, and if their unwilled acts imply weakness, then the same logic forces universal divine will. This refutes the idea that some events are outside God's will.
This is a classic kalam argument: assuming the opposite leads to an absurdity.
4
Reflect on the implications for divine attributes
Ashari connects will (irada) to power (qudra): if an event occurs against God's will, it would indicate inability to prevent it—a form of weakness. Since Allah is perfect, all events must be willed. Consider why this matters for tawhid.
This also defends the attribute of will as eternal and all-encompassing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ وأيضًا فلو كان يقعُ من الإنسان ما لا يريده الباري - سُبحانَه - ولا يلحقُ الباري بذلك وهن ولا ضعف ؛ لأنه قادر أن يُلجِنَّهُمْ إِلَيْهِ ، لجازَ أن يقعَ مِنَ الباري - سُبحانَه مِنْ أفعاله ما لا يريده، ولا يلحقه ضعف وتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده ؛ لأنه قادر على إيقاعه وتكوينه. فإن لم يجب هذا، ولزم بكون ما لا يريده من فعله الضعفُ والوهن ، لزم ذلك في فعلِ عباده. فإن قال قائل : ما أنكرتُم أن يكونَ كونُ ما لا يريدُهُ الإِنسانُ مِنْ فعلِهِ، يُوجِبُ وقوع ذلك عن سهو أو عن ضعفٍ وَوَهَنِ، وليسَ يلزم في كون ما لا يريدُه مِنْ فِعْلِ غيره، فوجب مثلُ ذلك في القديم أيضًا؟ قيل له : ليسَ الأمر كما ظننت ، بل القِصَّةُ فيما يكون من الإنسانِ ومن غيرِهِ واحدةٌ، وذلك أنَّ الإنسان إذا كان من فعله ما لا يريده، فإما أن يكون ذلك عن سهو، أو عن ضعف ووهن و تقصير عن بلوغ ما يُريدُه . وكذلك القولُ فيما يكونُ مِن غيرِهِ؛ لأنه إِنْ لم يكن ذلك عن سهو، فواجب أن يكون عن ضعف وتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده . وذلك أن العلة التي لها لزِمَ الإنسانَ - إذا كان عالما بما وقع منه ، وهو غيرُ مُريد له - الضعفُ والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده : أن مراده لم يقع ، وأنه لم يُرِدْهُ ؛ لأنه لو كان ما يريده لم يلحقه ضعف ولا وهَنْ ، فإذا لم يقع ، فإنّما لَحِقَهُ الوهنُ والتقصيرُ عن بلوغ ما يريدُهُ ؛ من أَجْلِ أنه وقع منه وهو عالم به غيرُ مُريدٍ لَه . فإذا كانت العلةُ ما ذكرنا ، وجَبَ أيضًا مثلُ ذلك فيما يقعُ من غيره وهو لا يريده ؛ لأنه إذا كانت العلة التي لها وجب أن يوصف الإنسان بضدّ العلم - في وقوع ما يقع منه - أنه وقع مِنهُ وهو لا يعلَمُه ، فكذلك قصة ما يقع من غيره وهو لا يعلمه؛ إذ كانت العلة في ذلك واحدة، وكذلك القولُ في الإرادة. وأيضًا فإنه : إذا كان من غيره ما لا يريده، فقد كَرِهَهُ، وإذا كره كَوْنَهُ فقَدْ أبَاهُ ، وهذا يُوجِبُ أن الشيء كان، شاء أم أبى، وهذه صفة الضَّعفِ والوهن.
Chapter on the discourse concerning the will and that it encompasses all created things. And also, if what occurs from a human is what the Creator, exalted is He, does not will, and the Creator is not thereby affected by weakness or feebleness—because He is able to compel them to it—then it would be permissible for what occurs from the Creator, exalted is He, of His acts to be what He does not will, and no weakness or failure to attain what He wills would affect Him, because He is able to bring it about and originate it. If this is not necessary, and by virtue of something He does not will being from His act, weakness and feebleness necessarily follow, then that would necessarily follow in the act of His servants. If someone says: 'What prevents you from holding that the occurrence of what a human does not will from his act entails that it occurs by forgetfulness or by weakness and feebleness, but that this does not necessarily follow in the occurrence of what He does not will from the act of another, so the like of that would be necessary regarding the Eternal as well?' It is said to him: 'The matter is not as you supposed. Rather, the case regarding what occurs from a human and from other than him is one and the same. For if something occurs from a human that he does not will, then it is either by forgetfulness or by weakness, feebleness, and failure to attain what he wills. And similarly the statement regarding what occurs from other than him; because if it is not by forgetfulness, then it must be by weakness and failure to attain what he wills. That is because the cause for which the human—if he is knowledgeable of what occurred from him and he does not will it—is necessarily characterized by weakness and failure to attain what he wills is that his intended object did not occur and that he did not will it; for if what he willed had occurred, no weakness or feebleness would have affected him. So when it did not occur, he is affected by feebleness and failure to attain what he wills, due to the fact that it occurred from him while he knows it and does not will it. So if the cause is as we have mentioned, then the like of that is also necessary regarding what occurs from another and he does not will it; because if the cause for which it is necessary to describe the human by the opposite of knowledge—in the occurrence of what occurs from him—is that it occurred from him while he does not know it, then similarly is the case of what occurs from another while he does not know it, since the cause in that is one. And likewise the statement concerning the will. And also, if what he does not will occurs from another, then he has disliked it, and if he dislikes its occurrence, he has rejected it, and this entails that the thing existed, whether he wills or rejects, and this is a description of weakness and feebleness.'
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the scope of divine will over all created events. Imam Ashari begins by stating the topic: the will (al-irada) encompasses all created things (al-muhdathat). He then presents a conditional argument: if it were possible for a human to do something that God does not will, and yet God suffers no weakness—because He can compel them—then it would also be possible for God to do something He does not will from His own acts, without weakness. But if one insists that an act contrary to will necessarily implies weakness in the agent, then the same would apply to God's servants (i.e., humans). This is a reductio ad absurdum aiming to show that all events must be willed by God.

An objector then raises a distinction: when a human does something he does not will, it is due to forgetfulness or weakness; but when another agent (God) does something He does not will, the same may not follow. Ashari replies that the situation is identical. He analyzes the cause: if a knowledgeable agent fails to bring about what he wills, weakness is implied. Conversely, if something occurs from him that he knows and does not will, that also indicates weakness because his will was not fulfilled. This cause is general: it applies whether the agent is human or divine. Therefore, for God, if something happened that He did not will, it would imply weakness, which is impossible. Hence, all events must be subject to His will.

He strengthens the argument by noting that disliking (karaha) an event is a form of rejection (aba'), and if something occurs despite that, it shows powerlessness—again impossible for God. The passage concludes that the description of weakness and feebleness would apply to anyone who fails to prevent an unwilled occurrence. Thus, God's will is universal.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المُحْدَثَات
ضعف
وهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that God can allow some events to occur without willing them, as long as He is able to prevent them.
Correction: Ashari argues that if an unwilled event occurs despite God's ability to prevent it, it still implies a failure to actualize His will, which is a form of weakness incompatible with divine perfection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the occurrence of an unwilled event imply about the agent?
A) It implies the agent is all-powerful.
B) It implies weakness or failure to achieve what the agent wills.
C) It implies the agent is indifferent.
D) It implies the agent is unaware.

Key Takeaway
All events must be willed by God because an unwilled event would imply a deficiency in divine power or will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something undesirable happens to you, do you genuinely accept that it was willed by God, or do you internally resist, thinking it should not have happened? How does that resistance reveal a hidden belief about the limits of God's will?
?
The argument shows that any event outside God's will would imply weakness in Him. Examine your own life: do you act as if some events are outside His control? What would change in your reactions if you fully internalised that everything is willed by Him?
?
If you truly believed that even your own mistakes are willed by God, how would your sense of guilt and your reliance on His mercy be transformed? Are you willing to submit to that belief today?

Defending Universality: Objection and Response
Ashari refutes an objection by identifying the universal cause of weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ashari refutes the objection that unwilled human acts result from forgetfulness or weakness, but the same does not apply to God.

Core Idea
The same cause—failure to achieve one's will—implies weakness in any agent, divine or human.

After setting out his initial argument, Imam Ashari anticipates an objection: perhaps when humans do something they do not will, it is due to forgetfulness or weakness, but God is not subject to such limitations. In this passage, Ashari dismantles the distinction by analyzing the underlying cause. He shows that if a knowledgeable agent fails to fulfill his will, that is weakness—regardless of whether the agent is human or divine. Thus, the objection collapses, and the universality of divine will stands.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector argues that unwilled human acts are due to forgetfulness or weakness, but this does not apply to God because the unwilled act is from another (i.e., from humans). Find the exact wording: 'ما أنكرتُم أن يكونَ كونُ ما لا يريدُهُ الإِنسانُ مِنْ فعلِهِ، يُوجِبُ وقوع ذلك عن سهو أو عن ضعفٍ وَوَهَنِ، وليسَ يلزم في كون ما لا يريدُه مِنْ فِعْلِ غيره'.
The objector tries to differentiate between acts of the self and acts of others.
2
Identify Ashari's initial reply
Ashari flatly rejects the distinction: 'ليس الأمر كما ظننت بل القصة فيما يكون من الإنسان ومن غيره واحدة'. He asserts that the case is identical. Then he explains why: if an unwilled act occurs from oneself, it's either by forgetfulness or weakness; if from another, the same cause (weakness) applies if not forgetfulness.
Notice the use of 'فإما أن يكون' for enumeration of possibilities.
3
Analyze the cause (illa)
Ashari identifies the underlying cause: when a knowledgeable agent does not will something that occurs, it is because his intended object did not occur. This indicates he could not achieve his will—which is weakness. This cause is universal: it applies whether the agent is human or divine. Trace the reasoning: 'العلة التي لها لزم الإنسان...'
The cause is not forgetfulness or the source of the act, but the mismatch between will and occurrence.
4
Extend the argument to God
If the cause is universal, then the same judgment applies to God: if something occurs that He does not will, He would be weak. Since that is impossible, all events must be willed by Him. The passage also adds that if God dislikes (karaha) something, it is a form of rejection (aba'), and if it still occurs, that is a sign of powerlessness.
This reinforces the earlier conclusion that God's will is all-encompassing.

D) The agent is not all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
Any unwilled event in a knowledgeable agent implies weakness because his will is not fulfilled; thus, God must will all events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something happens in your life that you strongly dislike, do you ever feel that Allah must not have willed it? Based on this lesson, what does that feeling imply about your view of Allah's power, and how can you train your heart to accept that His will is always effective?
?
If Allah's will is truly all-encompassing, then every hardship, loss, or trial you face is willed by Him. Are you willing to stop seeing any event as a 'mistake' or something that slipped past Allah? What would it cost you to hold that belief in your most painful moments?
?
Consider a time you felt weak because you could not achieve a goal despite trying. Now apply the source's logic: could it be that your weakness was precisely because Allah did not will it for you? How does recognizing that your will is subject to His change how you view your own efforts and failures?

Weakness Necessitated by Occurrence of What is Not Willed
Ash'ari argues that whatever occurs without one's will implies weakness, and answers the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari argues that anything occurring contrary to one's will implies weakness, and how he defends divine omnipotence through the answer to the objection.

Core Idea
If something occurs against one's will, that person is weak — and God cannot be weak.

Al-Ash'ari presents a clear analogy: just as ignorance is shown by events occurring without knowledge, weakness is shown by events occurring without will. He then faces an objection that perhaps God merely hates sin without being weak. His answer is decisive: if an act occurs despite opposition, the one opposed is shown to be weak. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the repetition of key terms like إرادة and كراهة.
2
Identify the core analogy
Ash'ari compares will to knowledge. Just as an event occurring without knowledge proves ignorance, an event occurring without will proves weakness. Find this analogy in the text.
The phrase 'كذلك القول في الإرادة' marks the final step of the analogy.
3
Examine the objection and answer
The opponent says God may hate an act without being weak. Ash'ari replies that hatred implies opposition, and if the act occurs despite opposition, it shows weakness. Understand this refutation.
The objection starts with 'فإن قالوا' and the answer with 'قيل لهم'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مريد له - الضعف والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده: أن مراده لم يقع، وأنه لم يرده؛ لأنه لو كان ما يريده لم يلحقه ضعف ولا وهن، فإذا لم يقع، فإنما لحقه الوهن والتقصير عن بلوغ ما يريده؛ من أجله أنه وقع منه وهو عالم به غير مريد له. فإذا كانت العلة ما ذكرنا، وجب أيضًا مثل ذلك فيما يقع من غيره وهو لا يريده؛ لأنه إذا كانت العلة التي لها وجب أن يوصف الإنسان بضد العلم - في وقوع ما يقع منه - أنه وقع منه وهو لا يعلمه، فكذلك قصة ما يقع من غيره وهو لا يعلمه؛ إذ كانت العلة في ذلك واحدة، وكذلك القول في الإرادة. وأيضًا فإنه: إذا كان من غيره ما لا يريده، فقد كرهه، وإذا كره كونه فقد أباه، وهذا يوجب أن الشيء كان شاء أم أبى، وهذه صفة الضعف والوهن. فإن قالوا: ما أنكرتم من أن الذي يجب في كون ما لا يريده الباري -تعالى- من عباده، أن يكون كارها لذلك فقط؛ ولا يجب في ذلك ضعف ولا وهن؟ قيل لهم: بل وقوع ذلك منهم، وهو كاره له، يوجب الضعف والوهن لا محالة؛ لأنه إذا كان ما كره كونه كان ما أبى كونه، وإذا كان ما أبى كونه، فقد كان الشيء شاءه أم أباه! وهذا يوجب أن الشيء كان شاء الله -تعالى- ذلك أم أباه، وهذه صفة الضعيف.
One who wills something - weakness and incapacity to attain what he wills: that his intention did not occur, and that he did not will it; because if what he willed had occurred, no weakness or feebleness would have befallen him. When it did not occur, feebleness and incapacity to attain what he wills befell him only because it occurred from him while he was knowing of it but not willing it. So if the cause is what we mentioned, the like of that is also necessary regarding what occurs from another and he does not will it; because if the cause on account of which a human is described as contrary to knowledge - in the occurrence of what occurs from him - is that it occurred from him while he does not know it, then likewise is the case of what occurs from another and he does not know it, since the cause in that is one. And likewise is the statement concerning will. And also: if there is from another what he does not will, then he has hated it, and if he hates its existence then he has opposed it. This necessitates that the thing occurred, whether he wills or refuses, and this is the attribute of weakness and feebleness. Then if they say: what do you deny that what is necessary regarding the occurrence of what the Exalted God does not will from His servants is that He hates that only, and no weakness or feebleness is necessary in that? It is said to them: rather, the occurrence of that from them while He hates it necessitates weakness and feebleness unavoidably; because if what He hates to exist exists, then what He opposes exists. And if what He opposes exists, then the thing occurred whether He wills it or opposes it! This necessitates that the thing occurred whether God - Exalted - wills that or opposes it, and this is the attribute of the weak.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument followed by an objection and answer. The author, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, first establishes a principle: if a person intends something but it does not occur, and he is aware of it but does not will it, this indicates weakness and incapacity. He then extends this by analogy to will: just as ignorance is shown when something occurs without knowledge, likewise feebleness is shown when something occurs without will. He adds that if something occurs that one does not will, one hates and opposes it, yet it still occurs; this implies the thing occurs despite opposition, which is a sign of weakness. The opponent objects: perhaps the only necessary implication is that God hates the act, not that He is weak. The author answers: hatred and opposition are the same; if the act occurs despite opposition, it shows the one opposing it is weak, because his opposition was not effective. The argument concludes that if something occurs even though God opposes it, that is a trait of the weak, which cannot be attributed to God. The author thus defends God's omnipotence by arguing that everything that occurs does so by God's will; if something occurred against His will, that would imply weakness, which is impossible.

Key Terms
إرادة
كراهة
ضعف
وهن
مريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for God to hate an act that occurs without implying weakness.
Correction: Ash'ari argues that if the act occurs despite God's hatred, it implies weakness because the hatred constitutes opposition, and the occurrence shows opposition is ineffective.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari, what does it imply if something occurs from someone while he is unwilling and aware of it?
A) It implies the person has perfect power.
B) It implies the person is weak and incapable.
C) It implies the person is unaware.
D) It implies the person is indifferent.

Key Takeaway
God's will encompasses all events; any occurrence against His will would imply weakness, which is impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage proves that if anything occurred against God's will, He would be weak. Do you truly live as if nothing happens outside His will, or do you secretly believe some events are beyond His control? What would change in your heart if you fully internalized this truth?
?
When you experience something painful or unjust, your soul may protest, 'This should not have happened.' This lesson says that such a thought implicitly accuses God of weakness. Can you repent from that hidden accusation and instead say, 'This happened by Your will, and Your will is perfect'? How would that reshape your patience?
?
You know that every sin you commit occurs by God's will (as this passage argues). Yet you are still responsible. Does this make you feel trapped or free? How does the truth that God wills even your failures drive you to depend on His mercy rather than your own strength?

Two Mu'tazilite Views on God's Will and Their Implications
Ash'ari identifies two Mu'tazilite positions on God's will and their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazilite positions on God's will regarding human actions and the problematic implication of equating will with command.

Core Idea
Equating God's will with His command leads to attributing hatred to Him for uncommanded actions.

Ash'ari presents two Mu'tazilite views on God's will in human actions. One view identifies God's will with His command; the other distinguishes them. He then shows that the first view leads to an unacceptable consequence: God would hate actions like those of children and the insane, which He does not command. This argument is used to refute the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice that Ash'ari directly introduces two groups within the Mu'tazila.
The passage is concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the two views
Find the two positions: one says God's will is command; the other says it is different from command. Note the Arabic terms: 'الأمر بها' and 'خلف غير الأمر بها'.
'خلف' here means 'different from'.
3
Analyze the implication
Ash'ari points out that if will equals command, then for actions God does not command (e.g., children's actions), He has no will. But absence of will necessitates hatred (كراهة), which is inappropriate for God. Ask yourself why this is a problem for the Mu'tazilite view.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا فإن المعتزلة رجلان أحدهما يقول: إن إرادة الله تعالى - في أفعال عباده الأمر بها. والآخر يقول: إرادته في أفعال عباده خلف غير الأمر بها. فمن ذهب إلى أنها الأمر، لزمه - إذا لم يكن الباري آمرًا بأفعال الأطفال والمجانين - أن يكون كارها لها؛ إذ كان يجب بنفي الإرادة لأفعال العباد الكراهة لها، والله -تعالى- لا يكره.
And also, the Mu'tazila are two men: one of them says that God's will - Exalted - regarding the actions of His servants is command to them. And the other says that His will regarding the actions of His servants is different from (khalaf), not command to them. So whoever holds that it is command, it follows for him - if the Creator is not commanding the actions of children and the insane - that He hates them; since the negation of will for the actions of servants necessitates hatred for them, and God - Exalted - does not hate.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a brief classification of the Mu'tazilite positions on God's will concerning human actions. The author identifies two main views: (1) Some Mu'tazila equate God's will with His command; that is, what God wills for His servants is what He commands them. (2) Others say God's will is different from (خلف) His command; it is something else. The author then points out an implication of the first view: if God's will is identical to command, then for actions He does not command—such as those of children and the insane—He would have no will (since He does not command them). The negation of will necessitates hatred, so God would hate those actions. But the author suggests that this leads to an undesirable consequence, as God does not hate (in the sense of weak opposition). The argument is that this view leads to attributing hatred to God for actions that occur, which would imply weakness as argued earlier.

Key Terms
إرادة
أمر
خلف
كراهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God does not will an action, He must hate it.
Correction: Ash'ari uses this as a consequence of the first Mu'tazilite view to show its absurdity, but it does not mean that God necessarily hates uncommanded actions in reality—the point is that the equation of will and command forces this unacceptable conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is the first Mu'tazilite view on God's will mentioned by Ash'ari?
A) God's will is identical to His command.
B) God's will is different from His command.
C) God's will is both command and prohibition.
D) God's will is irrelevant to human actions.

Key Takeaway
The first Mu'tazilite view equating God's will with His command leads to the problematic conclusion that God would hate actions He does not command, which is contrary to His perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions that are not explicitly commanded by God—like unconscious habits or unintentional mistakes—do you ever assume God hates them? This lesson shows that equating will with command leads to that false conclusion. How does separating God's will from His command change how you view those actions and your relationship with Him? Will you trust that His will for you is broader than just what He commands?
?
This passage argues that God does not hate the actions of children and the insane, even though He does not command them. Think of a time when you saw something happen that seemed contrary to God's commands—perhaps suffering or sin. Did you struggle with the idea that God allows it? How does the Ash'ari position that God's will is not limited to His commands help you accept that He has a purpose in all that He wills, even when it is not commanded? Can you truly submit to that?
?
If you had to choose between the two Mu'tazilite views, which one would you lean towards before reading this? The logical consequence of the first view is that God would hate uncommanded actions. Are you willing to hold a view about God that entails something unworthy of Him? This lesson challenges you to ensure your understanding of God's attributes is logically consistent and rooted in revelation. How will you guard your heart from adopting flawed theological notions?

The Implication of Weakness in God's Will
Al-Ash'ari presents an argument that God cannot hate an event that occurs without implying weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Ash'ari argues that if God hates an action yet it occurs, it would imply weakness in God.

Core Idea
A cause that fails to prevent its contrary is a weak cause.

Al-Ash'ari begins his refutation by exposing a logical flaw in the Mu'tazilite position. If God hates a human action, yet it still takes place, then God's will is not effective—this is a sign of weakness. This lesson explores that initial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the logical conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
The argument is compact; pay attention to the chain of implications.
2
Identify the key implication
Al-Ash'ari argues that if God hates an event and it still occurs, then the event happens regardless of His will. Why does this imply weakness?
Think about what it means for a being to be truly powerful.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that such a situation would be 'the attribute of the weak.' Ask yourself if this applies to God.
This argument sets the stage for refuting specific views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ
قيل لهم: بل وقوع ذلك منهم، وهو كاره له، يُوجِبُ الضعف والوهن لا محالة؛ لأنه إذا كان ما كَرِهَ كونَهُ كان ما أبَى كونه، وإذا كان ما أبَى كونه، فقد كان الشيء شاءه أم أباه! وهذا يوجب أن الشيء كان شاء الله -تعالى- ذلك أم أباه، وهذه صفة الضعيف.

وأيضًا فإن المعتزلة رجلان: أحدهما يقول: إن إرادة الله تعالى في أفعال عباده الأمر بها. والآخر يقول: إرادته في أفعال عباده خلف غير الأمر بها.

فمن ذهب إلى أنها الأمر، لزمه - إذا لم يكن الباري آمرًا بأفعال الأطفال والمجانين - أن يكون كارها لها؛ إذ كان يجب بنفي الإرادة لأفعال العباد الكراهة لها، والله -تعالى- لا يكره إلا معصية كما لا ينهى إلا عن معصية، وإذا لم يكن هذا عندهم هكذا أبطل ما قالوه.

وأيضًا فإذا كان يلزم فيما جاز الأمر به إذا لم يأمر به أن يكون له كارها، لزم من كان في عصر الرسول -صلى الله عليه وسلم- وجاز عنده أن يترك النهي عن المباح الذي ليس بطاعة، أن يكون إذا لم يرد من الله -تعالى- الأمر به فقد كرهه، وهذا يوجب أن كل مباح معصية.

ومن ذهب إلى القول الثاني - وهو قول الجبائي -: إن إرادة الله -تعالى- لأفعال عباده هي غير الأمر بها، يقال له: إذا كان يجب بنفي الإرادة لأفعال عباده الكراهة، فحدثنا: هل أراد الله كون الأفعال التي ليست بمعاصي ولا طاعات؟
Chapter on speech concerning the will and that it encompasses all originated things.

It was said to them: Rather, the occurrence of that from them, while He hates it, necessarily entails weakness and feebleness; because if what He hates to be happens, then what He refuses to be happens, and if what He refuses to be happens, then the thing occurs whether He wills it or abhors it! And this necessitates that the thing occurs whether God - exalted - wills that or abhors it, and this is the attribute of the weak.

Also, the Mu'tazila are two men: one of them says that God's will - exalted - in the acts of His servants is the command to them. The other says that His will in the acts of His servants is something else (khulf) other than the command to them.

So whoever holds that it is the command, it is necessary for him - if the Creator is not commanding the acts of children and the insane - that He hates them, since it follows from the negation of will for the acts of servants the hatred of them, and God - exalted - does not hate except disobedience just as He does not forbid except disobedience, and if this is not so according to them, then what they said is invalidated.

Also, if it is necessary that in what is permissible to command, if He does not command it, that He hates it, then it is necessary for one who was in the era of the Messenger - peace be upon him - and it was permissible in his view to abandon forbidding what is permissible that is not obedience, that if God - exalted - did not intend the command to it, then He hated it, and this necessitates that every permissible thing is disobedience.

And whoever holds the second view - which is the view of al-Jubba'i - that God's will - exalted - for the acts of His servants is other than the command to them, it is said to him: If it is necessary that with the negation of will for the acts of servants there is hatred, then tell us: Did God will the occurrence of acts that are neither disobediences nor acts of obedience?
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Mu'tazilite doctrines regarding divine will (iraada) and its relation to human actions. The passage begins with a chapter heading stating that the will encompasses all originated things (muhdathaat).

The author first addresses an argument: if it is claimed that God hates an action yet it occurs, that would imply weakness. The author argues that if what God hates occurs, then what He refuses also occurs, meaning the action happens regardless of His will or aversion—this is a sign of weakness, not strength.

Then the author identifies two positions among the Mu'tazila: (1) God's will concerning human acts is identical to His command (amr), and (2) His will is something other than command (khulf), as held by al-Jubba'i.

Refuting the first view: if will equals command, then since God does not command the acts of children and the insane (who are not morally accountable), He must hate those acts. But God only hates sin (ma'siya), and these acts are not sinful. Moreover, God only forbids sin. If the Mu'tazila do not accept this consequence, their position collapses.

Next, the author extends the argument: if God does not command a permissible (mubaah) act (which is not obedience), it would follow that He hates it, making all permissible acts sins—an absurd conclusion.

Finally, the author turns to the second view (al-Jubba'i). The question is posed: if negation of will necessitates hatred, then does God will the occurrence of acts that are neither sins nor acts of obedience (i.e., permissible acts)? The passage ends with this open question, leaving the reader to consider the implications. The argument implies that al-Jubba'i's view also leads to difficulty, as the answer must be consistent with the principle that negation of will implies hatred.

Key Terms
إرادة
كراهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can hate an action without it affecting His power.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that if God hates an action and it still occurs, then His will is ineffective, implying weakness.

Checkpoint
What does the occurrence of an action despite God's hatred imply according to Al-Ash'ari?
A) God's mercy
B) Weakness
C) God's command
D) Free will

Key Takeaway
If an event occurs despite God's hatred, then God's will is not sovereign, implying weakness—an impossibility for the Almighty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin against God, do you truly believe that He hates that sin, yet it still happens? What does this lesson force you to admit about your own view of God's sovereignty? Will you now humble yourself and acknowledge that every action occurs only by His permission, turning to Him in repentance?
?
This lesson argues that if God's will were overridden, He would be weak. Do you live as if your own desires are stronger than God's decree? In what specific habit or attitude do you need to submit to His absolute will today?
?
Consider the weight of this truth: nothing occurs against God's will. How does that change your perspective on the trials you face? Will you now accept them as from His wise will, or will you continue to resent what He has decreed?

Refuting the Identification of Will with Command
Al-Ash'ari refutes the first Mu'tazilite view that divine will is identical to command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why identifying God's will with His command leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
If God's will is merely His command, then acts not commanded become hated—making permissible acts sins.

The second part of Al-Ash'ari's refutation targets the Mu'tazilite view that God's will for human acts is nothing other than His command. This lesson unpacks the logical consequences for children, the insane, and permissible acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two positions
The passage begins by stating two Mu'tazilite views. Identify them before focusing on the first.
The first view is that will equals command.
2
Follow the argument against the first view
Al-Ash'ari says: if will equals command, then God must hate acts He does not command. Since He does not command acts of children and the insane, He would hate them. But God only hates sin, so these acts become sins—an absurdity.
Notice the use of 'if... then...' logic.
3
Understand the extension to permissible acts
The argument is extended: if God does not command a permissible act, He would hate it, making that permissible act a sin. This is clearly false.
This shows the view leads to a contradiction.

ومن ذهب إلى القول الثاني - وهو قول الجبائي -: إن إرادة الله -تعالى- لأفعال عباده هي غير الأمر بها، يقال له: إذا كان يجب بنفي الإرادة لأفعال عباده الكراهة، فحدثنا: هل أراد الله كون الأفعال التي ليست بمعاصي ولا طاعات؟
Chapter on speech concerning the will and that it encompasses all originated things.

It was said to them: Rather, the occurrence of that from them, while He hates it, necessarily entails weakness and feebleness; because if what He hates to be happens, then what He refuses to be happens, and if what He refuses to be happens, then the thing occurs whether He wills it or abhors it! And this necessitates that the thing occurs whether God - exalted - wills that or abhors it, and this is the attribute of the weak.

Also, the Mu'tazila are two men: one of them says that God's will - exalted - in the acts of His servants is the command to them. The other says that His will in the acts of His servants is something else (khulf) other than the command to them.

So whoever holds that it is the command, it is necessary for him - if the Creator is not commanding the acts of children and the insane - that He hates them, since it follows from the negation of will for the acts of servants the hatred of them, and God - exalted - does not hate except disobedience just as He does not forbid except disobedience, and if this is not so according to them, then what they said is invalidated.

Also, if it is necessary that in what is permissible to command, if He does not command it, that He hates it, then it is necessary for one who was in the era of the Messenger - peace be upon him - and it was permissible in his view to abandon forbidding what is permissible that is not obedience, that if God - exalted - did not intend the command to it, then He hated it, and this necessitates that every permissible thing is disobedience.

And whoever holds the second view - which is the view of al-Jubba'i - that God's will - exalted - for the acts of His servants is other than the command to them, it is said to him: If it is necessary that with the negation of will for the acts of servants there is hatred, then tell us: Did God will the occurrence of acts that are neither disobediences nor acts of obedience?
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Mu'tazilite doctrines regarding divine will (iraada) and its relation to human actions. The passage begins with a chapter heading stating that the will encompasses all originated things (muhdathaat).

The author first addresses an argument: if it is claimed that God hates an action yet it occurs, that would imply weakness. The author argues that if what God hates occurs, then what He refuses also occurs, meaning the action happens regardless of His will or aversion—this is a sign of weakness, not strength.

Then the author identifies two positions among the Mu'tazila: (1) God's will concerning human acts is identical to His command (amr), and (2) His will is something other than command (khulf), as held by al-Jubba'i.

Refuting the first view: if will equals command, then since God does not command the acts of children and the insane (who are not morally accountable), He must hate those acts. But God only hates sin (ma'siya), and these acts are not sinful. Moreover, God only forbids sin. If the Mu'tazila do not accept this consequence, their position collapses.

Next, the author extends the argument: if God does not command a permissible (mubaah) act (which is not obedience), it would follow that He hates it, making all permissible acts sins—an absurd conclusion.

Finally, the author turns to the second view (al-Jubba'i). The question is posed: if negation of will necessitates hatred, then does God will the occurrence of acts that are neither sins nor acts of obedience (i.e., permissible acts)? The passage ends with this open question, leaving the reader to consider the implications. The argument implies that al-Jubba'i's view also leads to difficulty, as the answer must be consistent with the principle that negation of will implies hatred.

Key Terms
إرادة
أمر
كراهة
معصية
مباح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will is the same as His command.
Correction: If it were, then God would hate acts He does not command, making them sins. But many acts (children's acts, permissible acts) are not commanded and are not sins.

Checkpoint
What follows from identifying God's will with His command?
A) God loves all acts
B) Acts not commanded become hated sins
C) God's will becomes limited to command
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Equating God's will with His command leads to the absurd conclusion that every act not commanded is a sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever subconsciously limit God's will to a set of commands and prohibitions, feeling that He is indifferent or even displeased with the 'neutral' moments of your life? How does this lesson challenge you to see His will encompassing even your permissible actions, and what practical change will you make today to acknowledge that?
?
Think of a person you have judged harshly for something you assumed was displeasing to God, even though no clear command existed. How does Al-Ash'ari's argument about children and the insane expose the danger of such judgments? Will you seek forgiveness and change your attitude toward that person?

Refuting the View that Will is Other than Command
Al-Ash'ari questions al-Jubba'i's view by applying the same principle of hatred.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed to al-Jubba'i's view that God's will is distinct from His command.

Core Idea
If will is not command, then the same principle—negation of will implies hatred—raises a difficult question about permissible acts.

The last part of the passage engages the second Mu'tazilite position, associated with al-Jubba'i. Al-Ash'ari poses a critical question that exposes an inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the second view
Al-Ash'ari states the view: God's will for human acts is something other than command.
This is the view of al-Jubba'i.
2
Note the principle applied
The same principle from earlier is used: negation of will implies hatred.
This principle is assumed by both sides.
3
Understand the dilemma
Al-Ash'ari asks: Does God will acts that are neither sins nor obedience (i.e., permissible acts)? If yes, then the principle fails (since these acts are not hated). If no, then God hates them, making them sins. Either way, the view is inconsistent.
Think about how this question traps the opponent.

The Absurdity of Equating Non-Command with Aversion
Al-Ash'ari's reductio against the Mu'tazilite view on non-command and aversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's reductio ad absurdum argument against the Mu'tazilite claim that God's not commanding something implies His aversion to it.

Core Idea
If not commanding meant aversion, then every permissible act would be a sin.

Al-Ash'ari here deploys a powerful logical argument to expose the absurd consequences of the Mu'tazilite principle linking non-command to divine aversion. By applying their own premise to a concrete example from the time of the Prophet, he shows that it would force the conclusion that all permissible acts are sins. This passage demonstrates the use of reductio ad absurdum in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
2
Identify the premise
The Mu'tazilite premise: if God does not command something, He is averse to it. Locate this in the text.
The phrase 'إذا لم يأمر به أن يكون له كارها' expresses this.
3
See the absurd conclusion
Applying the premise to permissible acts leads to the conclusion that every permissible act is a sin. Ask yourself why this is absurd.
This is the core of the reductio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا فإذا كان يلزم فيما جاز الأمر به إذا لم يأمر به أن يكون له كارها، لزم من كان في عصر الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وجاز عنده أن يترك النهي عن المباح الذي ليس بطاعة، أن يكون إذا لم يرد من الله تعالى الأمر به فقد كرهه، وهذا يوجب أن كل مباح معصية.
And also, if it is necessary that concerning what it is permissible to command, if He does not command it, that He is averse to it, then it is necessary for one who was in the era of the Messenger, peace be upon him, and it was permissible in his view to refrain from forbidding the permissible that is not an act of obedience, that if God Almighty does not will the command of it, He is indeed averse to it, and this necessitates that every permissible thing is a sin.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against the Mu'tazilite position that God's not commanding something implies His aversion to it. Al-Ash'ari first states the general principle: if it is necessary that when something is permissible to command but God does not command it, He is averse to it, then apply this to the time of the Prophet. At that time, it was permissible for the Prophet to refrain from forbidding permissible acts that are not acts of obedience. According to the Mu'tazilite principle, if God does not command such permissible acts (i.e., does not will the command of them), then He is averse to them. But aversion to something, in their view, makes it a sin. Therefore, every permissible act would be a sin, which is absurd. Thus, the Mu'tazilite principle is false.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
كراهة (aversion)
معصية (sin/disobedience)
مباح (permissible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Ash'ari is claiming that God actually has aversion to permissible acts.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari is not asserting that God is averse to permissible acts; he is showing that if the Mu'tazilite principle were true, then every permissible act would be a sin, which is absurd. So the principle must be false.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari's argument, what would follow from the Mu'tazilite view that not commanding implies aversion?
A) That permissible acts become obligatory.
B) That all permissible acts become sins.
C) That God never commands anything.
D) That the Prophet forbade all permissible acts.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazilite view by showing it leads to the absurd conclusion that all permissible acts are sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because God has not explicitly commanded something in the Quran or Sunnah, He must dislike it or it is sinful? How does al-Ash'ari's argument expose the danger of that assumption for your own life? Commit to not labeling permissible actions as sinful without clear evidence.
?
If you find yourself judging others' permissible actions as wrong because you think God 'would not want' them, what does this lesson reveal about the nature of such judgments? How will you change your attitude towards permissible differences among Muslims starting today?
?
The Mu'tazilite view logically leads to absurdity. But in your own heart, do you sometimes feel that God's silence on an issue means He is displeased? How can you train yourself to distinguish between your feelings and God's actual commands, especially when it comes to matters of personal preference?

The Dilemma of Neutral Actions
Al-Ash'ari questions al-Jubba'i about God's will for neutral actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses a dilemma to expose an inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite view of divine will and neutral actions.

Core Idea
Neutral actions cannot stay neutral under the Mu'tazilite view of God's will.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari engages the leading Mu'tazilite theologian al-Jubba'i in a crisp logical exchange. He poses a simple question about actions that are neither sins nor acts of obedience. Depending on the answer, the action must become either obedience or sin, revealing a flaw in the Mu'tazilite framework.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
Al-Ash'ari first states al-Jubba'i's view: God's will for human actions is distinct from the actions themselves. This means God does not directly will every human act.
This is the background needed for the dilemma.
2
Read the dilemma question
Al-Ash'ari asks: Did God will the occurrence of actions that are neither sins nor obedience? This is the hinge of the argument.
3
Follow the two horns
Trace both possible answers: "Yes" makes it obedience, "No" makes it sin. Neither leaves the action neutral.
Notice how al-Ash'ari uses al-Jubba'i's own definitions to force the contradiction.
4
Grasp the force of the argument
The dilemma shows that the Mu'tazilite framework cannot accommodate neutral actions, undermining their theology.
This is a classic example of dialectical reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذهب إلى القول الثاني - وهو قول الجبائي - : إن إرادة الله تعالى لأفعال عباده هي غير، يقال له: إذا كان يجب بنفي الإرادة لأفعال عباده الكراهة، فحدثنا: هل أراد الله كون الأفعال التي ليست بمعاصي ولا طاعات؟

بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ

فإن قال: نعم.
قيل له: فلزمك أن يكون طاعة؛ لأن الطاعة عندك إنما كانت طاعة للمطاع لأنه أرادها.
فإن قال: لم يردها.
قيل له: فيلزمك أنه كاره لكونها، وهذا يوجب أن تكون معصية؛ لأن ما كرهه الله سبحانه فهو معصية، كما أن ما نهى عنه فهو معصية عندكم.
And whoever holds the second opinion—which is the opinion of al-Jubba'i—that God Almighty's will for the actions of His servants is something else, it is said to him: If aversion is necessitated by negation of will for the actions of His servants, then tell us: did God will the occurrence of actions that are not sins nor acts of obedience?

Chapter on the discussion of will and that it encompasses all originated things.

If he says: Yes.
It is said to him: Then it follows for you that it is an act of obedience; because the act of obedience, in your view, is only an act of obedience to the obeyed because He willed it.
If he says: He did not will it.
It is said to him: Then it follows for you that He is averse to its occurrence, and this necessitates that it be a sin; because what God—exalted is He—is averse to is a sin, just as what He forbids is a sin according to you.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a dialogue with the Mu'tazilite theologian al-Jubba'i. Al-Ash'ari first states al-Jubba'i's position: God's will for human actions is something other than the actions themselves (i.e., God does not directly will all human actions). Then he poses a dilemma: Consider actions that are neither sins nor acts of obedience (i.e., neutral actions). Did God will their occurrence or not? If al-Jubba'i says yes, then according to his own definition, an act of obedience is only such because God willed it; so this neutral action would become an act of obedience, which is contradictory since it is neutral. If he says no, then God is averse to its occurrence, and aversion in their view makes it a sin, so it becomes a sin. Either way, the neutral action cannot remain neutral; thus the Mu'tazilite position fails. The chapter heading introduces the discussion on will and its encompassing all originated things, which is the broader context.

Key Terms
إرادة
كراهة
طاعة
معصية
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Ash'ari is claiming that neutral actions actually are obedience or sin.
Correction: He is not asserting that; he is showing that the Mu'tazilite view forces them into one category, which is problematic.

Checkpoint
What are the two possible answers al-Jubba'i could give to the question about God's will for neutral actions, and what are the consequences?
A) Yes: it becomes forbidden; No: it becomes recommended.
B) Yes: it becomes obedience; No: it becomes sin.
C) Yes: it becomes sin; No: it becomes obedience.
D) Yes: it remains neutral; No: it becomes disliked.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's dilemma shows that the Mu'tazilite definition of will leaves no room for neutral actions; they must be either obedience or sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about your daily actions that you consider 'neutral' (neither good nor bad). How does this dilemma challenge your assumption that some actions are morally insignificant before God? Are you willing to see every action as falling under His will, either as obedience or sin?
?
In your life, do you sometimes try to keep actions 'neutral' to avoid responsibility? How does al-Ash'ari's argument expose the impossibility of such a stance? What would it mean for you to consciously will every action as obedience?
?
If you truly believed that every action that is not willed by God becomes an object of His aversion, how would that change the way you approach even the smallest choices today? Can you identify one 'neutral' habit you need to transform into an act of worship?

Eternal Aversion from Negation of Will
Al-Ash'ari argues that if negation of will implies aversion, then God was eternally averse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses a logical reductio to refute the Mu'tazilite principle that negation of will implies aversion, showing it leads to an absurd eternal attribute.

Core Idea
If negation of will means aversion, then God was eternally averse before creation.

Al-Ash'ari delivers a final blow to the Mu'tazilite claim by forcing them to consider the logical consequences of their own rule applied to God's eternal state. Before any objects of will existed, God was not willing anything; if that implies aversion, then God must have been eternally averse—an absurd and theologically unacceptable conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
The phrase 'بَنَّةٌ' means 'at all'.
2
Identify the premises
The argument uses two premises: (1) Negation of will implies aversion. (2) Before creation, God was eternally not willing anything. Locate these in the text.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
What conclusion follows from the premises? Why is it problematic for the Mu'tazilites? Consider that they might not accept an eternal attribute of aversion.
Al-Ash'ari is not asserting that God has aversion; he is showing the absurdity of their principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: إذا كان نفي الإرادة يوجب إثبات كراهة، فيلزمكم - إذا كان الله تعالى لم يزل غير مريد لشيء بَنَّةٌ - أن يكون لم يزل كارها؛ إذ كان نفي الإرادة يوجب إثبات كراهة.
And it is said to them: If negation of will necessitates affirmation of aversion, then it follows for you—if God Almighty was ever not willing anything at all—that He was ever averse; since negation of will necessitates affirmation of aversion.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents a final argument against the Mu'tazilite principle that negation of will implies aversion. Al-Ash'ari points out that before creation, God was eternally not willing anything (since there were no objects of will). If the Mu'tazilite principle holds, then God must have been eternally averse to everything. But this is problematic because it implies an eternal attribute of aversion, which they might not accept, or it contradicts the fact that God only becomes willing at the time of creation. The argument forces them to abandon the principle.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
كراهة (aversion)
أزل (eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari believes God has eternal aversion.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari is using a logical reductio ad absurdum to refute the Mu'tazilite premise; he does not affirm aversion for God.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ash'ari conclude from the Mu'tazilite principle that negation of will implies aversion, when applied to God's eternal state?
A) God was eternally willing.
B) God was eternally averse.
C) God had no attributes.
D) The principle is only valid for temporal things.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazilite principle by showing it forces an eternal attribute of aversion on God, which is theologically unacceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's eternal will and the fact that He was not willing anything before creation, do you sometimes fall into the trap of imagining God as passive or indifferent? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
The Mu'tazilites tried to rationalize divine attributes, but ended up with an absurd consequence. Have you ever found yourself over-rationalizing matters of faith to the point of losing sight of God's transcendence? What would it take for you to submit to divine mysteries instead?
?
Al-Ash'ari's argument exposes the incoherence of denying God's will for fear of anthropomorphism. Are there any aspects of God's attributes that you are uncomfortable affirming because they seem 'too human'? How can you trust that God's attributes are perfect and unique, even when they share names with human traits?

Denying God's Will Implies Hatred and Disobedience
Ash'ari argues that denying God's will for acts implies hatred and disobedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how denying God's will for any act leads to the absurd conclusion that God hates that act, and why this is a key argument in Ash'ari's theology.

Core Idea
Denying God's will for an act forces you to say God hates it.

This passage presents a classic Ash'ari argument against the Mu'tazila concerning the scope of God's will. The discussion uses a dialectical format where the opponent is forced into a contradiction: if God did not will an act, then He must dislike it, and anything God dislikes is disobedience. This argument aims to prove that God's will encompasses all created acts, even those we consider evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the dialectical exchange.
Notice the question-and-answer format.
2
Understand the dialectical structure
The argument presents two possible responses from the opponent—'Yes' or 'No'—and draws consequences for each.
3
Identify the key moves
The first move: if God wills, the act is obedience. The second: if God does not will, He dislikes, so the act is disobedience.
The author uses the opponent's own definition of obedience and disobedience.
4
Grasp the final implication
The argument extends to show that if God never willed anything, He would be eternally disliking—an absurd consequence.
This forces the opponent to affirm that God's will encompasses all acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الكلام في الإرادة وأنها تعم سائرَ المُحْدَثَاتِ
فإن قال : نعم .
قيل له : فلزِمَك أن يكونَ طاعة؛ لأن الطاعة -عندك - إنما كانت طاعةٌ للمُطاعِ لأنَّه أرادَهَا .
فإن قال : لم يردها .
قيل له : فيلزمك أنه كاره لكونها، وهذا يُوجِبُ أن تكون معصية ؛
لأن ما كَرِهَهُ اللَّهُ -سبحانه - فهو معصية، كما أن ما نهى عنه فهو معصية عندكم.
ويقال لهم : إذا كان نفي الإرادة يوجب إثبات كراهة، فيلزمكم - إذا كان الله -تعالى- لم يزل غير مريد لشيءٍ بتةً، أن يكونَ لمْ يزل كارِها ؛ إذ كانَ نفي الإرادة يُوجِبُ إثباتَ كراهة.
Chapter on the discussion of will and that it encompasses all created things.
If he says: Yes.
It is said to him: You must then consider it obedience; because obedience, according to you, is only obedience to the one obeyed because He willed it.
If he says: He did not will it.
It is said to him: Then it necessarily follows that He dislikes its existence, and this obligates that it be disobedience; because what Allah, glorified be He, dislikes is disobedience, just as what He forbids is disobedience according to you.
And it is said to them: Since the negation of will obligates the affirmation of dislike, it follows necessarily for you—if Allah, exalted, never ceased to be non-willing of anything absolutely—that He never ceased to be disliking; since the negation of will obligates the affirmation of dislike.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the Mu'tazila regarding God's will (al-irada). The author begins by stating the chapter topic: that God's will encompasses all created things. He then presents a dialectical exchange. The opponent (Mu'tazili) is asked: if you say that God willed an act, then that act must be obedience, because obedience is defined as that which the obeyed wills. But if the opponent says God did not will it, then it follows that God dislikes (kariha) its existence, and whatever God dislikes is disobedience (ma'siya). The argument uses the Mu'tazili's own principle that what God dislikes is disobedience. Then the author extends the argument: if negation of will implies affirmation of dislike, then if God was eternally non-willing of anything, He would be eternally disliking—which is absurd. Thus, the Mu'tazili position leads to contradiction.

الكراهة

المعصية
نفي الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will only applies to good and obedient acts, not to sinful acts.
Correction: The passage argues that if God did not will a sinful act, then He must dislike it, making it disobedience. This implies that God's will encompasses all acts, even sinful ones, because denying will leads to hatred.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the Mu'tazili claim about God's will for an act lead to if they say God did not will it?
A) The act is obedience because it was created.
B) The act becomes disobedience because God dislikes it.
C) The act is neither obedience nor disobedience.
D) The act is permissible because God did not intend it.

Key Takeaway
Denying God's will for any act logically entails that God dislikes it, which contradicts the nature of God and makes the act disobedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

According to this passage, if you deny God's will for that success, you are implying God dislikes it. How does that challenge your gratitude? Will you now take a moment to sincerely acknowledge His will behind every blessing?
?
When you face a trial or sin, do you ever think 'God did not will this for me'? This passage shows that saying 'God did not will it' means 'God dislikes it.' Does your complaint about the trial actually accuse God of disliking you? How can you reconcile your difficulty with the belief that God's will covers all things, including tests that purify you?
?
The argument reveals that the Mu'tazila definition of obedience as 'what God wills' forces a contradiction. Look at your own understanding: do you sometimes define obedience as merely following rules, without acknowledging that God's will is behind your ability to obey? Will you now align your theology to affirm that every act of obedience and even every act of disobedience falls under God's will, and then humble yourself before His absolute sovereignty?
